Contingent Properties Prove Divine Will
Sanusi refutes preponderance without preponderator and doctrines of nature/cause, proving divine will from contingent bodily properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specific size and quality of the body prove that the Creator has choice (will) and why this refutes the doctrines of nature and necessary cause.

Core Idea
Specific bodily properties prove the Creator's free choice.

Sanusi presents a powerful argument from the contingency of our physical characteristics. By observing that our body has a specific size and quality among many possibilities, we can deduce that its Creator must have chosen this particular form. This reasoning demolishes the idea that things happen by mere nature or necessary cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the refuted doctrines
The passage begins by invalidating two false doctrines: preponderance without a preponderator, and ascribing creation to nature or necessary cause. Notice these are stated as headings.
These are the views that Sanusi will refute with his argument.
2
Observe the contingency of bodily properties
Sanusi points out that your body could have had a different specific quantity and quality. This is a key observation: it is not necessary that it be exactly as it is.
Contingency means it could have been otherwise.
3
Understand the conclusion: divine choice
From this contingency, Sanusi concludes that the Maker has choice (اختيار) in specifying your essence with one of the possibilities. This is the core proof of divine will.
The specification implies a choosing agent.
4
Reflect on the refutation
This argument directly undermines the idea of preponderance without a preponderator (that something could occur without a cause) and the doctrines of nature or cause (that properties arise necessarily).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ بطلان الرجحان بغير مرجح ، وبطلان القول بالطبع والعلة ] ثم إذا نظرت إلى هذا الزائد من ذاتك وجدته جرماً يعمر فراغاً ، يجوز أن يكون على ما هو عليه من المقدار المخصوص والصفة المخصوصة ، وأن يكون على خلافهما ، فتعلم قطعاً أن لصانعك اختياراً في تخصيص ذاتك ببعض ما جاز عليها ، فيخرج لك من هذا البرهان
[The invalidity of preponderance without a preponderator, and the invalidity of the doctrine of nature and cause.] Then if you look at this increase from your essence, you find it is a body occupying a void. It is possible that it be as it is in its specific quantity and specific quality, and that it be contrary to them. So you know decisively that your Maker has choice in specifying your essence with some of what is possible for it. So from this proof comes out for you...
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is headed by a two-part claim: the invalidity of preponderance without a preponderator (الرجحان بغير مرجح) and the invalidity of ascribing creation to nature (الطبع) or necessary cause (العلة). Sanusi then explains: when you examine the increase in your body, you find it is a body occupying space. It is possible for it to have a different specific quantity and quality, yet it is as it is. This shows that its maker (صانع) has choice (اختيار) in specifying it with a particular possibility. Thus, the proof that the maker has will is derived from the contingency of physical properties. The final sentence is cut off but the argument is clear: the specification of the body proves the creator's free choice.

Key Terms
رجحان بغير مرجح
طبع
علة
اختيار
تخصيص
مقدار مخصوص
صفة مخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That physical properties are necessary or caused by nature.
Correction: Sanusi argues that because the body could have different properties, they are contingent, not necessary, and thus require a choosing creator.

Checkpoint
What does the specific size and quality of the body prove about its creator?
A) That the creator is powerful.
B) That the creator is all-knowing.
C) That the creator has choice (اختيار).
D) That the creator is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The specific, contingent qualities of the body prove that the Creator has will and choice, refuting the doctrines of nature and necessary cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body could have been different in its specific quantity and quality. Do you live as if your existence is a product of random chance or of a deliberate, loving choice? What would change if you truly internalized that your Maker chose you exactly as you are?
?
Sanusi refutes the idea that 'nature' or 'cause' determines everything. In what areas of your life do you act as if things are 'just the way they are' instead of seeing the hand of the Creator's will? Will you begin to look for His choice in the details of your daily existence?
?
If your specific body - its shape, abilities, limitations - is a direct result of God's choice, how does that affect your gratitude or resentment towards your physical self? Can you thank Him for making you exactly as He willed, even in aspects you dislike?

Proof of God's Pre-eternity (Qidam)
Sanusi proves God's pre-eternity and refutes temporal origination through logical contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's rational proof that God must be pre-eternal (qadīm) and why temporal origination leads to logical absurdities.

Core Idea
If God were not pre-eternal, we would face impossible infinite regress or circularity.

Sanusi now turns to a specific divine attribute: pre-eternity (qidam). He argues that the Maker of the universe cannot have come into existence after non-existence, because that would require a regress that never ends or a vicious circle. This brief but powerful proof establishes a foundational attribute of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the key terms: qadīm, tasalsul, dawr.
2
Understand the definition of pre-eternal
Sanusi defines qadīm as 'not preceded by non-existence'. Grasp that this is a precise logical meaning, not a vague 'old'.
He contrasts it with temporal origination (being preceded by non-existence).
3
Follow the contrapositive argument
The proof argues: if not pre-eternal, then needs a maker. That maker leads to either infinite regress or circularity, both impossible.
Identify the two branches: the original maker is either not an effect of the current maker, or it is.
4
Understand why each option is impossible
Infinite regress: a chain without beginning cannot exist. Circularity: a thing cannot be prior to itself.
These are basic rational axioms in kalam.
5
Appreciate the conclusion
Since every alternative is impossible, the original assumption (that God is not pre-eternal) is false. Therefore God must be pre-eternal.
This establishes qidam as a necessary attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ في إثباتِ صفة القدم له تعالى ، ونفي الزمانِ عنه ] ثم نقولُ : يجب أن يكون هذا الصانع لذاتِكَ ولسائرِ العالم قديماً ؛ أي : غير مسبوق بعدم ، وإلا لافتقر إلى محدثِ ، وذلك يؤدّي إلى التسلسل إنْ كَانَ محدثه ليسَ أثراً له ، أو إلى الدور إنْ كانَ ، والتسلسل والدور محالانِ ؛ لما في الأَوَّلِ مِنْ فراغ ما لا نهاية له بالعددِ ، وفي الثاني مِنْ كون الشيء الواحدِ سابقاً على نفسه مسبوقاً بها
Chapter: On establishing the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam) for Him, Most High, and negating time from Him. Then we say: It is necessary that this Maker, for your own essence and for the rest of the world, be pre-eternal (qadīm); i.e., not preceded by non-existence. Otherwise, He would need a maker, and that leads to infinite regress (tasalsul) if that maker is not His effect, or to circularity (dawr) if it is. Both infinite regress and circularity are impossible; because in the first there is the void of an infinite number of things, and in the second a single thing would be prior to itself and preceded by itself.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a proof for God's pre-eternity (qidam). Sanusi begins by stating that the Maker must be pre-eternal, defining 'pre-eternal' as not preceded by non-existence. He then argues by contradiction: if the Maker were not pre-eternal, He would need an originator. That originator is either not His effect (leading to infinite regress) or is His effect (leading to circularity). Both are impossible, as infinite regress implies an infinite chain with no beginning, while circularity makes a single thing prior to and preceded by itself. Therefore, the Maker must be pre-eternal.

Key Terms
قديم
تسلسل
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that pre-eternity means existing for a very long time.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly defines pre-eternal as 'not preceded by non-existence', which means having no beginning at all, not merely a long duration.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the meaning of 'qadīm' for God?
A) Existing for a long time
B) Not preceded by non-existence
C) Having no cause
D) Being permanent

Key Takeaway
God's pre-eternity means He was never preceded by non-existence, and any alternative leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not pre-eternal, He would need a maker. Do you truly live each moment as though God has no beginning, or do your actions imply that His existence started when you decided to turn to Him? What would change if you fully internalized that He was never absent?
?
The proof shows that infinite regress is impossible. Are you stuck in an endless cycle of excuses and delays, avoiding turning to the Pre-eternal One? What is the first step you will take today to break that chain and rely on Him who never began?
?
Circularity would make a thing prior to itself. Are there patterns in your life where you justify wrong actions by saying 'I'll repent later,' creating a circle of delay and false hope? How can you cut through that loop and face the Eternal now?

The Proof of God's Persistence (Baqā')
Sanusi proves God's persistence through a reductio linking it to pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for why God must be perpetual and how it is linked to His pre-eternity.

Core Idea
If God can cease, He would need a cause — but He is eternal.

This short but powerful proof by Sanusi establishes that God's existence has no end. He shows that the very attribute of pre-eternity (qidam) logically entails persistence (baqā'). As you read, notice how he uses a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opposite leads to a contradiction with what has already been proven.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, syllogistic style of Sanusi.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Find the 'if' clause: 'otherwise (illa) would be...' Sanusi shows what would follow if God were not persistent. Identify each step in the chain.
The argument is a reductio: assume the opposite, derive impossible conclusion.
3
Connect to the general principle
At the end, Sanusi extracts a general rule: whatever is pre-eternal cannot cease. Reflect on why this is a necessary implication.
This principle is used later in kalam to discuss other attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ في إثباتِ صفةِ البقاء ، والدليل عليها ] ثم نقولُ : ويجب أن يكونَ باقياً ؛ أي : لا يلحق وجوده عدمٌ ، وإلا لكانَتْ ذاتُهُ تقبلهما ، فيحتاجُ في ترجيح وجودِهِ إِلى مُخصِّص (۱) ، فيكون حادثاً ، كيف وقد مرَّ بالبرهان آنفاً وجوبُ قدمِهِ ؟! ومِنْ هنا تعلمُ برهانَ أَنَّ كلَّ ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه
Chapter: On establishing the attribute of persistence (baqā') and its proof. Then we say: He must be persistent (bāqī); i.e., non-existence does not attach to His existence. Otherwise, His essence would be receptive to both existence and non-existence, thus He would need a particulariser (mukhassis) to give preponderance to His existence, and thus He would be originated. How could that be, given that the necessity of His pre-eternity has already been proven by demonstration? From this you learn the proof that everything whose pre-eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a proof for God's attribute of persistence (baqā'), meaning His existence has no end. Sanusi argues that if God were not persistent, His essence would be receptive to both existence and non-existence, making Him contingent and requiring a particulariser (mukhassis) to bring Him into existence. But this contradicts His proven pre-eternity. The author also deduces a general principle: whatever is proven pre-eternal cannot cease to exist.

Key Terms
البقاء
باقي
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Persistence is a separate attribute that could be absent even if pre-eternity is proven.
Correction: Sanusi shows that pre-eternity logically entails persistence; they are not independent.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue would be the consequence if God were not persistent?
A) He would have a beginning
B) He would be eternal
C) He would need a particulariser
D) He would be like other beings

Key Takeaway
God's persistence is a necessary consequence of His pre-eternity; whatever is eternal cannot cease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you truly consider that God's existence is so certain that non-existence is impossible, does your heart find complete security in Him? Or do you secretly doubt that He will always be there for you? What would change if you lived each moment with the unwavering conviction that God is persistent and never fails?
?
Sanusi argues that if God could cease, He would need a cause. Do you sometimes treat God's attributes as if they were uncertain, demanding proofs and signs in your life to reassure you? How does this teaching call you to trust in God's eternal nature without condition?
?
The proof shows that pre-eternity entails persistence. Is your faith in God's attributes built on rational certainty, or is it merely inherited from tradition? Are you willing to examine the evidence so that your belief becomes unshakeable, even when faced with doubts?

Proof of God's Power and Will
Sanusi proves God's power and will from creation and its specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi argues that God must possess both power and will based on the fact of creation and its specificity.

Core Idea
Creation's existence proves God's power; its particularity proves His will.

Sanusi presents a concise rational proof for two of God's essential attributes: power and will. He argues that the mere fact that we exist demands a powerful Maker, while the fact that our existence is specified with particular measure and time demands a willing Maker. This lesson unpacks that proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the parallel structure: 'otherwise...' for each attribute.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two attributes
Notice the two necessary attributes: power (qādir) and will (murīd). Each is argued separately.
3
Understand the argument
See how Sanusi connects each attribute to a different aspect of creation: existence vs. specification. Power explains existence; will explains specificity.
4
Reflect on the necessity
Ask yourself why both attributes are required. Why isn't power alone enough? Think about a builder who can build but needs a specific blueprint.
This connects to the misconception that power alone suffices.
5
Apply the logic
Consider how this argument fits into broader kalam proofs. This is a foundational proof for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب لهذا الصانع أن يكون قادراً ، وإلا لَمَا أوجدَكَ ، ومريداً ، وإلا لَمَا اختصصت بوجود ولا مقدار
It is necessary for this Maker that He be powerful (qādiran); otherwise He would not have brought you into existence. And that He be willing (murīdan); otherwise you would not have been specified with existence and measure.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof for two of God's essential attributes: power (qudra) and will (irāda). Sanusi argues that the fact that we exist demands that the Maker be powerful, because bringing into existence requires power. Additionally, our existence is specified with a particular measure and time, which requires will to specify it. Without will, existence would not be specific.

Key Terms
قادر
مريد
قدرة
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power alone is sufficient to explain creation; will is unnecessary.
Correction: Sanusi demonstrates that while power explains existence, will is specifically required to account for the particularization of existence with measure and time.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that God must have will?
A) Because He is powerful
B) Because creation is specified
C) Because He is eternal
D) Because He is unique

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's concise proof establishes that God must be both powerful and willing, because existence requires power and specificity requires will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your existence is insignificant or accidental? Sanusi says your very existence proves God's power. How does reflecting on this truth change the way you see your own life today?
?
Are there circumstances in your life that you resent or wish were different? This lesson teaches that your specific measure and existence are a direct result of God's will. Can you accept that His will for you is wise, even when it is hard?
?
What would need to change in your daily attitude if you truly believed that every detail of your life—your health, your family, your trials—is a divine specification, not random chance?

Proof of the Attribute of Permanence (Baqā')
Sanusi proves God's permanence and deduces the impossibility of perishing for the eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi proves that God must be permanent (bāqī) and how he deduces that anything eternal cannot cease to exist.

Core Idea
Eternal existence cannot be followed by non-existence.

Sanusi now turns to another essential divine attribute: permanence (baqā'). He argues that God's existence is such that non-existence can never attach to it. Using a concise proof by contradiction, he shows that the Eternal cannot perish. This passage also establishes a general rational principle: whatever is proven pre-eternal cannot become non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its structure
Read the Arabic and the translation. Notice that Sanusi begins with the thesis, then gives a conditional argument, and ends with a general principle.
Pay attention to the marker (1) referring to a footnote in the original text.
2
Understand the proof by contradiction
Sanusi assumes the opposite (that non-existence could attach) and shows it leads to an absurdity: God would be originated. Trace the logic carefully: if non-existence could attach, then His essence would be receptive to both existence and non-existence, requiring a cause to prefer existence – making Him originated, which contradicts His proven eternity.
The proof relies on the principle that whatever is equally receptive to two opposites needs an external particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ).
3
Grasp the broader implication
The lesson concludes with a universal rule: anything proven eternal cannot become non-existent. This applies not only to God but to any necessary existent.
This principle is foundational in kalām for arguments about the attributes of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ في إثباتِ صفةِ البقاء ، والدليل عليها ] ثم نقولُ : ويجب أن يكونَ باقياً ؛ أي : لا يلحق وجوده عدمٌ ، وإلا لكانَتْ ذاتُهُ تقبلهما ، فيحتاجُ في ترجيح وجودِهِ إِلى مُخصِّص (۱) ، فيكون حادثاً ، كيف وقد مرَّ بالبرهان آنفاً وجوبُ قدمِهِ ؟! ومِنْ هنا تعلمُ برهانَ أَنَّ كلَّ ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه
A chapter on the proof of the attribute of permanence (al-baqā'), and the proof for it. Then we say: He must be permanent (bāqyan), i.e., non-existence does not attach to His existence. Otherwise, His essence would accept both (existence and non-existence), and thus it would need a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to prefer His existence, making it originated (ḥādith). How (can that be) when the proof has just passed for the necessity of His pre-eternity (qidam)? And from here you learn the proof that whatever has been proven to be pre-eternal, its non-existence is impossible.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a proof. Sanusi first states the thesis: God must be permanent (bāqī), meaning that His existence is not followed by non-existence. He then argues by contradiction: if His existence could be followed by non-existence, then His essence would be capable of both existence and non-existence. Such a balanced essence would require an external particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to tip the scale toward existence. But anything requiring a particularizer is originated (ḥādith). This contradicts the already established proof of God's pre-eternity (qidam). The lesson concludes with a general principle: whatever is proven eternal cannot perish.

Key Terms
باقٍ (baqī)
عدم (ʿadam)
مُخصِّص (mukhaṣṣiṣ)
حادث (ḥādith)
قدم (qidam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that baqā' means mere temporal endurance, or that God could potentially cease to exist if He chose.
Correction: Sanusi proves that baqā' is a necessary attribute of the Eternal. Because God is pre-eternal, non-existence is impossible for Him; it is not a matter of choice but of logical necessity.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if God's existence could be followed by non-existence?
A) He would have a cause for His existence.
B) His essence would accept both existence and non-existence, requiring a cause.
C) He would still be eternal by another proof.
D) He would need to be created by another.

Key Takeaway
God's permanence is a necessary attribute because the Eternal cannot cease to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi proves that if God could perish, He would need a particularizer—making Him like created things. Do you truly live as if God is permanent and unchangeable, or do you sometimes treat His promises as conditional? What would change in your life if you fully internalized that nothing can remove His existence or His attributes?
?
The proof says the eternal cannot cease. Yet you find yourself attached to people, possessions, and plans that will inevitably end. How does the certainty of God's permanence confront the anxiety you feel over losing what you love? Will you entrust your heart to the One who never perishes, even if it means loosening your grip on what is temporary?
?
Sanusi deduces God's permanence from His pre-eternity. If God is both eternal and permanent, you are accountable for your response to a Lord who always was and always will be. Is there any area of your life where you act as if God might change or weaken? What concrete step will you take this week to remind yourself of His unchanging permanence—perhaps in prayer, reading His names, or trusting Him in a difficult situation?

Divine Attributes

Proof of Divine Power and Will from Specific Determination
Sanusi proves power and will using your own existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence and specific qualities of a contingent being prove that its Creator must possess both power and will.

Core Idea
Specific determination demands a willing Creator.

Sanusi begins by establishing two essential attributes of God: power (qudra) and will (irāda). The proof is drawn directly from our own existence: we are contingent beings with specific qualities; without a powerful and willing Creator, we would either be eternal or never exist. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'otherwise...'
2
Identify the two attributes
The passage proves two attributes: power and will. Find the reason given for each one.
3
Understand the logical alternatives
For will, Sanusi presents two impossible alternatives: your pre-eternity or continued non-existence. Grasp why these are impossible.
This shows that without will, the only options are absurd.
4
Reflect on specific determination
Consider how your own existence—your time, place, and qualities—is a specific choice among many possibilities.
This is the core insight that led to the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصلُ [ في الحديث عن الصفاتِ المعنوية ] ويجب لهذا الصانع أن يكون قادراً ، وإلا لَمَا أوجدَكَ ، ومريداً ، وإلا لَمَا اختصصت بوجود ولا مقدار ولا صفة ولا زمن بدلاً عن نقائضها الجائزة ، فيلزم إمَّا قدمُكَ ، أو استمرار عدمِكَ ، وهما محالانِ . ومِنْ هنا تعلم استحالة كون الصانع طبيعة أو عِلَّةً مُوجِبةً . فإن أُجيب عن التأخُرِ في الطبيعة بالمانع أو فواتِ الشرط .. لزم عدم القديم أو التسلسل ؛ لنقل الكلام إلى ذلك المانع وذلك الشرط .
A chapter on discussing the meaning-based attributes (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyya). It is necessary for this Maker to be powerful (qādiran); otherwise He would not have created you. And willing (murīdan); otherwise you would not have been singled out for existence, nor quantity, nor quality, nor time, instead of their possible opposites. Thus either your pre-eternity (qidam) would follow, or the continuation of your non-existence, both of which are impossible. And from here you learn the impossibility of the Maker being a nature (ṭabī‘a) or a necessitating cause (‘illa mūjiba). And if someone answers concerning delay in nature by a preventing factor (māni‘) or absence of condition (fawt al-sharṭ) — then the non-existence of the eternal (qadīm) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, because the discussion would be transferred to that preventing factor and that condition.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage contains a proof and an objection-refutation. Sanusi first establishes two essential attributes: power (qudra) and will (irāda). The proof for power: without power, creation would be impossible. The proof for will: if the Maker were not willing, you (the human being) would not have been specifically selected for existence, with your particular quantity, quality, and time, instead of the many possible alternatives. If there were no will, either you would be eternal (which is false) or you would never have existed (also false). From the necessity of will, Sanusi deduces the impossibility of the Maker being a nature (ṭabī‘a) or a necessitating cause (ʿilla mūjiba), because these operate without will and cannot produce specific determinations. He then anticipates an objection: perhaps the delay in the effect of a nature is due to a preventing factor (māniʿ) or the absence of a condition (sharṭ). The answer: this postpones the problem. If the preventing factor is eternal, then the eternal (the nature) would never have effect, so it is non-existent; if it is temporal, then the preventing factor itself needs a cause, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
قادر (qādir)
مريد (murīd)
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Someone might think that the existence of a contingent being does not require a willing Creator; perhaps it came about by chance or necessity.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the specific determination (takhṣīṣ) of each contingent being—its particular existence, quantity, quality, and time—cannot be explained by chance or necessity, because those would not select one alternative over its possible opposites. Only a willing agent can choose.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what follows if the Maker were not willing?
A) The Maker would be powerless.
B) The contingency of the world would be impossible.
C) Either the creature would be eternal or never exist.
D) The creature would be self-caused.

Key Takeaway
The fact that you exist with specific qualities proves that your Creator has both power (to bring you into existence) and will (to choose this particular existence over alternatives).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that your existence is proof of God's power and will. Do you truly believe that you are not a random accident? How would your decisions today change if you lived as if every detail of your existence is intentionally chosen by God?
?
The source says you were singled out for your specific qualities by God's will. What is one quality you have that you have never thanked Him for? Will you pause right now and thank Him for it?
?
The proof shows that without God's will, either you would be eternal or you would not exist at all. Since you are neither, do you live as if your existence is a gift or an entitlement? What can you do today to treat it as a gift?

Refuting the View that God is a Nature or Necessitating Cause
Sanusi refutes nature and necessitating cause, answers objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Creator cannot be a nature (ṭabī‘a) or necessitating cause (ʿilla mūjiba), and how the objection about a preventing factor leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
A nature or necessity cannot explain specific choices; only will can.

After establishing power and will, Sanusi draws an immediate corollary: the Maker cannot be a nature (ṭabī‘a) that produces necessarily without will, nor a necessitating cause (ʿilla mūjiba) that necessitates its effect. He then refutes a potential objection involving a preventing factor or missing condition, showing that such defences entail either the non-existence of the eternal or an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from 'Min hā hunā...' onwards
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the key term 'ṭabī‘a' (nature) and 'ʿilla mūjiba' (necessitating cause).
2
Understand the link to the previous proof
Sanusi says 'And from here you learn...' — meaning from the proof of will, we know that specific determination requires will, so the Maker cannot be a nature.
3
Grasp the objection and refutation
The opponent says delay in a nature's effect could be due to a preventing factor or missing condition. Sanusi shows this leads to either non-existence of the eternal or infinite regress.
Trace the logic: if the preventing factor is eternal, the nature never acts; if temporal, it needs a cause, leading to regress.
4
Reflect on the impossibility of necessity
Consider why a necessitating cause cannot explain the specific and varied order of the world.
Necessitating cause would produce one invariable effect, not the diverse and contingent world we see.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصلُ [ في الحديث عن الصفاتِ المعنوية ] ويجب لهذا الصانع أن يكون قادراً ، وإلا لَمَا أوجدَكَ ، ومريداً ، وإلا لَمَا اختصصت بوجود ولا مقدار ولا صفة ولا زمن بدلاً عن نقائضها الجائزة ، فيلزم إمَّا قدمُكَ ، أو استمرار عدمِكَ ، وهما محالانِ . ومِنْ هنا تعلم استحالة كون الصانع طبيعة أو عِلَّةً مُوجِبةً . فإن أُجيب عن التأخُرِ في الطبيعة بالمانع أو فواتِ الشرط .. لزم عدم القديم أو التسلسل ؛ لنقل الكلام إلى ذلك المانع وذلك الشرط .
A chapter on discussing the meaning-based attributes (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyya). It is necessary for this Maker to be powerful (qādiran); otherwise He would not have created you. And willing (murīdan); otherwise you would not have been singled out for existence, nor quantity, nor quality, nor time, instead of their possible opposites. Thus either your pre-eternity (qidam) would follow, or the continuation of your non-existence, both of which are impossible. And from here you learn the impossibility of the Maker being a nature (ṭabī‘a) or a necessitating cause (‘illa mūjiba). And if someone answers concerning delay in nature by a preventing factor (māni‘) or absence of condition (fawt al-sharṭ) — then the non-existence of the eternal (qadīm) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, because the discussion would be transferred to that preventing factor and that condition.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage contains a proof and an objection-refutation. Sanusi first establishes two essential attributes: power (qudra) and will (irāda). The proof for power: without power, creation would be impossible. The proof for will: if the Maker were not willing, you (the human being) would not have been specifically selected for existence, with your particular quantity, quality, and time, instead of the many possible alternatives. If there were no will, either you would be eternal (which is false) or you would never have existed (also false). From the necessity of will, Sanusi deduces the impossibility of the Maker being a nature (ṭabī‘a) or a necessitating cause (ʿilla mūjiba), because these operate without will and cannot produce specific determinations. He then anticipates an objection: perhaps the delay in the effect of a nature is due to a preventing factor (māniʿ) or the absence of a condition (sharṭ). The answer: this postpones the problem. If the preventing factor is eternal, then the eternal (the nature) would never have effect, so it is non-existent; if it is temporal, then the preventing factor itself needs a cause, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
طبيعة (ṭabī‘a)
علة موجبة (ʿilla mūjiba)
مانع (māni‘)
شرط (sharṭ)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the world could be produced by a nature (like fire heating) but with occasional hindrances.
Correction: Sanusi shows that such hindrances cannot explain the specific determination of each event without falling into infinite regress.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue follows if someone claims that the delay in a nature's effect is due to a preventing factor?

B) The effect would occur after a period.
C) Either the eternal would not exist or infinite regress would occur.
D) The preventing factor must be part of the nature.

Key Takeaway
The specific determination of contingent beings cannot be explained by a nature or necessitating cause; it requires a willing creator, and objections involving preventing factors lead to absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, do you sometimes act as if God is a nature—expecting Him to act uniformly without considering His will and wisdom? When have you assumed that because something didn't happen as you expected, it must be due to a 'preventing factor' rather than trusting His deliberate choice? This week, commit to acknowledging His will in every outcome, even those that seem delayed or denied.
?
The argument shows that infinite regress is absurd. Yet, in your own thoughts, do you sometimes fall into an infinite regress of excuses—blaming conditions or obstacles instead of facing the fact that you are a free agent accountable to a Willing Creator? Where in your life have you used 'if only this condition were met' as an excuse to avoid responsibility? This week, stop blaming conditions and repent sincerely.
?
This lesson forces you to see that only a willing Creator can explain the specific details of your existence. Write them down and thank Allah for His specific will for you.

Proof That God Is All-Knowing
Sanusi proves God's knowledge from the precise design of the human body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intricate design of the human body rationally proves that God is all-knowing.

Core Idea
The precise design of your body proves your Maker knows what He is doing.

Sanusi now turns to the attribute of knowledge (ʿilm). He offers a simple but powerful argument: look at your own body. Every part is perfectly suited for its function and sustained in a way that preserves that function. Such fine-tuned craftsmanship cannot come from an ignorant maker. This passage invites you to reflect on the human body as a sign of divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'otherwise you would not be...'.
The argument is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the evidence
Find the two specific examples Sanusi gives: the specialization of each body part for its benefit, and the provision of means to preserve that benefit.
These are the 'fine details of craftsmanship'.
3
Reflect on the argument's logic
Ask yourself: Why does the existence of such precise design necessarily imply a knowing Maker? The commentary will help clarify the reasoning.
This is a rational argument, not dependent on revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يجب أيضاً لصانعِكَ أنْ يكون عالماً ، وإلا لم تكن على ما أنتَ عليهِ مِنْ دقائقِ الصنع ؛ في اختصاص كل جزءٍ منكَ بمنفعتِهِ الخاصَّةِ بهِ ، وإمدادِهِ بما يحفظها عليه ، ونحو ذلكَ مِنَ المحاسنِ التي تعجز عقول البشر عن الإحاطة بأسرارها .
Then it is also necessary for your Maker to be knowing (ʿāliman); otherwise you would not be as you are, with the fine details (daqāʾiq) of craftsmanship: in the specialization of each part of you for its specific benefit, and its being supplied with what preserves that benefit for it, and similar excellences that human minds are incapable of encompassing their secrets.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a proof. Sanusi argues that the Maker must possess the attribute of knowledge (ʿilm). The evidence is the intricate design (daqāʾiq al-ṣunʿ) observable in the human body. Each bodily part is uniquely specialized for a particular benefit (manfaʿa) and is supplied with what sustains that benefit. These finely tuned details, which human intellects cannot fully grasp, testify to a Maker who knows exactly what He is doing. If He were ignorant, such precise craftsmanship would not exist.

Key Terms
عالم (ʿālim)
دقائق الصنع (daqāʾiq al-ṣunʿ)
منفعة (manfaʿa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge is proven only by revelation, not by rational observation.
Correction: Sanusi uses rational observation of the design in the human body as evidence for God's knowledge.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proof That God Is Living, Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking
Sanusi proves God's life and then the attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be alive and possess the attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech, and why their opposites are impossible for Him.

Core Idea
God's life is the basis for His attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech, which are proven by reason, not revelation.

In this passage, Sanusi continues his demonstration of the Divine Attributes. Having already established that God possesses attributes like power, will, and knowledge, he now proves that God must be alive, hearing, seeing, and speaking. He argues from the necessity of perfection and absolute self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the key terms like 'حي', 'سميع', 'بصير', 'متكلم'.
2
Understand the proof for God's life
Identify why God must be living (ḥayy). Sanusi states 'otherwise He would not possess these attributes whose necessity has preceded.' Reflect on why the attributes of power, will, and knowledge require a living subject.
3
Understand the proof for hearing, seeing, speech
If God were alive but not hearing, seeing, speaking, He would have their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness). These are defects (āfāt) and deficiency (naqṣ), impossible for the absolutely self-sufficient God.
Ask: Why would these defects imply need for completion?
4
Review the methodological conclusion
Notice Sanusi's final sentence: 'reliance in these three is upon the proof.' This emphasizes that these attributes are established by rational proof, not just scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحيّاً، وإلا لم يكن بهذه الأوصاف التي سبق وجوبها • وسميعاً بصيراً مُتكلّماً ، وإلا لاتّصف لكونه حيّاً بأضدادِها ، وأضدادها آفات ونقص ، وهي عليه محال ؛ لاحتياجهِ حِينَئِذٍ إلى مَنْ يُكمِّلُهُ ، كيف وهو الغني بإطلاق المفتقِرُ إليه ما سواه على العموم ؟! والتحقيقُ : الاعتمادُ في هذه الثلاثة على الدليل
And alive (ḥayyan); otherwise He would not possess these attributes whose necessity has preceded. And hearing (samīʿan), seeing (baṣīran), speaking (mutakalliman); otherwise, since He is alive, He would be characterized by their opposites, and their opposites are defects (āfāt) and deficiency (naqṣ), and those are impossible for Him because He would then need someone to complete Him. How (can that be) when He is absolutely self-sufficient (al-ghanī bi-iṭlāq) and everything other than Him is in need of Him in general? And the verification: reliance in these three is upon the proof.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage proves two sets of attributes: life (ḥayāt) and the three attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech. First, life is necessary because the previously proven attributes (power, will, knowledge) cannot exist without a living subject. Second, if God were alive but not possessed of hearing, seeing, and speech, then necessarily He would have their opposites: deafness, blindness, and muteness. These are defects (āfāt) and deficiencies (naqṣ). Such imperfections are impossible for God, for they would imply that He is in need of someone to complete Him. Yet God is absolutely self-sufficient (al-ghanī bi-iṭlāq) and all others are in need of Him. The passage ends with a methodological note: the verification for these three attributes (hearing, seeing, speech) is based on rational proof, not mere tradition.

Key Terms

سميع (samīʿ)
بصير (baṣīr)
متكلم (mutakallim)
آفة (āfah)
نقص (naqṣ)
الغني (al-ghanī)
التحقيق (al-taḥqīq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think hearing, seeing, and speech are metaphorical attributes of God.
Correction: Sanusi treats them as real attributes proven by rational proof, not merely metaphorical. He argues that their opposites are actual defects, which cannot apply to God.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
God must be alive, hearing, seeing, and speaking because their opposites are deficiencies incompatible with His absolute self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not all-hearing, all-seeing, and all-speaking, He would be incomplete and in need of someone to complete Him. Do you truly live as if God hears every word you say, sees every action you do, and speaks to you through His revelation? If not, what would change if you took these attributes seriously today?
?
Sanusi proves these attributes through reason, not just tradition. You have now reasoned that God must hear, see, and speak. Does that reasoning move your heart to awe, or do you treat it as mere information? What would it mean to live in constant awareness that you are before a God who sees you and hears you?
?
The opposite of hearing is deafness—a defect. If you believe God is all-hearing, do you also believe He hears your whispered sin? And if He hears, do you repent? Or do you act as if He is deaf to some of your actions? Confront that now and decide what you will change today.

Proof That God is Knowing and Living
Sanusi proves God's knowledge and life from creation and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that God must be knowing and living based on the evidence of creation and the necessity of possessing attributes.

Core Idea
The intricacy of creation proves God's knowledge; life is necessary to have attributes.

In this portion of his theological treatise, Sanusi continues building the case for divine attributes. He first draws attention to the extraordinary design of the human body, arguing that such precision cannot stem from ignorance. Then he asserts that God must be living, for otherwise He could not possess the attributes already established, such as knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Notice the flow from the proof of knowledge to the proof of life.
The argument for knowledge uses the human body as evidence.
2
Examine the argument for knowledge
Identify the phrase 'وإلا لم تكن على ما أنت عليه من دقائق الصنع'. Reflect on how the intricate design of the body necessitates a knowing creator.
Sanusi points to the specialization of each part and its benefit.
3
Examine the argument for life
See how Sanusi connects life to the preceding attributes: 'وحيّاً، وإلا لم يكن بهذه الأوصاف التي سبق وجوبها'. Understand that life is a prerequisite for possessing other attributes.
Life is not an independent proof but follows from other necessary attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يجب أيضاً لصانعكَ أنْ يكون عالماً ، وإلا لم تكن على ما أنتَ عليهِ مِنْ دقائقِ الصنع ؛ في اختصاص كل جزء و منكَ بمنفعتِهِ الخاصَّةِ بهِ ، وإمدادِهِ بما يحفظها عليه ، ونحو ذلكَ مِنَ المحاسنِ التي تعجز عقول البشر عن الإحاطة بأسرارها . وحيّاً، وإلا لم يكن بهذه الأوصاف التي سبق وجوبها • وسميعاً بصيراً مُتكلّماً ، وإلا لاتّصف لكونه حيّاً بأضدادِها ، وأضدادها آفات ونقص ، وهي عليه محال ؛ لاحتياجهِ حِينَئِذٍ إلى مَنْ يُكمِّلُهُ ، كيف وهو الغني بإطلاق المفتقِرُ إليه ما سواه على العموم ؟! والتحقيقُ : الاعتمادُ في هذه الثلاثة على الدليل السمعي ؛ لأنَّ ذاتَهُ تعالى لم تُعرَف حتى يُحكَمَ في حقه بأنَّهُ يجب الاتصافُ بأضدادها عندَ عدمِها • ولا يُستغنى بكونه تعالى عالماً عن كونه سميعاً بصيراً ؛ لما نجده ضرورةً مِنَ الفرقِ بينَ علمنا بالشيء حالَ غَيبَتِهِ عنَّا ، وبينَ تعلَّقِ سمعنا وبصرنا بهِ قبلُ
Then it is also necessary for your Maker to be knowing, otherwise you would not be in the state you are in of the intricacies of craftsmanship in the specialization of each part and of you for its specific benefit, and its supply with what preserves it, and the like of those excellences whose secrets the minds of human beings are incapable of encompassing. And living, otherwise he would not possess these attributes whose necessity has preceded. And hearing, seeing, speaking, otherwise being living he would be characterized by their opposites, and their opposites are defects and deficiency, and that is impossible for Him, because then He would need someone to complete Him. How can that be, when He is absolutely self-sufficient, and everything other than Him is in need of Him?! And the verification is: reliance in these three [hearing, seeing, speaking] is on scriptural proof, because His essence, exalted be He, is not known such that it can be judged concerning Him that He must be characterized by their opposites when they are absent. Nor is it sufficient that He be knowing for Him to be hearing and seeing, because we find necessarily a difference between our knowledge of a thing when it is absent from us and the attachment of our hearing and sight to it previously.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and methodological clarification. Sanusi first proves that God must be knowing (ʿālim): the intricate design of the human body and its benefits cannot be the product of chance or ignorance. Then he proves God must be living (ḥayy): otherwise He would lack the attributes already proven necessary (knowledge, etc.). Then he argues that God must be hearing, seeing, and speaking (samīʿ, baṣīr, mutakallim): since He is living, if He lacked these, He would have their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness), which are defects. This is impossible because it would imply need for completion, contradicting God's absolute self-sufficiency. However, Sanusi then states the verification (taḥqīq): for these three (hearing, seeing, speaking), we rely on scriptural proof (dalīl samʿī), not pure reason, because God's essence is not known to us such that we can rationally deduce that He must be characterized by the opposites when the attributes are absent. Finally, he clarifies that being knowing does not suffice for being hearing and seeing, because we experience a clear difference between knowledge of an absent thing and direct sensory perception.

Key Terms
عالِم
حي
دقائق الصنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fact that God is living.
B) The intricate design of the human body.
C) The necessity of hearing and seeing.
D) The impossibility of ignorance in a creator.

Key Takeaway
The intricacy of creation proves God's knowledge, and life is necessary for possessing the attributes that have already been established.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If God were not living, could He truly know you or care for you? The passage insists that life is necessary for God to have attributes like knowledge. Does your daily life reflect a belief in a living God who sees and hears you? Or do you sometimes act as if He is distant or inactive? Commit to one action today that acknowledges His life—like speaking to Him in du'a with certainty that He hears.
?
The passage states that human minds cannot fully grasp the secrets of creation. Are you truly humble before this truth, or do you secretly believe that science will eventually explain everything? Consider the arrogance of claiming ultimate understanding. Pray for the humility to accept that God's knowledge is infinite and that your intellect has limits.

Proof of God's Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking
Sanusi argues for hearing, seeing, speaking then clarifies their scriptural basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that God must have sensory attributes, but ultimately relies on scriptural evidence for hearing, seeing, and speaking.

Core Idea
God must have sensory attributes to avoid defects, yet their proof is from revelation.

Sanusi now turns to the attributes of hearing, seeing, and speaking. He argues that if God were living but lacked these, He would have their opposites—defects incompatible with God's perfection. However, he then makes an important methodological clarification: for these three attributes, we cannot rely on pure reason alone; we must depend on scriptural proof because God's essence is not known to us in a way that would force us to deduce the necessity of these attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the shift from rational argument to methodological clarification.
The passage contains a conditional argument and then a 'تحقيق' (verification).
2
Understand the argument from opposites
Identify the reasoning: if God is living but not hearing, seeing, speaking, then He would be deaf, blind, mute—defects that imply need for completion. This is impossible for the self-sufficient God.
The phrase 'وإلا لاتصف لكونه حياً بأضدادها' is key.
3
Analyze the methodological turn
Sanusi says 'والتحقيق: الاعتماد في هذه الثلاثة على الدليل السمعي'. Understand why reason alone is insufficient: God's essence is not known, so we cannot rationally judge that He must have these attributes or their opposites.
Compare this with knowledge, which was proven by reason.
4
Distinguish knowledge from perception
Notice the final point: being knowing does not suffice for being hearing and seeing, because there is a clear difference between knowing something absent and perceiving it directly.
This clarifies that these attributes add something beyond knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يجب أيضاً لصانعكَ أنْ يكون عالماً ، وإلا لم تكن على ما أنتَ عليهِ مِنْ دقائقِ الصنع ؛ في اختصاص كل جزء و منكَ بمنفعتِهِ الخاصَّةِ بهِ ، وإمدادِهِ بما يحفظها عليه ، ونحو ذلكَ مِنَ المحاسنِ التي تعجز عقول البشر عن الإحاطة بأسرارها . وحيّاً، وإلا لم يكن بهذه الأوصاف التي سبق وجوبها • وسميعاً بصيراً مُتكلّماً ، وإلا لاتّصف لكونه حيّاً بأضدادِها ، وأضدادها آفات ونقص ، وهي عليه محال ؛ لاحتياجهِ حِينَئِذٍ إلى مَنْ يُكمِّلُهُ ، كيف وهو الغني بإطلاق المفتقِرُ إليه ما سواه على العموم ؟! والتحقيقُ : الاعتمادُ في هذه الثلاثة على الدليل السمعي ؛ لأنَّ ذاتَهُ تعالى لم تُعرَف حتى يُحكَمَ في حقه بأنَّهُ يجب الاتصافُ بأضدادها عندَ عدمِها • ولا يُستغنى بكونه تعالى عالماً عن كونه سميعاً بصيراً ؛ لما نجده ضرورةً مِنَ الفرقِ بينَ علمنا بالشيء حالَ غَيبَتِهِ عنَّا ، وبينَ تعلَّقِ سمعنا وبصرنا بهِ قبلُ
Then it is also necessary for your Maker to be knowing, otherwise you would not be in the state you are in of the intricacies of craftsmanship in the specialization of each part and of you for its specific benefit, and its supply with what preserves it, and the like of those excellences whose secrets the minds of human beings are incapable of encompassing. And living, otherwise he would not possess these attributes whose necessity has preceded. And hearing, seeing, speaking, otherwise being living he would be characterized by their opposites, and their opposites are defects and deficiency, and that is impossible for Him, because then He would need someone to complete Him. How can that be, when He is absolutely self-sufficient, and everything other than Him is in need of Him?! And the verification is: reliance in these three [hearing, seeing, speaking] is on scriptural proof, because His essence, exalted be He, is not known such that it can be judged concerning Him that He must be characterized by their opposites when they are absent. Nor is it sufficient that He be knowing for Him to be hearing and seeing, because we find necessarily a difference between our knowledge of a thing when it is absent from us and the attachment of our hearing and sight to it previously.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and methodological clarification. Sanusi first proves that God must be knowing (ʿālim): the intricate design of the human body and its benefits cannot be the product of chance or ignorance. Then he proves God must be living (ḥayy): otherwise He would lack the attributes already proven necessary (knowledge, etc.). Then he argues that God must be hearing, seeing, and speaking (samīʿ, baṣīr, mutakallim): since He is living, if He lacked these, He would have their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness), which are defects. This is impossible because it would imply need for completion, contradicting God's absolute self-sufficiency. However, Sanusi then states the verification (taḥqīq): for these three (hearing, seeing, speaking), we rely on scriptural proof (dalīl samʿī), not pure reason, because God's essence is not known to us such that we can rationally deduce that He must be characterized by the opposites when the attributes are absent. Finally, he clarifies that being knowing does not suffice for being hearing and seeing, because we experience a clear difference between knowledge of an absent thing and direct sensory perception.

Key Terms
سميع
بصير
متكلم
الدليل السمعي
الغني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's knowledge alone implies He also hears and sees directly, or that reason can prove all attributes equally.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that knowledge and sensory perception are different, and that for hearing, seeing, and speaking, we rely on scriptural evidence.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi say that for hearing, seeing, and speaking, we must rely on scriptural proof rather than reason?
A) Because reason is insufficient to prove any divine attribute.
B) Because God's essence is not known, so we cannot judge that their opposites are necessary when absent.
C) Because these attributes are not necessary for God.
D) Because they are proven by the same argument as knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Hearing, seeing, and speaking are necessary for God to avoid defects, but their proof is based on revelation, not reason alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If God's hearing and seeing are known through scripture, how does that shape your approach to the Quran and hadith? Are you actively seeking to know God through His speech, or are you content with your own assumptions? Resolve to read one passage of the Quran with the intention of knowing His attributes.
?
The passage says defects are impossible for God because He needs no completion. Do you live as if you are self-sufficient, or do you constantly feel in need of others? How does realizing God's absolute self-sufficiency affect your reliance on Him versus reliance on creation? Ask yourself: what am I asking from people that only God can give?

The Three Opinions on Divine Perception (Idrak)
Sanusi enumerates three opinions on God's perception and recommends suspension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi recommends suspending judgment regarding the attribute of perception (idrak) for God, based on the principle that negating defects from God relies on revelation.

Core Idea
When revelation is silent, the safe path is to suspend judgment.

In this passage, Sanusi applies a key methodological principle: when evaluating whether a particular attribute can be negated from God, we rely on revelation. Since revelation explicitly mentions hearing, sight, and speech but does not mention perception (idrak), some scholars argue that perception must be negated or affirmed. Sanusi presents three opinions and argues that suspension is the most prudent position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Recall that Sanusi established earlier that negating defects from God relies on revelation, not independent reason. This principle governs the discussion of idrak.
2
Identify the three opinions
As you read the passage, note the three positions: (1) affirming idrak, (2) negating it because it entails connection with bodies, (3) suspending judgment. Sanusi favors the third.
3
Examine the argument for negation
Some scholars argue that perception entails connection with bodies (ittisal bil-ajsam), which is impossible for God. Sanusi refutes this, saying it does not necessarily entail that.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that the most appropriate opinion is suspension, since revelation does not explicitly address idrak, and the entailment argument is weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يثبتُ كونُهُ مُدْرِكاً عندَ مَنْ أَثْبَتَهُ ، والتحقيق فيهِ : الوقفُ ؛ لما تقدَّمَ أنَّ التحقيق في نفي النقائص الاعتماد على السمع ، وقد ورد في السمع والبصر والكلام ، ولم يرد في الإدراك ، وجزم بعضُهم بنفيهِ لمَّا رآه ملزوماً للاتصال بالأجسام ؛ يعني : ويدخل في العلم ، والحق : أَنَّهُ لا يستلزمه . وبالجملة : فمجموع ما فيه ثلاثة أقوال ، وأقربها الوقف كما قدمناه
And by this His being perceptive is established for those who affirm it, but the verification regarding it is suspension; because it has preceded that the verification in the negation of defects is reliance on revelation, and [revelation] has come concerning hearing, sight, and speech, and has not come concerning perception. And some are decided upon its negation because they saw it as entailing connection with bodies, meaning: and it enters into knowledge. But the truth is that it does not entail that. And in summary: the total regarding it is three opinions, and the most likely is suspension as we have stated before.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion about the attribute of perception (idrāk) in God. Sanusi states that the previous argument (about hearing, seeing, speaking) establishes that God is 'perceiver' (mudrik) for those who affirm that attribute. However, the correct verification (taḥqīq) is to suspend judgment (waqf), because the principle established earlier is that in negating defects from God, we rely on revelation (samʿ). Revelation has explicitly mentioned hearing, sight, and speech, but it has not mentioned 'perception' (idrāk). Some scholars categorically deny perception because they see it as entailing connection with bodies (ittiṣāl bil-ajsām), and they consider perception to be included in knowledge (ʿilm). But Sanusi notes that the truth is that perception does not entail connection with bodies. He then summarizes that there are three opinions on this issue, and the most appropriate (aqrabuhā) is suspension, as previously stated.

Key Terms

إدراك
الوقف
الاتصال بالأجسام
نفي النقائص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That perception (idrak) is equivalent to hearing and seeing and must be affirmed or negated similarly.
Correction: Sanusi shows that idrak is a separate attribute not explicitly mentioned in revelation, so suspension is warranted rather than affirmation or negation.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi recommend suspension (waqf) regarding the attribute of perception (idrak) for God?
A) Because reason definitely proves it.
B) Because revelation explicitly negates it.
C) Because revelation does not mention it, and the rule is to rely on revelation for negating defects.
D) Because all three opinions are equally valid.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi recommends suspending judgment on whether God is 'perceiver' because revelation does not explicitly mention it, and the argument that it entails connection with bodies is not proven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Consider an area of your life today where you lack clear guidance from the Qur'an and Sunnah. Are you willing to adopt 'waqf' and remain silent, or do you feel compelled to have an answer? What is the spiritual danger of insisting on an opinion where Allah has not spoken?

The Attributes of Hearing, Sight, and Perception
Sanusi proves hearing/sight and suspends judgment on idrak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves the necessity of hearing and sight for Allah, and why the attribute of perception (idrak) is a matter of suspension (waqf) rather than affirmation or negation.

Core Idea
Revelation and reason confirm hearing and sight, but perception requires caution.

Sanusi addresses three sensory attributes of Allah. He first argues that Allah must possess hearing and sight based on the impossibility of their opposites and the distinction between knowledge and perception. Then he turns to the attribute of perception (idrak), explaining that the correct stance is suspension (waqf) because revelation only mentions hearing, sight, and speech, not perception. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the three opinions on idrak.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Sanusi moves from hearing/sight to perception.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the proof for hearing and sight
Identify two arguments: 1) Allah's essence is not known unless we judge He must have these attributes when their opposites (deafness, blindness) are absent. 2) Knowledge alone is insufficient because we experience a difference between knowing something in absence and perceiving it through senses.
This shows that knowledge and perception are distinct.
3
Analyze the discussion on idrak
Sanusi presents three opinions on idrak: affirmation, negation, and suspension. He argues that suspension is closest to the truth because revelation does not explicitly mention idrak, and verification of divine attributes relies on revelation.
4
Consider the refutation of the objection
Some deny idrak because they think it entails connection to bodies. Sanusi clarifies that idrak does not necessarily imply that. Reflect on why this objection arises and how it is answered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعي ؛ لأنَّ ذاتَهُ تعالى لم تُعرَف حتى يُحكَمَ في حقه بأنَّهُ يجب الاتصافُ بأضدادها عندَ عدمِها • ولا يُستغنى بكونه تعالى عالماً عن كونه سميعاً بصيراً ؛ لما نجده ضرورةً مِنَ الفرقِ بينَ علمنا بالشيء حالَ غَيبَتِهِ عنَّا ، وبينَ تعلَّقِ سمعنا وبصرنا بهِ قبلُ • وبهذا يثبتُ كونُهُ مُدْرِكاً عندَ مَنْ أَثْبَتَهُ ، والتحقيق فيهِ : الوقفُ ؛ لما تقدَّمَ أنَّ التحقيق في نفي النقائص الاعتماد على السمع ، وقد ورد في السمع والبصر والكلام ، ولم يرد في الإدراك ، وجزم بعضُهم بنفيهِ لمَّا رآه ملزوماً للاتصال بالأجسام ؛ يعني : ويدخل في العلم ، والحق : أَنَّهُ لا يستلزمه • وبالجملة : فمجموع ما فيه ثلاثة أقوال ، وأقربها الوقف كما قدمناه
The [attribute of] hearing: because His essence, exalted, is not known until it is judged regarding Him that He must be qualified with their opposites when they are absent. And it is not dispensed with His being, exalted, knowing from His being hearing, seeing; because we find necessarily from the difference between our knowledge of a thing when it is absent from us, and between the relation of our hearing and sight to it before. And by this, His being perceiving is established for those who affirm it. The verification regarding it: suspension; because what preceded that verification in negating deficiencies relies on revelation, and revelation has come regarding hearing, sight, and speech, and has not come regarding perception. Some asserted its negation because they saw it entailing connection to bodies. Meaning: and it enters into knowledge. The truth: that it does not entail it. In summary: the sum of positions regarding it is three opinions, and the closest is suspension as we have presented.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage discusses God's attributes of hearing, sight, and perception. The author argues that God must have hearing and sight because His essence is not known unless we judge that He possesses these attributes when their opposites (deafness, blindness) are absent. He then distinguishes between knowledge and sensory perception: we experience a difference between knowing something when it is absent and actually hearing or seeing it, so knowledge alone is not sufficient. The third attribute, 'idrak' (perception), is discussed separately. The correct position is 'waqf' (suspension), because verification of negating deficiencies relies on revelation, and revelation mentions hearing, sight, and speech but not idrak explicitly. Some deny idrak because they think it entails connection to bodies, but the author clarifies that idrak does not necessarily imply that. There are three opinions: affirmation, negation, and suspension; the closest is suspension.

Misconception: Some think that idrak is identical to knowledge or that it necessarily implies physical connection to bodies.
Correction: Sanusi argues that idrak is distinct from knowledge (as shown by the experiential difference) and that it does not entail connection to bodies.

Proof of the Attributes of Meaning
Sanusi proves that the seven attributes are accompanied by subsisting meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's rational proof that the seven essential attributes of Allah are accompanied by distinct meanings (ma'ani) that subsist in the divine essence.

Core Idea
Attributes like power and will require distinct realities—otherwise the essence would be a contradiction.

Sanusi now turns to proving that the seven attributes (power, will, knowledge, etc.) are not mere negations or relations but are accompanied by real meanings that inhere in God's essence. He presents two arguments: one from analogy with creation, and another from the absurd consequences of denying such meanings. This passage is central to Ash'ari theology, as it grounds the distinction between the essence and its attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the thesis
Sanusi claims that each of the seven attributes (power, will, knowledge, etc.) has a corresponding meaning that subsists in God's essence. Read the opening sentence carefully.
2
Examine the first argument: analogy
The first proof is from the 'witnessed realm' (shahid): in creation, qualities like power and will are accompanied by distinct realities. By analogy, the same holds for God.
This is a standard Ash'ari argument from empirical observation.
3
Examine the second argument: reductio
If the attributes were identical to the essence, the essence would have to be power, will, knowledge, etc. all at once—an impossibility. A single thing cannot be both an essence and a meaning, as that entails contradictory properties.
This argument is based on the impossibility of a single entity having multiple distinct properties without separate principles.
4
Understand the example
Sanusi references the famous problem of 'blackness-sweetness' to illustrate how a single substance can have multiple accidents, but a single essence cannot be multiple meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ في إثباتِ صفاتِ المعاني ، وملازمتها للمعنوية ] ثم نقولُ : يتعيَّنُ أنْ تكون هذه الأوصاف السبع تلازمها معان تقومُ بذاتِهِ تعالى ، فيكون قادراً بقدرة ، مريداً بإرادة ، ثم كذلك إلى آخرها • إما لتحققِ تلازمهما في الشاهدِ ، وإِمَّا لأنَّها لو ثبَتَتْ بالذاتِ للزمَ أنْ تكونَ الذات قدرةً إرادةً علماً كذلك ثم ما بعدها ؛ لثبوتِ خاصِّيَّةِ هذهِ الصفات لها ، وكونُ الشيء الواحد ذاتاً معنى محال ؛ لأنه يلزمُ أَنْ يُضادَّ وألا يُضادَّ ، وأنْ يستلزم وجود محل ولا يستلزمه ، وذلكَ جَمْعُ بينَ متنافيين ، وأنْ يكونَ الوجودانِ فأكثر وجوداً واحداً على القول بنفي الأحوالِ ، وأصلُ ذلك : المسألة المشهورة بـ ) سوادِ حلاوة ) .
Chapter [On establishing the attributes of meanings and their concomitance with the attribute-of-meaning]. Then we say: It is determined that these seven attributes are accompanied by meanings subsisting in His essence, exalted, so that He is powerful by power, willing by will, and likewise to the rest. Either because of the verification of their concomitance in the witnessed realm, or because if they were established by the essence, then the essence would necessarily be power, will, knowledge, likewise, and what follows; due to the establishment of the specific properties of these attributes for it, and that a single thing being an essence and a meaning is impossible; because it would necessitate that it be opposite and not opposite, and that it necessitate existence of a locus and not necessitate it, and that is combining two contradictories; and that two or more existences be one existence according to the denial of states. The basis of that is the famous problem of 'blackness-sweetness'.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the seven attributes (power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech) must be accompanied by particular meanings (ma'ani) that subsist in the divine essence. Two arguments are given. First, from the witnessed realm (shahid): we observe in created things that attributes like power and will are accompanied by distinct realities; by analogy, the same holds for God. Second, if these attributes were identical to the essence (i.e., God were powerful by His essence without a distinct meaning), then the essence would simultaneously have to be power, will, knowledge, etc., which is absurd because a single thing cannot have multiple contradictory properties. Moreover, a single thing cannot be both an essence (dhat) and a meaning (ma'na), as that would entail combining contradictories. This also leads to the problem of two or more existences being one, according to those who deny states (ahwal). The classic example is 'blackness-sweetness', where a single substance is said to have both blackness and sweetness as separate accidents.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
المعنى
الذات
الشاهد
الأحوال
خاصية
سواد حلاوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming distinct meanings in God leads to multiplicity and composition in the divine essence.
Correction: Sanusi argues that denying distinct meanings leads to a worse absurdity: the essence would have to be multiple things (power, will, etc.) at once. The Ash'ari position avoids this by affirming that the essence is one but has multiple attributes, each with a distinct meaning.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what would necessarily happen if the seven attributes were established by the essence without distinct meanings?
A) The essence would be multiple distinct essences
B) The essence would be power, will, knowledge, etc. simultaneously
C) The attributes would not be eternal
D) The essence would be simple without attributes

Key Takeaway
Sanusi proves that the seven essential attributes require distinct meanings; otherwise, the essence would have to be multiple contradictory things at once.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you truly believe that God's attributes—His power, will, and knowledge—are real and distinct from His essence? Or have you been treating them as mere abstract concepts, not affecting how you trust Him in your life?
?
If you deny that God's attributes have distinct meanings, you would be forced to say that His essence contradicts itself. How does this realization press you to submit to the truth of Allah's perfection, even if your mind cannot fully grasp it?
?
The argument uses logical rigor to defend tawheed. Are you willing to exert such intellectual effort to understand your creed, or do you prefer comfortable assumptions that may undermine God's transcendence?

Refuting the Ta'lil Objection: Concomitance Not Causality
Sanusi refutes the claim that ma'ani would make God contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that divine attributes (ma'ani) would entail causation (ta'lil) of Allah, and why this does not imply contingency.

Core Idea
Dependency in attributes is concomitance, not efficient causality.

This short but crucial exchange addresses a common objection: if Allah has attributes (ma'ani) distinct from His essence, doesn't that make Him dependent on them? The objectors argue that this would imply Allah is caused. Sanusi's reply deals with the meaning of 'ta'lil' itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: 'they said... we said...'
2
Identify the key term: ta'lil
Focus on the word ta'lil. What does the objector mean by it? How does Sanusi reinterpret it?
Sanusi distinguishes two meanings of ta'lil: efficient causality vs. concomitance.
3
Understand the concept of talazum
Talazum means necessary concomitance or mutual entailment. It does not imply one thing gives existence to another.
Think of the relationship between fire and heat: heat does not cause fire, but they accompany each other.
4
See why the objection fails
Once ta'lil is understood as mere concomitance, it does not entail that Allah is contingent (ja'iz). The objection collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِها تعليل الواجب ، وذلك مستلزم جوازه . قلنا : معنى التعليل هنا : التلازم ، لا إفادة العلَّة معلولها الثبوت .
They said: From their existence follows the causation (ta'lil) of the Necessary Being, and that entails its being possible. We said: The meaning of causation here is concomitance (talazum), not that the cause gives existence to its effect.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This is an objection and answer. The objectors argue that if there are meanings (ma'ani) in God's essence, then God would be 'caused' (mu'allal) by these meanings, meaning He is dependent on them, which would make Him contingent (ja'iz). The author replies that the term 'ta'lil' here does not refer to efficient causality where a cause brings its effect into existence. Rather, it is merely a relationship of concomitance (talazum) between the attribute and its meaning. The meaning does not give existence to the attribute; they simply accompany each other. Therefore, no contingency follows.

Key Terms
تعليل
تلازم
علة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any ta'lil (dependency) implies contingency (imkan) in the dependent.
Correction: Sanusi explains that ta'lil here is only talazum (concomitance), not efficient causality, so no contingency follows.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi respond to the objection that ma'ani entail ta'lil of the Necessary Being?
A) He denies that ma'ani exist
B) He says ta'lil here means the meaning gives existence to the attribute
C) He says ta'lil here means simple concomitance
D) He says ta'lil is impossible for the Necessary Being

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the objection by redefining ta'lil as mere concomitance, preserving God's necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before this lesson, did you ever harbor a subtle doubt that God's attributes might make Him 'dependent' on them in a limiting way? How does the distinction between causation and concomitance remove that doubt from your heart, and what specific thought will you use to remind yourself of God's absolute independence when you next recite His names?
?
Imagine you are tempted to think that God's power is somehow 'added' to His essence, as if He needs it to be powerful. How does Sanusi's clarification of talazum force you to correct that thought? Write one sentence you will repeat to yourself when you catch yourself diminishing God's perfection.
?
If you truly believe that God's attributes are neither separate causes nor mere accidents but are inherently concomitant with His essence, how will that change the way you beg Him for mercy or strength tomorrow? Be concrete: what will you say differently in your du'a?
