Second Objection: Ma'ani Entail Multiplicity of the Eternal
Sanusi refutes the claim that ma'ani would make multiple eternal entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that multiple eternal attributes would entail multiple eternal beings, using the analogy of the individual substance (jawhar fard).

Core Idea
Attributes do not multiply the essence; one substance can have many attributes.

This lesson presents a key objection against the Ash'ari position that God's attributes are real and eternal. Critics argue that if attributes are distinct realities, they would make multiple eternal beings, contradicting the consensus that God is one. Sanusi responds with a clear analogy from the physical world, showing that multiplicity of attributes does not imply multiplicity of the subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the objectors' claim is presented and how Sanusi responds concisely.
The response uses a specific technical term: jawhar fard.
2
Identify the objectors' claim and their evidence
The objectors claim that if attributes (ma'ani) were real, they would make multiple eternal entities, and they appeal to consensus (ijma'). Understand what consensus they refer to.
Consensus that God is one is about the subject, not the attributes.
3
Examine Sanusi's rebuttal and analogy
Sanusi states that the described subject does not become multiple by its attributes. He uses the analogy of an individual substance (jawhar fard) which has many attributes but remains one. Reflect on why this analogy is effective.
In kalam, jawhar fard is the indivisible atom, which is simple yet has multiple accidents.
4
Reflect on the key lesson
Understand that multiplicity of attributes does not necessitate multiplicity of the essence. The consensus about God being one refers to the subject, not to the attributes being non-existent or identical with the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : لو وُجِدَتْ لَلزِمَ تكثُرُ القديم بها ، والإجماعُ أَنَّ القديم واحد . قلنا : الموصوفُ لا يتكثَرُ بصفاتِهِ ؛ بدليل أنَّ الجوهر الفردَ يتّصفُ بصفاتٍ عديدةٍ وهو واحد ، ومعنى الإجماع : أنَّ الموصوف بصفاتِ الألوهية واحدٌ .
They said: If they existed, multiplicity of the eternal would necessarily follow from them, and the consensus is that the eternal is one. We said: The described (al-mawsuf) does not become multiple by its attributes; evidence that the individual substance (al-jawhar al-fard) is described with many attributes while being one. The meaning of consensus is that the described with the attributes of divinity is one.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This is another objection and answer. The objectors claim that if there are multiple eternal meanings (ma'ani), then there would be multiple eternal entities, contradicting the consensus (ijma') that the eternal is one. The author replies that the subject (mawsuf) does not become multiple by its attributes. He gives the analogy of the individual substance (jawhar fard, the atom in kalam), which can be described with many attributes (e.g., color, taste, etc.) while remaining one. Likewise, God is one despite having many attributes. The consensus about God being one refers to the subject qualified with divine attributes, not to the attributes themselves being one with the essence.

Key Terms
القديم
الموصوف
الجوهر الفرد
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple eternal attributes would necessarily mean multiple eternal beings.
Correction: The described subject does not become multiple by its attributes, as shown by the analogy of an individual substance (jawhar fard) which has many attributes but remains one.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Sanusi use to refute the claim that ma'ani lead to multiplicity of the eternal?
A) A whole and its parts
B) An individual substance (jawhar fard) with many attributes
C) A body and its limbs
D) A genus and species

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the objection by demonstrating that the subject remains one despite multiple attributes, using the analogy of the jawhar fard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection assumes that if God has many attributes, He must be many things. Do you ever treat God’s attributes as separate beings in your worship—calling on His Mercy without remembering His Justice, or seeking His Power without His Knowledge? How can you unify your devotion to the One who is all these together?
?
The indivisible atom is simple, yet it bears many qualities. Your own soul has many attributes (anger, patience, love) but you remain one person. How does this truth about God being One with many attributes shape your trust in Him? Are you comfortable with a God whose essence you cannot fully comprehend, or do you try to reduce Him to something you can grasp?
?
Sanusi uses logic and analogy to defend the oneness of God. Have you ever doubted God’s oneness because of the apparent multiplicity of His acts and names? After this lesson, what specific thought will you hold onto when such doubts arise? Commit to recalling the jawhar fard analogy the next time you feel confused.

Objection 1: Does Causation Imply Possibility?
Sanusi refutes the first objection regarding causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that attributes would cause the Necessary Being fails because 'causation' means logical entailment, not efficient causation.

Core Idea
Causation in theology means correlation, not creation.

The first objection against distinct divine attributes argues that they would cause God, implying His possibility. Sanusi clarifies that 'causation' here refers to logical entailment, not efficient causation. This distinction preserves God's necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: 'They said... We said...'
Pay attention to the key term 'ta'lil'.
2
Understand the key distinction
Sanusi redefines 'causation' (ta'lil) as 'correlation' (talazum). Why does this refute the objection?
3
Reflect on the implication
If causation only means logical consequence, it does not entail possibility. How does that protect God's necessity?
This is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : يلزم مِنْ وجودِها تعليل الواجب ، وذلك مستلزم جوازه . قلنا : معنى التعليل هنا : التلازم ، لا إفادة العلَّة معلولها الثبوت . قالوا : لو وُجِدَتْ لَلزِمَ تكثُرُ القديم بها ، والإجماعُ أَنَّ القديم واحد . قلنا : الموصوفُ لا يتكثَرُ بصفاتِهِ ؛ بدليل أنَّ الجوهر الفردَ يتّصفُ بصفاتٍ عديدةٍ وهو واحد ، ومعنى الإجماع : أنَّ الموصوف بصفاتِ الألوهية واحدٌ . قالوا : لو وُجِدَتْ لَلزِمَ تعدُّدُ الآلهة ؛ لمشاركتها له في أخص وصفِهِ وهو القدمُ ، وذلك يُوجِبُ الاشتراك في الأعم . قلنا : ممنوع أنَّ القدمَ صفةٌ ثبوتية ، فضلاً أنْ يكونَ صفةً نفسيَّةً ، فضلاً أنْ يكونَ أخصّ . ثم الإيجاب للأخص في باب التماثل ممتنع ؛ لوجودِ الاشتراك في الأعم مع انتفائِهِ في الأخص .
They said: From their existence follows causation of the Necessary, and that necessitates its possibility. We said: The meaning of causation here is correlation, not the cause giving existence to its effect. They said: If they existed, multiplicity of the Eternal would be necessitated, and there is consensus that the Eternal is one. We said: The described does not become multiple by its attributes; evidence: the atom (jawhar fard) is described by many attributes yet is one, and the meaning of consensus is that the described with attributes of divinity is one. They said: If they existed, multiplicity of gods would be necessitated, because they share with Him His most specific attribute, which is eternity (qidam), and that necessitates sharing in the general. We said: It is denied that eternity is a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya), let alone that it is a self-attribute (sifa nafsiyya), let alone that it is the most specific. Then affirming the most specific in the case of similarity (tamathul) is impossible, because general sharing exists while specific sharing is absent.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the existence of attributes distinct from God's essence, along with their refutations. The first objection: if attributes exist, they would cause the Necessary Being, implying possibility. Sanusi answers that 'causation' here means logical entailment, not efficient causation. The second objection: attributes would multiply the Eternal, contradicting the consensus that He is one. Sanusi replies using the analogy of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard), which has many attributes yet remains one, and clarifies that the consensus refers to the described being one, not the attributes. The third objection: attributes would share God's most specific attribute (eternity), leading to multiple gods. Sanusi denies that eternity is even a positive attribute, let alone the most specific, and states that in the case of similarity, affirming the most specific is impossible because the general can be shared without the specific.

Key Terms
التعليل
التلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Causation always implies possibility.
Correction: In theology, causation can mean logical entailment without possibility.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi mean by 'ta'lil' in his response?
A) Efficient causation
B) Logical entailment
C) Creation
D) Possibility

Key Takeaway
Sanusi uses 'ta'lil' to mean correlation, preserving God's necessity from the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever fall into thinking of them as separate causes that affect God? How does this passage challenge your mental model? What would it mean for your worship to truly believe that God's attributes are not separate causes but are identical to His essence?
?
Consider a time you saw a cause-and-effect relationship in your life and assumed it implies possibility or weakness in God. How does Sanusi's distinction between correlation and efficient causation free you from that misconception? What step will you take today to deepen your trust in God's utter necessity?

Objection 2: Do Attributes Multiply the Eternal?
Sanusi refutes the second objection using the atom analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that attributes do not multiply the divine essence, as illustrated by the example of the atom.

Core Idea
Attributes multiply descriptions, not the essence.

The second objection claims that distinct attributes would make the Eternal multiple, contradicting His oneness. Sanusi refutes by showing that a subject can have many attributes while remaining one, using the analogy of the indivisible atom.

Objection 3: Would Attributes Imply Multiple Gods?
Sanusi refutes the third objection by denying that eternity is a positive attribute and invoking the principle of similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why eternity cannot be considered the most specific attribute of God, and why sharing a general attribute does not imply sharing the specific.

Core Idea
Eternity is not a positive attribute, so it cannot be God's most specific.

The third objection argues that if attributes exist, they would share God's eternity, leading to multiple gods. Sanusi denies that eternity is even a positive attribute, and explains that sharing a general attribute does not require sharing the specific.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Note the logical structure: sharing most specific → sharing general → multiple gods.
2
Understand Sanusi's denial
He denies that eternity is a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya) or a self-attribute (sifa nafsiyya). Why does this matter?
If eternity is not a positive attribute, it cannot be used for similarity reasoning.
3
Grasp the similarity principle
In cases of similarity (tamathul), sharing the general does not force sharing the specific. How does this block the objection?
The argument assumes that sharing the most specific implies sharing the general, but the reverse is not required.

Source Text

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Proof of the Eternity of God's Attributes
Sanusi proves the eternity of divine attributes and refutes an objection about potentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's logical proof that all of God's attributes are eternal and why the objection about potentiality fails.

Core Idea
God's attributes are eternal; if any were originated, absurdity would follow.

Sanusi presents a rigorous argument for the eternity of God's attributes. He shows that if any attribute were temporally originated, it would contradict God's eternality. He then anticipates an objection about the possibility of God's essence being devoid of both an attribute and its opposite, and refutes it by showing it leads to infinite regress or circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: assertion, proof, objection, answer.
Pay special attention to the logical connectives (لو, فإن, فإن قلت).
2
Understand the proof
Identify why Sanusi says an attribute cannot be originated. The key is that originatedness implies non-existence at some point, which is impossible for the Eternal.
The proof rests on the impossibility of non-existence for the Eternal.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector suggests that God's essence could be realized without an attribute if He was devoid of both the attribute and its opposite before the attribute came to be.
This objection challenges the premise that the receiver cannot be devoid of both.
4
Examine Sanusi's answer
Sanusi argues that if it were possible to be devoid of one attribute, it would be possible to be devoid of all, because the capacity to receive is uniform. This leads to absurd consequences.
The answer invokes the uniformity of receptivity to avoid circularity or infinite regress.
5
Summarize the conclusion
Conclude that all divine attributes must be eternal. Try to restate the argument in your own words.
This lesson establishes a cornerstone of Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في قدم صفات الله تعالى وبقائها ثم نقول : يتعيَّنُ أنْ تكونَ هذه الصفات كلُّها قديمةً ؛ إذ لو كان شيء منها حادثاً للزِمَ ألا يعرى عنه أو الاتّصافِ بضدِّهِ الحادثِ ، ودليل حدوثِهِ : طريان عدمِهِ ؛ لما علمت من استحالة عدم القديم، وما لا تُتحقَّقُ ذاتُهُ بدون حادث .. يلزم حدوثه ضرورةً ، وقد تقدَّمَ مثل ذلكَ في الاستدلال على حدوث العالم . فإن قلت : إنَّما يتمُّ ذلكَ إذا وجبَ أَنَّ القابل للشيء لا يخلو عنه أو عن ضدّهِ ، ولِمَ لا يُقالُ بجواز خلوه عنهما ثم يطرأ الاتصافُ بهما ، فتُتحقَّقَ ذاته دونهما ، فلا يلزم الحدوث ؟ فالجواب : أنَّهُ لو خلا عنهما معَ قَبولِهِ لهما . . لجازَ أَنْ يخلو عن جميع ما يقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ ؛ إِذِ القَبولُ لا يختلفُ ؛ لأنَّه نفسي ، وإلا لزم الدورُ أو التسلسلُ .
Chapter on the Eternity of God's Attributes and Their Perpetuity. Then we say: It is determined that all these attributes are eternal; for if any of them were originated (hadith), it would necessitate that He either be devoid of it or be described by its opposite which is originated. The proof of its originatedness: the occurrence of its non-existence, because you know the impossibility of non-existence of the Eternal, and whatever whose essence cannot be realized without an originated thing necessarily entails its originatedness. The like has preceded in the proof of the world's origination. If you say: That only holds if it is necessary that the receiver of a thing is not devoid of it or its opposite; why not say it is possible that it be devoid of both, then it becomes described by both, so its essence is realized without them, thus origination does not follow? The answer: If it were devoid of both while capable of receiving them, then it would be possible for it to be devoid of all the attributes it can receive, because the capacity to receive does not differ, as it is self-evident (nafsi); otherwise circularity or infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the eternity of God's attributes. Sanusi argues that if any attribute were originated, God would either be without it (impossible) or be described by its opposite, which would also be originated. The proof of originatedness is the occurrence of non-existence, which is impossible for the Eternal. Moreover, if God's essence cannot be realized without an originated attribute, then that attribute must be originated. He then presents an objection: why couldn't God be devoid of both the attribute and its opposite before the attribute came to exist? Sanusi answers that if that were possible, then God could be devoid of all attributes, because the capacity to receive does not differ; if it could be without one, it could be without all. That would lead to absurdity, and the only way to avoid it is to accept that the attribute is eternal. The objection about circularity or infinite regress is a logical consequence of denying the uniformity of receptivity.

Key Terms
قدم الصفات
الحادث
القابل
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The capacity to receive attributes could differ for different attributes, so God might be without some but not others.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the capacity to receive (قبول) is a single self-evident quality; if it were possible to be devoid of one attribute, it would be possible for all, leading to absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, if an attribute of God were originated, what would be the consequence regarding His essence?
A) His essence would be impossible
B) His essence would be realized without the attribute
C) His essence would be preceded by non-existence
D) His essence would be composed

Key Takeaway
All divine attributes are eternal; any claim of origination leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are eternal, then His mercy, justice, and power have always been perfect and never increase. How does this truth confront your tendency to doubt God's constancy in moments when He seems distant? Will you today choose to trust that His eternal attributes are unchanging, even when your feelings fluctuate?
?
Sanusi warns that if it were possible for God to be devoid of one attribute, He could be devoid of all. When has a doubt crept into your heart that God might lack something He promised — perhaps patience in your trials, or wisdom in your confusion? How does this logical guarantee of His perfect attributes challenge that doubt and demand you repent of it?
?
The proof hinges on the impossibility of God being without His attributes. Knowing that God is perfectly described by these eternal attributes — that He is always seeing, always hearing, always powerful — how does this reality change the way you will speak to Him in prayer today? Will you approach Him with more awe or more familiarity?

Eternity is Not a Positive Attribute
Sanusi refutes the classification of eternity (qidam) as a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi refutes the classification of eternity (qidam) as a positive attribute and the logical reasoning involving specificity and similarity.

Core Idea
Eternity is a negative attribute, not a positive one.

In this lesson, we examine a concise but powerful argument from Sanusi against classifying qidam as a positive attribute. The author uses logical terms (specific/general) to show why eternity cannot be a basis for similarity between God and creation. By understanding this refutation, you will grasp a key distinction in kalam theology: the difference between positive and negative attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the three levels of prohibition: thubūtiyya, nafsiyya, akhaṣṣ.
2
Identify the three levels of prohibited classification
Sanusi says it is prohibited (mamnūʿ) to consider eternity a positive attribute, let alone a self-attribute, let alone a more specific attribute. Trace these three levels.
Each level is more specific than the previous.
3
Understand the logical reasoning
Sanusi then argues that affirming the more specific (akhaṣṣ) in matters of similarity is impossible because the more general (aʿamm) may be shared while the more specific is absent. Think about why this is a problem.
This reasoning shows why eternity cannot be a positive attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : ممنوع أنَّ القدمَ صفةٌ ثبوتية ، فضلاً أنْ يكونَ صفةً نفسيَّةً ، فضلاً أنْ يكونَ أخصّ . ثم الإيجاب للأخص في باب التماثل ممتنع ؛ لوجودِ الاشتراك في الأعم مع انتفائِهِ في الأخص
We say: It is prohibited that eternity (al-qidam) is a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya), let alone that it be a self-attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya), let alone that it be more specific (akhaṣṣ). Then affirming the more specific in the matter of similarity (tamāthul) is impossible because there is commonality in the more general (aʿamm) while it is absent in the more specific.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a claim that eternity (qidam) is a positive attribute. The author states that it is not permitted (mamnūʿ) to consider eternity a positive attribute, and even less so to consider it a self-attribute (nafsiyya), and even less so to consider it a more specific attribute (akhaṣṣ). The reasoning follows: if eternity were a more specific attribute, then in discussions of similarity (tamāthul) between two entities, affirming the more specific would be impossible because while both may share the more general attribute (al-aʿamm), one of them may lack the more specific attribute. This is a logical argument about the impossibility of using a more specific attribute as a criterion for similarity when the more general attribute is common but the specific one is absent.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
صفة ثبوتية (ṣifa thubūtiyya)
صفة نفسية (ṣifa nafsiyya)
أخص (akhaṣṣ)
تماثل (tamāthul)
أعم (aʿamm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'eternity' (qidam) is a positive attribute of God, similar to power or knowledge.
Correction: Sanusi argues it is prohibited to consider it a positive attribute; rather, it is a negative attribute that simply denies non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on considering 'eternity' (al-qidam) as a positive attribute?
A) It is obligatory
B) It is prohibited
C) It is permissible
D) It depends on context

Key Takeaway
Eternity (qidam) is not a positive attribute; it is a negative attribute, and trying to treat it as a more specific positive attribute leads to logical problems in arguments about similarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you reflect on God's eternity, do you ever inadvertently imagine it as an endless extension of time? ?
The author warns against using a shared attribute like 'eternity' to claim similarity between God and other beings. In your own life, have you ever justified a flawed understanding of God by pointing to a shared attribute? What specific step will you take today to uproot any mental tashbih?
?
This lesson shows that a seemingly minor misclassification of an attribute can lead to logical contradictions in your faith. What other areas of your aqeedah might you be treating carelessly? Are you willing to examine your beliefs with this same rigor, even if it disrupts comfortable assumptions?

The Proof for the Eternity of God's Attributes
Sanusi presents the initial proof for the eternity of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that all of God's attributes must be eternal, based on the impossibility of origination.

Core Idea
If any attribute were created, God's essence would depend on a created thing.

Sanusi begins the chapter by establishing a foundational proof: all of God's attributes are eternal. He argues that if any attribute were originated, it would force a contradiction—either the attribute or its opposite would always accompany God, and the very sign of origination is the occurrence of non-existence, which is impossible for the eternal. This lesson unpacks that core argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the logical flow: assumption → consequence → contradiction.
Notice the reference to the earlier proof of the world's origination.
2
Identify the conditional argument
The proof is structured as a conditional: if any attribute were originated, then... Trace the 'if' and the 'then'.
3
Understand the sign of origination
The author says the proof of origination is 'the occurrence of its non-existence'. Why does that prove origination? Connect to the impossibility of non-existence for the eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في قدم صفات الله تعالى وبقائها
ثم نقول: يتعين أن تكون هذه الصفات كلها قديمة؛ إذ لو كان شيء منها حادثا للزم ألا يعرى عنه أو الاتصاف بضده الحادث، ودليل حدوثه: طريان عدمه؛ لما علمت من استحالة عدم القديم، وما لا تتحقق ذاته بدون حادث يلزم حدوثه ضرورة، وقد تقدم مثل ذلك في الاستدلال على حدوث العالم.
فإن قلت: إنما يتم ذلك إذا وجب أن القابل للشيء لا يخلو عنه أو عن ضده، ولم لا يقال بجواز خلوه عنهما ثم يطرأ الاتصاف بهما، فتتحقق ذاته دونهما، فلا يلزم الحدوث معا؟
فالجواب: أنه لو خلا عنهما مع قبوله لهما لجاز أن يخلو عن جميع ما يقبله من الصفات؛ إذ القبول لا يختلف؛ لأنه نفسي، وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل.
وخلو القابل عن جميع ما يقبله من الصفات محال مطلقا في الحادث؛ لوجوب اتصافه بالأكوان ضرورة، وفي القديم؛ لوجوب اتصافه بما دل عليه فعله؛ كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة، ولو فرضت حادثة للزم الدور أو التسلسل؛ لتوقف إحداثها عليها.
وإذا عرفت وجوب قدم الصفات عرفت استحالة عدمها؛ لما قدمنا من بيان استحالة العدم على القديم.
فخرج لك بهذا استحالة التغير على القديم مطلقا.
أما في ذاته: فلوجوب قدمه وبقائه؛ لما مر.
وأما في صفاته: فلما ذكر الآن، ومن ثم استحال على علمه تعالى أن يكون كسبيا؛ أي: يحصل له عن دليل، أو ضروريا؛ أي: يقارنه ضرر؛ كعلمنا بألمنا، أو يطرأ عليه سهو أو غفلة، واستحال على قدرته أن تحتاج إلى آلة أو معاونة، وعلى إرادته أن تكون لغرض، وعلى سمعه وبصره وكلامه وإدراكه على القول به أن تكون بجارحة أو مقابلة أو اتصال، أو يكون كلامه حرفا أو صوتا، أو يطرأ عليه سكوت؛ لاستلزام جميع ما ذكر التغير والحدوث.
Chapter on the eternity of God's attributes and their permanence.
Then we say: It is necessary that all these attributes be eternal; because if any of them were originated, it would necessitate that it is never free from it or from its opposite which is originated, and the proof of its origination is the occurrence of its non-existence; because of what you know of the impossibility of non-existence of the eternal, and whatever essence cannot be realized without an originated thing necessarily entails its origination, and similar has preceded in the proof of the origination of the world.
If you say: That is only valid if it is necessary that the substrate of a thing is never free from it or its opposite, and why not say that it is permissible for it to be free from both and then the acquisition occurs, so its essence is realized without them, thus origination does not necessarily occur?
The answer: If it were free from both while having capacity for them, then it would be permissible for it to be free from all the attributes it receives; because capacity does not differ, since it is essential, otherwise there would be circularity or infinite regress.
And the substrate being free from all attributes it receives is absolutely impossible in the originated [entity] because of the necessity of its being qualified by accidents by necessity, and in the eternal [entity] because of the necessity of its being qualified by what His action indicates, such as knowledge, power, and will; and if they were supposed to be originated, there would be circularity or infinite regress, because their causing to exist depends on them.
And when you know the necessity of the eternity of the attributes, you know the impossibility of their non-existence, due to what we have presented of the impossibility of non-existence for the eternal.
Thus it becomes clear for you through this the impossibility of change absolutely for the eternal.
As for His essence, due to the necessity of its eternity and permanence, as has passed.
As for His attributes, due to what has been mentioned now; and from this, it is impossible for His knowledge to be acquired, i.e., obtained through proof, or necessary, i.e., accompanied by harm, like our knowledge of our pain, or that forgetfulness or heedlessness occur to it; and it is impossible for His power to need an instrument or assistance; and for His will to be for a purpose; and for His hearing, sight, speech, and perception (on the view that holds it) to be with a limb, facing, or connection; or for His speech to be a letter or sound; or for silence to occur to it; because all that entails change and origination.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the eternity of God's attributes and a refutation of objections. It begins with a chapter heading. The author states that all of God's attributes must be eternal. The proof: if any attribute were originated, then the essence of God would either never be free from it (if it is always present) or from its opposite (if it is absent at some point, then its opposite would have to be present, which is also originated). The sign of origination is the occurrence of non-existence after existence. Since the eternal cannot cease to exist, it cannot have an originated attribute. Furthermore, any essence that cannot be realized without an originated thing must itself be originated. This is analogous to the proof for the origination of the world. Then an objection is raised: this reasoning depends on the principle that a substrate capable of a quality is never free from it or its opposite; but perhaps it can be free from both and then acquire them. The answer: if the substrate could be free from both while having capacity, then it could be free from all attributes it can receive, because the capacity is uniform (being a self-attribute). That would lead to the absurdity that a contingent being could be free from all its essential attributes. Moreover, for originated beings, they cannot be free from accidents (akwān) by necessity. For God, He must be qualified by what His actions indicate, i.e., knowledge, power, will. If these were originated, they would depend on other attributes, leading to circularity or infinite regress. Therefore, the attributes must be eternal. From this, it follows that non-existence is impossible for them, hence change is impossible both in essence and attributes. The author then enumerates consequences: God's knowledge cannot be acquired or subject to forgetfulness; His power needs no instrument; His will is without purpose; His attributes are without body parts or speech in letters and sounds, etc., because all such would imply change and origination.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
صفات (ṣifāt)
حادث (ḥādith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's attributes could be created in time, like His actions.
Correction: The passage proves that if any attribute were created, God would be subject to change and non-existence, which is impossible for the Eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the proof that a thing is originated?
A) It is composed of parts
B) The occurrence of its non-existence
C) It is perceived by the senses
D) It has a cause

Key Takeaway
The eternity of God's attributes is proven because their origination would imply non-existence, which is impossible for the Eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every attribute of God is eternal and unchanging, then His mercy, justice, and power have always been exactly as they are now. When you sin or face hardship, do you doubt that His mercy is still fully present? What would change in your life if you truly believed that no circumstance can alter His attributes even a little? Commit to a specific practice this week that reminds you of one eternal attribute daily.
?
The proof shows that God's essence cannot depend on anything originated. Yet you often act as if God's attention or help is temporary—that He might forget or change. Where in your life do you rely on created things for what only the Eternal can provide? What step will you take today to transfer that trust to God's unchanging essence and attributes?
?
The impossibility of non-existence for the Eternal means God never changes. How does this confront your own inconsistency in faith—your moments of spiritual high and low? If God's attributes are constant, why are your hopes and fears so variable? What would it mean for you to anchor your soul in the steadfastness of His eternal attributes rather than in your own changing feelings?

Responding to the Objection about the Substrate
Sanusi refutes an objection against the eternity of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the objection that God could be free from attributes and later acquire them.

Core Idea
If a substrate can be empty, it could be empty of all attributes, which is absurd.

An objector challenges the previous proof: perhaps God's essence can be free from both an attribute and its opposite, and then acquire them later. Sanusi answers by showing this leads to absurdity—if God could be free from one attribute, He could be free from all, but that is impossible for both originated beings (which cannot lack accidents) and for God (whose attributes are indicated by His actions). This lesson focuses on that objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector questions the principle that a substrate is never free from a quality or its opposite. Understand the alternative: it could be free from both.
2
Follow the answer's logic
Sanusi's answer uses the concept of 'capacity' (qubūl). If capacity allows being free from one pair, it would allow freedom from all pairs, leading to impossible consequences.
He mentions circularity and infinite regress.
3
Apply the argument to God
For God, His actions (like creating) indicate He has knowledge, power, will. These cannot be acquired later, as that would depend on prior attributes.

Divine Attributes

5 min
Consequences: Impossibility of Change in God
Sanusi derives the impossibility of change and its consequences for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the eternity of attributes entails the impossibility of any change in God's essence, attributes, and the specific attributes like knowledge, power, and will being free from imperfection.

Core Idea
If attributes are eternal, change is impossible; thus God's knowledge cannot be acquired, His power needs no tool, etc.

Having established the eternity of attributes, Sanusi now draws out major theological implications. Change (taghayyur) is impossible for the Eternal in both essence and attributes. This leads to specific negations: God's knowledge is not acquired or subject to forgetfulness, His power requires no instrument, His will has no purpose, and His attributes are not bodily. This lesson unpacks these consequences, which are central to Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on impossibility of change
The author first states that change is impossible absolutely, then splits into essence and attributes.
2
List the specific negations for each attribute
Notice the attributes listed: knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, speech, perception. For each, a specific imperfection is negated.
These negations follow from the principle of no change.
3
Reflect on why these negations matter
Think about how these affirm God's perfection and transcendence above all creaturely limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في قدم صفات الله تعالى وبقائها
ثم نقول: يتعين أن تكون هذه الصفات كلها قديمة؛ إذ لو كان شيء منها حادثا للزم ألا يعرى عنه أو الاتصاف بضده الحادث، ودليل حدوثه: طريان عدمه؛ لما علمت من استحالة عدم القديم، وما لا تتحقق ذاته بدون حادث يلزم حدوثه ضرورة، وقد تقدم مثل ذلك في الاستدلال على حدوث العالم.
فإن قلت: إنما يتم ذلك إذا وجب أن القابل للشيء لا يخلو عنه أو عن ضده، ولم لا يقال بجواز خلوه عنهما ثم يطرأ الاتصاف بهما، فتتحقق ذاته دونهما، فلا يلزم الحدوث معا؟
فالجواب: أنه لو خلا عنهما مع قبوله لهما لجاز أن يخلو عن جميع ما يقبله من الصفات؛ إذ القبول لا يختلف؛ لأنه نفسي، وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل.
وخلو القابل عن جميع ما يقبله من الصفات محال مطلقا في الحادث؛ لوجوب اتصافه بالأكوان ضرورة، وفي القديم؛ لوجوب اتصافه بما دل عليه فعله؛ كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة، ولو فرضت حادثة للزم الدور أو التسلسل؛ لتوقف إحداثها عليها.
وإذا عرفت وجوب قدم الصفات عرفت استحالة عدمها؛ لما قدمنا من بيان استحالة العدم على القديم.
فخرج لك بهذا استحالة التغير على القديم مطلقا.
أما في ذاته: فلوجوب قدمه وبقائه؛ لما مر.
وأما في صفاته: فلما ذكر الآن، ومن ثم استحال على علمه تعالى أن يكون كسبيا؛ أي: يحصل له عن دليل، أو ضروريا؛ أي: يقارنه ضرر؛ كعلمنا بألمنا، أو يطرأ عليه سهو أو غفلة، واستحال على قدرته أن تحتاج إلى آلة أو معاونة، وعلى إرادته أن تكون لغرض، وعلى سمعه وبصره وكلامه وإدراكه على القول به أن تكون بجارحة أو مقابلة أو اتصال، أو يكون كلامه حرفا أو صوتا، أو يطرأ عليه سكوت؛ لاستلزام جميع ما ذكر التغير والحدوث.
Chapter on the eternity of God's attributes and their permanence.
Then we say: It is necessary that all these attributes be eternal; because if any of them were originated, it would necessitate that it is never free from it or from its opposite which is originated, and the proof of its origination is the occurrence of its non-existence; because of what you know of the impossibility of non-existence of the eternal, and whatever essence cannot be realized without an originated thing necessarily entails its origination, and similar has preceded in the proof of the origination of the world.
If you say: That is only valid if it is necessary that the substrate of a thing is never free from it or its opposite, and why not say that it is permissible for it to be free from both and then the acquisition occurs, so its essence is realized without them, thus origination does not necessarily occur?
The answer: If it were free from both while having capacity for them, then it would be permissible for it to be free from all the attributes it receives; because capacity does not differ, since it is essential, otherwise there would be circularity or infinite regress.
And the substrate being free from all attributes it receives is absolutely impossible in the originated [entity] because of the necessity of its being qualified by accidents by necessity, and in the eternal [entity] because of the necessity of its being qualified by what His action indicates, such as knowledge, power, and will; and if they were supposed to be originated, there would be circularity or infinite regress, because their causing to exist depends on them.
And when you know the necessity of the eternity of the attributes, you know the impossibility of their non-existence, due to what we have presented of the impossibility of non-existence for the eternal.
Thus it becomes clear for you through this the impossibility of change absolutely for the eternal.
As for His essence, due to the necessity of its eternity and permanence, as has passed.
As for His attributes, due to what has been mentioned now; and from this, it is impossible for His knowledge to be acquired, i.e., obtained through proof, or necessary, i.e., accompanied by harm, like our knowledge of our pain, or that forgetfulness or heedlessness occur to it; and it is impossible for His power to need an instrument or assistance; and for His will to be for a purpose; and for His hearing, sight, speech, and perception (on the view that holds it) to be with a limb, facing, or connection; or for His speech to be a letter or sound; or for silence to occur to it; because all that entails change and origination.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the eternity of God's attributes and a refutation of objections. It begins with a chapter heading. The author states that all of God's attributes must be eternal. The proof: if any attribute were originated, then the essence of God would either never be free from it (if it is always present) or from its opposite (if it is absent at some point, then its opposite would have to be present, which is also originated). The sign of origination is the occurrence of non-existence after existence. Since the eternal cannot cease to exist, it cannot have an originated attribute. Furthermore, any essence that cannot be realized without an originated thing must itself be originated. This is analogous to the proof for the origination of the world. Then an objection is raised: this reasoning depends on the principle that a substrate capable of a quality is never free from it or its opposite; but perhaps it can be free from both and then acquire them. The answer: if the substrate could be free from both while having capacity, then it could be free from all attributes it can receive, because the capacity is uniform (being a self-attribute). That would lead to the absurdity that a contingent being could be free from all its essential attributes. Moreover, for originated beings, they cannot be free from accidents (akwān) by necessity. For God, He must be qualified by what His actions indicate, i.e., knowledge, power, will. If these were originated, they would depend on other attributes, leading to circularity or infinite regress. Therefore, the attributes must be eternal. From this, it follows that non-existence is impossible for them, hence change is impossible both in essence and attributes. The author then enumerates consequences: God's knowledge cannot be acquired or subject to forgetfulness; His power needs no instrument; His will is without purpose; His attributes are without body parts or speech in letters and sounds, etc., because all such would imply change and origination.

Key Terms
تغير (taghayyur)
كسبي (kasbī)
ضروري (ḍarūrī)
أكوان (akwān)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think God's attributes are like human attributes, subject to acquisition or change.
Correction: The passage proves that any change in attributes would imply origination, which is impossible for the Eternal.

Checkpoint

C) Because God does not speak
D) Because letters are physical

Key Takeaway
The eternity of attributes entails that God is absolutely unchanging, and thus His attributes are free from all imperfections that imply change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever pray as if your words can change God's mind or add to His knowledge? How does the truth that God's knowledge is not acquired—never learned or increased—challenge the way you approach Him in supplication? What would it look like to truly trust that He already knows and has willed all things perfectly, without your reminders?
?
Reflect on a time you felt God was silent or distant. The passage says His speech is not letters or sounds, and silence is impossible for Him. Could it be that you were measuring His 'speech' by human standards? In light of this lesson, how might you re-examine your expectation of how God communicates?
?
If God's will is not for any purpose—not even to create or to reward—then your existence is not because God 'needed' to create you or because He wanted something from you. Does that humble you or trouble you? How does this truth reshape your sense of purpose? Come up with one concrete way you will live today solely for God's sake, not for any benefit to yourself.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
10 min
Necessity of Eternity of Attributes and Impossibility of Change
Sanusi argues for the eternity of attributes and impossibility of change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's attributes must be eternal and how any change in them is impossible, based on the argument that their origination would lead to circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
God's attributes are eternal because if they were created, they would need themselves to exist.

Sanusi presents a rigorous argument that God's attributes cannot be originated. He begins with the principle that any recipient of qualities cannot be empty of all qualities it can receive. For God, His actions indicate attributes like knowledge, power, and will. If these were created, they would need their own existence to create themselves—an impossibility. This lesson walks through that argument and its implications for each attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the principle about the recipient and the application to originated versus eternal.
2
Identify the main argument
Focus on the logical problem if attributes were originated: either circularity or infinite regress. Understand why that forces eternity.
3
Explore the implications for each attribute
Look at how the author applies the principle to knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, speech, and perception. Note what is impossible for each.
4
Connect to the impossibility of change
Understand that change in essence or attributes is impossible because it would imply origination or non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخلو القابل عن جميع ما يقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ محال مطلقاً في الحادثِ ؛ لوجوب اتصافه بالأكوان ضرورةً ، وفي القديم ؛ لوجوب اتصافه بما دلّ عليه فعله عليه فعله ؛ كالعلم والقدرةِ والإرادةِ ، ولو فُرِضَتْ حادثة للزِمَ الدور أو التسلسل ؛ لتوقف إحداثها عليها وإذا عرفت وجوب قدم الصفاتِ.. عرفت استحالة عدمها ؛ لما قدَّمنا مِنْ بيانِ استحالة العدم على القديم فخرج لك بهذا استحالة التغير على القديم مطلقاً أمَّا في ذاتِهِ : فلوجوبِ قدمِهِ وبقائِهِ ؛ لما مرَّ . وأما في صفاتِهِ : فلما ذُكِرَ الآنَ ، ومِنْ ثَمَّ استحال على علمه تعالى أنْ يكونَ كَسْبيّاً ؛ أي : يحصل له عن دليل ، أو ضروريّاً ؛ أي : يقارنه ضررٌ ؛ كعلمنا بألمنا ، أو يطرأ عليه سهو أو غفلةٌ ، واستحال على قدرته أن تحتاج إلى آلةٍ أو معاونةٍ ، وعلى إرادتِهِ أنْ تكون لغرض ، وعلى سمعه وبصره وكلامه وإدراكه على القول به أنْ تكونَ بجارحة أو مقابلةٍ أو اتصال ، أو يكون كلامه حرفاً أو صوتاً ، أو يطرأ عليه سكوت ؛ لاستلزام جميعِ ما ذُكر التغير والحدوث .
And the devoidness of the recipient from all qualities that it is capable of receiving is absolutely impossible in the originated (al-ḥādith) because of the necessity of its being described by the [four] primary qualities (al-akwān) necessarily, and in the Eternal (al-Qadīm) because of the necessity of His being described by what His action indicates—like knowledge, power, and will. And if they were supposed to be originated, then circularity (al-dawr) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul) would be necessary, because their bringing about would depend upon them. And when you know the necessity of the eternity of the attributes, you know the impossibility of their non-existence, due to what we have already presented of the proof of the impossibility of non-existence upon the Eternal. So it follows for you from this the impossibility of change (al-taghayyur) upon the Eternal absolutely. As for in His essence: because of the necessity of His eternity and His permanence, as has passed. And as for in His attributes: because of what has been mentioned now. And therefore it is impossible for His knowledge, exalted is He, to be acquired (kasbī), i.e., obtained through evidence, or necessary (ḍarūrī), i.e., accompanied by harm—like our knowledge of our pain—or for forgetfulness or heedlessness to occur to it. And it is impossible for His power to need a tool or assistance, and for His will to be for a purpose, and for His hearing, sight, speech, and perception (on the view that affirms it) to be by a limb, confrontation, or connection, or for His speech to be a letter or sound, or for silence to occur to it, because all of that entails change and origination.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of the eternity of God's attributes and the impossibility of change (al-taghayyur) in God. It begins with a general principle: a substrate (qābil) cannot be devoid of all qualities it is capable of receiving. For originated things (al-ḥādith), this is necessary because they must be described by the primary qualities (al-akwān) like motion and rest. For the Eternal (al-Qadīm), it is necessary because His actions indicate attributes like knowledge, power, and will. If these attributes were originated (ḥāditha), a vicious circle (al-dawr) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul) would follow, since their own creation would depend on them. Hence, they must be eternal (qadīma). From the eternity of attributes, it follows that their non-existence is impossible (since non-existence of the eternal is impossible, as previously shown). Therefore, change (taghayyur) is absolutely impossible for the Eternal. The author then applies this to the essence and attributes. For the essence, the impossibility of change follows from the necessity of His eternity and permanence. For the attributes, it follows from the preceding argument. Consequently, each attribute is described with a specific impossibility: knowledge cannot be acquired (kasbī, i.e., derived from evidence) or necessary (ḍarūrī, i.e., accompanied by harm like our knowledge of pain); it cannot be subject to forgetfulness or heedlessness. Power cannot need a tool or assistance. Will cannot be for a purpose. Hearing, sight, speech, and perception (for those who affirm it) cannot occur by limb, confrontation, connection; speech cannot be letter or sound; silence cannot occur. All these would entail change and origination, which are impossible for the Eternal.

Correction: The passage shows that knowledge cannot be acquired or necessary because that would entail change and origination.

D) Because attributes are unnecessary

Key Takeaway
The eternity of God's attributes is necessary; any change in them would entail impossibility.

?
The passage says God's power needs no tool or assistance. In what areas of your life do you still think God's power is limited by circumstances, people, or your own efforts? How would your reliance change if you internalized that His power is utterly independent?
?

The Unlimited Scope of God's Attributes
Sanusi proves the unlimited scope of divine attributes and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why each divine attribute must have an unlimited scope of objects and how Sanusi refutes the objection of a preventer.

Core Idea
God's attributes cannot be limited in what they apply to without contradiction.

This passage from Sanusi's matn establishes a crucial principle: the objects of God's attributes are without limit. After stating the rule, he provides a logical proof and defends it against a hypothetical objection. Understanding this argument reinforces why nothing in existence escapes God's knowledge, power, or will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi lists the objects for each attribute before giving the proof.
2
Identify the claim and the enumeration
The passage claims that each attribute has no limit in its objects. It then specifies: power and will attach to every possible, knowledge and speech to all rational judgments, and hearing/sight/perception to every existent.
3
Examine the proof for lack of limit
Sanusi argues: if the attributes were restricted to only some objects, then what is known to be possible would become impossible, and the attribute would need a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ). This is absurd.
4
Study the objection and its refutation
Someone might say: the attribute could attach to all but a preventer (māniʿ) blocks it. Sanusi refutes: the preventer either opposes the attribute (leading to its non-existence, impossible) or does not oppose (then it has no effect).
5
Understand the additional argument
Sanusi adds: the attachment itself (al-taʿalluq al-nafsī) cannot be prevented by any external factor. In human beings, we become heedless of one known object when attending to another, but that is due to deficiency in the attribute's existence, not the attachment.
This further solidifies that no external preventer can restrict God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تتمة أحكام الصفاتِ ثم نقولُ : يجبُ لهذهِ الصفاتِ الوَحدةُ ، فتكون قدرةً واحدةً ، وإرادةً واحدةً ، وعلماً واحداً ، وكذا ما بعدها ويجب لها عدم النهاية في متعلقاتِها ؛ فتتعلَّق القدرة والإرادة بكل ممكن ، والعلم والكلام بجميع أقسام الحكمِ العقلي، وهي كلُّ واجب وجائز ومستحيل ، والسمعُ والبصر والإدراك - على القول به ـ بكل موجود . أما عدمُ النهاية في مُتعلقاتِها : فلأنَّها لو اختصتْ ببعض ما تصلحُ له.. لاستحالَ ما عُلِمَ جوازه ، وافتقرَتْ إلى مُخصص لا يُقالُ : جازَ التعلق بالجميع ، لكن منع منه مانع لأنَّا نقولُ : المانعُ إِنْ ضادَّ الصفةَ لزمَ عدمُها ، وعدمُ القديم محال ، وإلا فلا أثر له . وأيضاً : فالتعلُّقُ نفسي يستحيل أن يمنع منه مانع ، والمانع في حقنا إنَّما منع وجود الصفة لتعدُّدها بالنسبة إلينا ، بدليل صحة ذهولنا عن أحد المعلومين مع بقاء الآخر ، لا تعلقها وأما دليلُ وَحدتِها : فلأنَّها لو تعدَّدَتْ بعددِ مُتعلّقاتِها لزمَ
Chapter on the Completion of the Rulings of the Attributes. Then we say: Unity is necessary for these attributes, so power is one, will is one, knowledge is one, and likewise what follows them. And it is necessary for them to have no limit in their objects: so power and will attach to every possible, knowledge and speech to all divisions of rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī), which are every necessary, possible, and impossible; and hearing, sight, and perception (on the view that affirms it) to every existent. As for the lack of limit in their objects: because if they were restricted to some of what they are suitable for, then what is known to be possible would become impossible, and they would need a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ). It cannot be said: it is possible for them to attach to all, but a preventer (māniʿ) prevented them, because we say: if the preventer opposes the attribute, then its non-existence would be necessary, and the non-existence of the Eternal is impossible; otherwise, it has no effect. Also, the attachment itself (al-taʿalluq al-nafsī) is impossible for any preventer to prevent, and the prevention in our case is only because of the multiplicity of the attribute relative to us, as evidenced by the validity of our heedlessness of one of two known objects while the other remains, not its attachment. And as for the proof of its unity: because if they were multiple according to the number of their objects, then...
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is divided into two main parts: the necessity of the unity (al-waḥda) of each attribute, and the necessity of their objects (mutaʿalliqāt) having no limit (lā nihāya). First, it states that each attribute is one: one power, one will, one knowledge, etc. Second, they must have no limit in what they apply to: power and will apply to every possible (kull mumkin), knowledge and speech to all divisions of rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī) — which are the necessary, possible, and impossible — and hearing, sight, and perception (on the view that affirms it) to every existent (kull mawjūd). The proof for the lack of limit: if they were restricted to only some of what they are suitable for, then what is known to be possible would become impossible (since the attribute would be unable to apply to it), and the attribute would need a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to explain the restriction. Then it refutes a potential objection: one might say that the attribute could attach to all but a preventer (māniʿ) prevents it. The answer: if the preventer opposes the attribute, then the attribute would cease to exist (which is impossible for the Eternal); if it does not oppose, it has no effect on the attachment. Moreover, the attachment itself (al-taʿalluq al-nafsī) cannot be prevented by any external preventer. In our case (human attributes), prevention occurs only because of the multiplicity of the attribute relative to us (e.g., we cannot attend to two known objects simultaneously), but this is due to the deficiency of the attribute's existence, not its attachment. The passage then begins the proof for unity: if attributes were multiple according to the number of objects, then... (the text cuts off).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a preventer could stop God's attribute from applying to some things, even though God is omnipotent.
Correction: Any preventer would either oppose the attribute (impossible, as it would negate an eternal attribute) or have no effect on the attachment itself.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why can the attributes of God have no limit in their objects?
A) Because God is omnipotent

D) Because they are eternal

Key Takeaway
God's attributes extend to all things that are possible, existent, or rationally conceivable, without any restriction, otherwise impossibility or a need for a particularizer would arise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge covers every possible, necessary, and impossible scenario, then nothing that happens in your life—no doubt, no confusion, no hidden detail—escapes His perfect awareness. What would it mean for you to live today as if that is true? Can you point to one decision you are currently agonizing over that you should instead entrust to His comprehensive knowledge?
?
The passage argues that no external preventer can block God's attributes from acting. Where have you been believing that a person, a circumstance, or even your own sin could limit God's mercy or power toward you? How would your prayer life change if you truly believed that nothing can stop His attributes from reaching you?
?
Sanusi says that any restriction on God's attributes would require a particularizer—but God's attributes are eternal and without cause. Is there an area of God's character—His justice, His patience, His provision—that you have mentally restricted because of your past experiences or disappointments? What would it take for you to tear down that mental barrier and accept that His attributes are truly unlimited?

Reply to Objection: Multiplicity of Divine Attributes
Sanusi replies to an objection about the unity of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine attributes cannot substitute for each other despite human attributes being multiple within the same type.

Core Idea
Attributes differing in genus cannot replace each other without inverting their realities.

Sanusi addresses a potential objection: if human knowledge can be many for many objects, why can't divine knowledge replace other attributes? The answer hinges on the distinction between attributes of the same type and attributes of different genera. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the objection (فإن قلت) and the answer (قلنا).
The objection is presented as a hypothetical challenge.
2
Identify the objector's argument
The objector claims that since human knowledge is multiple due to multiple objects, divine knowledge, being one, could stand for multiple knowledges and even other attributes like power.
The objector extends the argument to absurdity: the essence could replace all attributes.
3
Understand Sanusi's distinction
Sanusi distinguishes between multiplicity within the same type (e.g., knowledge of many objects) and multiplicity across different genera (e.g., knowledge vs. power). The former is due to objects, the latter is due to different realities.
This distinction is key to the answer.
4
Grasp the consequence
If one attribute could replace another of a different genus, it would invert their realities, like calling blackness 'sweetness'. This is impossible and absurd.
Sanusi references a previous discussion (blackness and sweetness).
5
Reflect on the lesson
The objection fails because it conflates multiplicity within a type with multiplicity across types. Each divine attribute retains its distinct reality.
This preserves the unity and distinctness of each attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: العلم مثلاً في حقنا متعدد بحسب تعدد متعلقه، وكذا غيره، فلو قام العلم مثلاً في حقه تعالى مقام علوم.. لجاز أن يقوم في حقه تعالى مقام القدرة وسائر الصفات؛ بجامع قيامه مقام صفات متغايرة، بل ويلزم عليه أن يجوز قيام ذاته مقام الصفات كلها، وذلك مما يأباه كل مسلم. قلنا: الفرق أن التغاير في العلوم لأجل التغاير في المتعلق مع الاتحاد في النوع، فحيث فرضت الوحدة في العلم مثلاً زال التغاير، أما العلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات فمتغايرة في حقائقها جنساً، فلو قام بعضها مقام بعض للزم قلب الحقائق، ولزم ما تقدم في مسألة (سواد حلاوة).
If you say: Knowledge in our case, for example, is multiple according to the multiplicity of its object, and likewise other attributes. So if knowledge in His case, exalted, were to take the place of multiple knowledges, then it could take the place of power and the rest of the attributes, on the basis that it takes the place of diverse attributes; nay, it would follow that His essence could take the place of all the attributes, and that is rejected by every Muslim. We say: The difference is that the diversity in knowledges is due to the diversity in the object while being united in type. So when unity is assumed in knowledge, for example, the diversity disappears. As for knowledge, power, and the rest of the attributes, they are diverse in their realities in genus. So if some were to take the place of others, it would entail the inversion of realities, and what was previously mentioned in the case of 'blackness' and 'sweetness' would follow.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair defending the unity of each divine attribute. The objector argues: human knowledge is multiple (e.g., knowledge of many things) yet still called knowledge, so why can't divine knowledge, being one, stand for multiple knowledges? And by extension, why can't it even stand for other attributes (like power)? That would mean one attribute suffices for all, or even the essence suffices for all, which any Muslim rejects. Sanusi answers by distinguishing between attributes of the same type (type unity) and attributes of different genera. In humans, multiple knowledges differ only by object but share the same type (knowledge). For the divine, if one knowledge is assumed, it covers all objects because the type is one. But knowledge and power differ in genus (their realities are fundamentally different). If one could replace the other, it would invert the essences, like saying blackness is sweetness—a category error. Thus, the objection fails because it conflates multiplicity within a genus (knowledge) with multiplicity across genera (knowledge vs. power).

Key Terms
العلم
القدرة
الوحدة
التغاير
النوع
الجنس
قلب الحقائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since a single divine attribute can relate to many objects, it could also replace other attributes of different genera.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that this ignores the difference in genus: attributes of different genera have distinct realities and cannot substitute for each other.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi differentiate between knowledge and power in response to the objection?
A) They are the same because both are attributes.
B) They differ in genus, so one cannot replace the other.
C) Knowledge is limited, power is unlimited.
D) Power is greater than knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes differ in their realities by genus, so one cannot substitute for another without inverting their essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes, do you ever unconsciously assume that because two attributes share a similar name (like 'knowledge' in humans and in Allah) they are essentially the same? How can Sanusi's careful distinction protect you from that error? What will you do today to check your assumptions?
?
This passage shows that even a seemingly logical argument (the objector's) can lead to absurdity if categories are mixed. Is there an area in your faith where you have accepted a comfortable but flawed analogy about Allah? What step will you take to unlearn it and align with sound theology?
?
The objector's argument seems plausible on the surface. Are there times in your own life when you have been drawn to a superficially appealing idea about Islam without examining its deeper implications? How can you cultivate the habit of looking for 'genus differences' in your understanding of divine matters?

Objection and Answer: Unity of Divine Attributes
Sanusi answers the objection that if knowledge is multiple in us, it could replace other attributes in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the diversity of human knowledge does not allow one divine attribute to replace another.

Core Idea
Attributes differ generically, not just numerically.

This lesson presents a classic objection-and-answer format in kalam. The objector tries to extend the multiplicity of human knowledge to God's attributes, but Sanusi shows a key distinction: the difference between numerical multiplicity within a type and generic difference between types.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is an objection-and-answer (فإن قلت... قلنا) structure.
2
Identify the objection and its reasoning
The objector argues that if human knowledge is multiple due to objects, then in God one knowledge could stand for many sciences, and by extension for all attributes, leading to absurdity. Pinpoint the exact steps of this reasoning.
3
Understand the answer and its key distinction
The answer distinguishes between diversity within a type (numerical) and diversity between types (generic). Ask yourself why this distinction blocks the objection.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : العلم مثلاً في حقنا مُتعدِّدُ بحَسَبِ تعدُّدِ متعلَّقِهِ ، وكذا غيرُه ، فلو قامَ العلمُ مثلاً في حقِّهِ تعالى مَقامَ علومٍ لجاز أنْ يقوم في حقه تعالى مقام القدرة وسائرِ الصفاتِ ؛ بجامع قيامِهِ مَقامَ صفاتٍ متغايرةٍ ، بل ويلزمَ عليهِ أنْ يجوز قيام ذاتِهِ مَقامَ الصفاتِ كلها ، وذلكَ ممَّا يأباه كلُّ مسلم . قلنا : الفرقُ أَنَّ التغاير في العلوم لأجل التغاير في المتعلَّقِ معَ الاتحادِ في النوع ، فحيثُ فُرِضَتِ الوَحدةُ في العلم مثلاً زال التغايرُ ، أمَّا العلم والقدرة وسائر الصفاتِ فمتغايرة في حقائقها جنساً ، فلو قام بعضُها مَقامَ بعضٍ للزمَ قلبُ الحقائقِ ، ولزمَ ما تقدَّمَ في مسألة (سوادٍ حلاوة ) .
If you say: Knowledge, for example, in our case is multiple according to the multiplicity of its objects, and likewise others. So if knowledge, for example, in His (exalted) right were to take the place of multiple sciences, it would be permissible for it to take the place of power and all attributes, by the commonality of its taking the place of diverse attributes. Rather, it would necessarily follow that it is permissible for His essence to take the place of all attributes. That is rejected by every Muslim. We say: The difference is that the diversity in sciences is due to diversity in the object while they are united in type. So if unity is assumed in knowledge, for example, the diversity disappears. As for knowledge, power, and other attributes, they differ in their realities generically. So if some of them took the place of others, it would necessitate the inversion of realities, and what was previously stated in the issue of 'blackness and sweetness' would follow.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (فإن قلت... قلنا) concerning the multiplicity of divine attributes. The objector argues: in us, knowledge is multiple due to multiple objects; if in God, one knowledge could stand for many sciences, then it could also stand for power and other attributes, since it already stands for diverse attributes. That would even allow God's essence to replace all attributes, which is unacceptable. The answer distinguishes: the multiplicity of human sciences is numerical, within one type (knowledge). If unified, that numerical diversity disappears. But knowledge and power differ generically in their realities. If one generic attribute were to substitute for another, it would invert the essences of the attributes, leading to absurdities similar to saying 'blackness is sweetness'.

Misconception: If knowledge can cover many objects, it can also cover other attributes like power.
Correction: Knowledge and power differ in their essential nature (genus), so one cannot replace the other without inverting realities.

Answering the Objection of Dividing the World Between Two Gods
Sanusi refutes the division objection in burhān al-tamānuʿ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that two gods could divide the world is impossible, through Sanusi's two rational arguments.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot divide creation because infinity and interdependence preclude any division.

In the proof of mutual hindrance (burhān al-tamānuʿ), a key objection is raised: why can't two gods simply divide the world to avoid conflict? Sanusi addresses this directly, showing that the supposition is impossible both in principle and in detail. This lesson unpacks his two-part refutation, which relies on the infinitude of divine power and the necessary connection between atoms and their accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector suggests two gods can divide the world to avoid conflict. Notice the wording: 'Why is it not permissible that the world be divided...?'
This is a plausible objection that challenges the proof of mutual hindrance.
2
Understand the first answer
Sanusi says the supposition is impossible because God's power and will are infinite. Any division would imply finitude. Why is that?
The infinitude of divine attributes is a key principle.
3
Examine the second answer
Even if we suppose division, two scenarios are considered: both parts are atoms (jawāhir), or one is atoms and the other accidents (aʿrāḍ). For each, a contradiction arises.
Pay attention to the concepts of tamāthul (similarity) and talāzum (necessary connection).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since both answers lead to impossibility, the objection fails. This strengthens the proof that there can be only one God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : فلِمَ لا يجوز أن ينقسم العالم بينهما ، فيكونَ أحدهما قادراً على أحد القسمين ، والآخرُ على الآخر ، فلا يلزم التمانع ؟
فالجوابُ : أَنَّهُ تقرَّرَ قبلُ استحالة التناهي في مقدوراتِ الإلهِ ومراداتِهِ، فيستحيلُ هذا الفَرْضُ الذي ذُكِرَ في السؤالِ.
وأيضاً : فالقسمان إن كانا معاً في الجواهر لزمَ مِنْ تَعَلُّقِ القدرة ببعضها تعلقها بالجميع ؛ للتماثل ، فيلزم التمانع ، وإنْ كانَ أحد القسمين الجواهر والآخرُ الأعراض .. فذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ ؛ إذ القدرة على إيجاد الجواهر لا تُعقل بدونِ القدرة على أعراضها ، وكذا العكس ؛ للتلازم الذي بينهما ، ثم ذلك لا يدفعُ التمانع عندما يريدُ أحدهما أنْ يُوجِدَ الجوهر ، والآخرُ لا يريدُ أنْ يُوجِدَ عرضَهُ
You may say: Why is it not permissible that the world be divided between them, so that one be capable over one of the two parts, and the other over the other, and thus hindrance does not occur? The answer: It has been established previously [that] the impossibility of finitude in the divine power and will; thus this supposition mentioned in the question is impossible. Also: if the two parts are together in the atoms (jawāhir), then the attachment of power to some of them necessitates its attachment to all due to similarity (al-tamāthul), so hindrance occurs. And if one of the two parts is the atoms and the other the accidents (aʿrāḍ), that is inconceivable, for power to bring into existence the atoms is inconceivable without power over their accidents, and likewise the reverse, due to the necessary connection (al-talāzum) between them. Then that does not ward off hindrance when one of them wills to bring into existence the atom and the other does not will to bring into existence its accident.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (suʾāl wa-jawāb) within the proof of mutual hindrance (burhān al-tamānuʿ). The objector asks: why cannot two gods simply divide the world into two parts, each ruling one part, thus avoiding conflict? The answer gives two points. First, it has been established earlier that God's power and will are infinite (lā nihāya), so any division would imply finitude, which is impossible; thus the supposition is impossible. Second, even if we suppose a division, if both parts consist of atoms (jawāhir), then due to the similarity (tamāthul) of all atoms, if power is attached to some, it necessarily attaches to all—power is indivisible—leading to both gods having power over the same atoms, causing mutual hindrance. If one part is atoms and the other accidents (aʿrāḍ), this is inconceivable because creating atoms requires creating their accidents, and vice versa, due to their necessary connection (talāzum). So when one wants to create an atom and the other does not want to create its accident, hindrance occurs.

Key Terms
برهان التمانع
الجواهر
الأعراض
التماثل
التلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could avoid conflict by dividing the world between them.
Correction: Any division is impossible because divine power is infinite and atoms and accidents are inseparable, leading inevitably to mutual hindrance.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why is it impossible for one god to have power over atoms and the other over accidents?
A) Because atoms and accidents are not real.
B) Because power over atoms necessitates power over accidents due to their necessary connection.
C) Because accidents do not exist.
D) Because the gods would be identical.

Key Takeaway
The division objection against the proof of mutual hindrance fails because divine infinitude and the necessary connection between atoms and accidents make any division impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to divide your loyalties between Allah and something else, thinking you could balance them without conflict? How does Sanusi's argument that any division leads to contradiction expose the impossibility of serving two masters, and what will you do today to cut out competing loyalties?
?
The proof shows that divine power cannot be limited or partitioned. In your own life, are there areas where you subconsciously believe that Allah's authority does not reach—like your private thoughts, finances, or ambitions? How does the absolute infinity of Allah's power demand that you surrender every corner of your life to Him, and what is one step you will take this week to submit that area?
?
When you consider the necessary connection between atoms and accidents, you see that nothing in creation exists independently of Allah's will. How does this truth challenge your tendency to attribute outcomes to secondary causes or to your own efforts, rather than seeing Allah's sole control? What will you do today to consciously acknowledge His sole agency in a specific situation?
