The Validity of Scriptural Proof for Divine Unity
Sanusi states the validity of scriptural proof and records an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate among scholars about whether divine unity (waḥdāniyya) can be proven through scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
Scriptural proof for God's oneness is contested because scripture itself presupposes God's existence.

Sanusi opens a subtle discussion on whether the oneness of God can be established by scriptural texts. Some scholars argue that since the authority of scripture depends on knowing God exists, using scripture to prove an essential attribute of God is circular. This lesson examines that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note that it starts with 'It is valid...' then presents an opposing opinion.
2
Analyze the objection
The objection says scriptural proof cannot establish divine unity because the existence of God cannot be proven by scripture itself. Understand the reasoning.
3
Consider Sanusi's stance
Sanusi calls the objection 'an opinion' (ra'y), neither fully endorsing nor rejecting it. Reflect on why he might present it neutrally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصح إثباتُ هذا العَقْدِ ـ وهو الوحدانية ـ بالدليل السمعي ، ومنعَهُ بعضُ المحققين ، وهو رأي ؛ لأنَّ ثبوت الصانع لا يتحقَّقُ بدونِها ، ولا أثر للدليل السمعي في ثبوتِ الصانع ، فكذا ما يتوقَّفُ عليه ، والله أعلم .
ويصحُ أنْ يُستدَلَّ على الوحدانية بما تقدَّم في وحدة الصفات ؛ فنقولُ : يلزمُ مِنْ تعدُّدِ الإلهِ وجودُ ما لا نهاية لهُ عدداً إن تعدد بعددِ الممكناتِ، أو الاحتياج إلى مُخصص إنْ وقف دونَ ذلكَ ، وكلاهما محالٌ .
It is valid to establish this tenet — namely, divine unity (al-waḥdāniyya) — through scriptural (samʿī) proof, but some verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) have forbidden it, and it is an opinion; because the existence of the Maker cannot be realized without it (i.e., without unity), and scriptural proof has no effect in proving the Maker, so likewise what depends upon it. And God knows best. And it is valid to argue for unity using what was presented earlier concerning the unity of attributes (waḥdat al-ṣifāt); so we say: from the multiplicity of gods would follow either the existence of something infinite in number if they multiplied by the number of possibles, or the need for a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) if they stopped short of that. And both are impossible.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage discusses proofs for divine unity (waḥdāniyya). First, Sanusi notes that it is possible to prove unity by scriptural (samʿī) evidence, but some scholars (muḥaqqiqūn) object that this is not valid because the existence of the Maker (the first necessary proof) cannot be established by scripture (since scripture requires the existence of God to be authoritative), and unity is a necessary concomitant of that existence. So scriptural proof cannot underpin it. However, Sanusi does not fully endorse this objection; he calls it 'an opinion' (raʾy). Then he provides a rational proof: from the multiplicity of gods, we would get either an infinite number of gods (if they correspond to the number of possible things) or the need for a specifier to stop the regress. Both are impossible. The first is impossible because an actual infinite is absurd; the second is impossible because it would imply a creator distinct from them, contradicting their deity. This argument draws on the earlier argument about the unity of attributes (waḥdat al-ṣifāt).

Key Terms
الوحدانية (al-waḥdāniyya)
الدليل السمعي (al-dalīl al-samʿī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that scripture is the primary source for all theological truths, including God's existence.
Correction: In kalam, scripture is used after rational proofs establish God's existence and necessary attributes. Using scripture to prove unity can be seen as circular if unity is needed to validate scripture itself.

Checkpoint
According to the objectors, why can't scriptural proof establish divine unity?
A) Because scripture is ambiguous.
B) Because scriptural proof cannot establish the existence of the Maker, and unity depends on that.
C) Because unity is not mentioned in scripture.
D) Because scripture is only for legal rulings.

Key Takeaway
Scriptural proof for divine unity is debated; some argue it is circular because scripture presupposes what it tries to prove.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses about tawḥīd, do you ever question whether your acceptance is based on the authority of Scripture alone or also on rational conviction? How would you respond if someone used this objection to challenge your faith?
?
This passage suggests that scriptural proof cannot prove God's existence or His essential attributes. Does this unease you? Are you willing to examine the rational foundations of your belief in Allah's oneness, or do you rely solely on revelation?
?
If the method of proving tawḥīd is debated among scholars, what does that imply about your own responsibility to understand the reasons behind your creed? Would you defend your belief using reason if asked, or would you only quote verses?

A Rational Proof for Divine Unity
Sanusi provides a rational proof for divine unity based on impossibility of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's rational argument for divine unity, which shows that multiple gods lead to an impossible consequence.

Core Idea
Multiple gods would imply either an actual infinite or a need for a specifier, both impossible.

Sanusi concludes his discussion by providing a rational proof for divine unity. Building on the earlier argument on the unity of attributes, he demonstrates that assuming multiple gods leads to absurdity. This lesson walks through the logical steps of that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational proof
Read the second part of the passage starting from 'وَيَصِحُّ أَنْ يُسْتَدَلَّ'. Note the conditional structure: if multiple gods, then either infinite or need specifier.
2
Understand the two possibilities
The proof lists two outcomes: if gods multiply to match all possible beings, they become infinite; if they stop, they need a specifier. Both are impossible.
3
Connect to unity of attributes
Sanusi says this argument is based on the unity of attributes discussed earlier. Reflect on how that argument reinforces this one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصح إثباتُ هذا العَقْدِ ـ وهو الوحدانية ـ بالدليل السمعي ، ومنعَهُ بعضُ المحققين ، وهو رأي ؛ لأنَّ ثبوت الصانع لا يتحقَّقُ بدونِها ، ولا أثر للدليل السمعي في ثبوتِ الصانع ، فكذا ما يتوقَّفُ عليه ، والله أعلم .
ويصحُ أنْ يُستدَلَّ على الوحدانية بما تقدَّم في وحدة الصفات ؛ فنقولُ : يلزمُ مِنْ تعدُّدِ الإلهِ وجودُ ما لا نهاية لهُ عدداً إن تعدد بعددِ الممكناتِ، أو الاحتياج إلى مُخصص إنْ وقف دونَ ذلكَ ، وكلاهما محالٌ .
It is valid to establish this tenet — namely, divine unity (al-waḥdāniyya) — through scriptural (samʿī) proof, but some verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) have forbidden it, and it is an opinion; because the existence of the Maker cannot be realized without it (i.e., without unity), and scriptural proof has no effect in proving the Maker, so likewise what depends upon it. And God knows best. And it is valid to argue for unity using what was presented earlier concerning the unity of attributes (waḥdat al-ṣifāt); so we say: from the multiplicity of gods would follow either the existence of something infinite in number if they multiplied by the number of possibles, or the need for a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) if they stopped short of that. And both are impossible.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage discusses proofs for divine unity (waḥdāniyya). First, Sanusi notes that it is possible to prove unity by scriptural (samʿī) evidence, but some scholars (muḥaqqiqūn) object that this is not valid because the existence of the Maker (the first necessary proof) cannot be established by scripture (since scripture requires the existence of God to be authoritative), and unity is a necessary concomitant of that existence. So scriptural proof cannot underpin it. However, Sanusi does not fully endorse this objection; he calls it 'an opinion' (raʾy). Then he provides a rational proof: from the multiplicity of gods, we would get either an infinite number of gods (if they correspond to the number of possible things) or the need for a specifier to stop the regress. Both are impossible. The first is impossible because an actual infinite is absurd; the second is impossible because it would imply a creator distinct from them, contradicting their deity. This argument draws on the earlier argument about the unity of attributes (waḥdat al-ṣifāt).

Key Terms
المخصص (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ)
وحدة الصفات (waḥdat al-ṣifāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that multiple gods could coexist without contradiction.
Correction: This proof shows that multiple gods lead to logical impossibilities, thus divine unity is rationally necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the second impossible consequence of multiple gods according to the rational proof?
A) They would be equal.
B) They would be finite but need a specifier.
C) They would be infinite.
D) They would be all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
The rational proof for divine unity shows that multiple gods lead to an infinite regress or dependence on a specifier, both absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that if there were multiple gods, they would either be infinite (impossible) or need a specifier. This leaves no logical room for polytheism. But where in your life do you act as if there are multiple 'gods' pulling you in different directions? Which attachments or fears compete with your acknowledgment of the One? How will you use this logical certainty to dethrone them today?
?
The argument relies on the impossibility of an actual infinite. Have you ever felt overwhelmed by the infinitude of your own desires or worries? How does knowing that only God is infinite and all else is limited change the way you prioritize your commitments? What finite thing will you stop treating as infinite starting now?
?
If a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) would be needed to choose among finite gods, then any god that is not self-sufficient is not truly divine. Does your worship of God reflect a belief that He is absolutely self-sufficient, or do you sometimes act as if He needs your obedience or praise? How will you realign your practice to honor His utter independence?

Necessary and Impossible as Opposites
Sanusi states that knowing what is necessary for Allah entails knowing what is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that for Allah's attributes, the necessary and the impossible are logical opposites, and knowing one directly implies the other.

Core Idea
Necessity and impossibility are logical opposites in divine attributes.

This brief but powerful statement by Sanusi draws a crucial logical connection. He has just enumerated several attributes that are necessary for Allah. Now he explains that these necessary attributes immediately tell us what cannot be true of Allah – their opposites. Understanding this relationship is key to building a consistent theological framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi links necessity and impossibility.
The phrase 'وهو ضدُّ ذلك الواجب' explicitly states the opposite relationship.
2
Identify the logical relationship
The passage asserts that knowing what is necessary (wājib) for Allah yields knowledge of what is impossible (mustaḥīl). This is not a mere coincidence but a direct implication: impossibility is the opposite of necessity.
3
Reflect on the broader context
Consider why Sanusi places this statement after enumerating divine attributes. It serves as a transition to discuss impossible attributes, showing the logical coherence of the entire discussion.
This relationship is foundational for 'ilm al-kalam.
4
Apply to an example
Take one necessary attribute, e.g., existence. Its opposite is non-existence, which is impossible for Allah. See how the principle works in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلُّهُ ممَّا هذا الذي ذُكِرَ في أوصافه تعالى إلى هنا هو يجب في حقِّهِ تعالى ، وإذا عُلِمَ ما يجبُ عُلِمَ ما يستحيلُ ؛ وهو ضدُّ ذلك الواجب
All of it, from what has been mentioned in His attributes up to here, is necessary in His right, and when what is necessary is known, what is impossible is known; and it is the opposite of that necessary.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This brief passage is a concluding statement regarding the divine attributes discussed in the preceding section. It asserts that everything mentioned up to this point concerning Allah's attributes is necessary (wājib) for Him. Then it explains an epistemological principle: when one knows what is necessary for Allah, one thereby knows what is impossible (mustaḥīl) for Him, because impossibility is simply the opposite of necessity. The passage thus forms a transition from the discussion of necessary attributes to the recognition of their corresponding opposites, preparing the reader for the following topics.

Key Terms
يجب
يستحيل
أوصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That necessity and impossibility are unrelated categories; they are independent determinations about Allah.
Correction: Sanusi shows they are direct opposites: impossibility is the opposite of necessity. Knowing one gives knowledge of the other.

Checkpoint

A) By studying creation.
B) By knowing what is necessary for Him.
C) By revelation only.
D) By logical deduction from His essence.

Key Takeaway
Knowing what is necessary for Allah directly reveals what is impossible, because impossibility is the opposite of necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This passage shows that affirming necessity and negating impossibility are two sides of the same coin. How will you ensure that your heart rejects all impossibilities as firmly as it affirms all necessities? Make a specific commitment.

From Wajib to Mustahil: The Logical Opposite
Sanusi concludes the discussion of divine attributes by linking the wajib to its opposite, the mustahil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical relationship between what is necessary (wajib) and what is impossible (mustahil) for Allah, and how knowing one leads to knowing the other.

Core Idea
The impossible is simply the opposite of the necessary — know one, you know the other.

Sanusi concludes his discussion of divine attributes by revealing a key logical principle. Everything discussed so far about Allah's attributes is what is necessary for Him. But this principle also gives us a direct way to know what is impossible for Him: it is simply the opposite of what is necessary. This insight forms the backbone of the method for deriving the full set of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Sanusi states a conclusion; he is not introducing new attributes but defining a relationship.
The passage is compact and uses key terms: واجب, مستحيل, ضد.
2
Identify the two categories mentioned
The passage mentions two rational categories: wajib (necessary) and mustahil (impossible). Understand that they are opposites.
3
Understand the logical link
Sanusi says when you know the wajib, you know the mustahil because the mustahil is its opposite (ضد). This means the set of wajib attributes and the set of mustahil attributes are directly correlated.
For every wajib attribute, its negation is mustahil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الذي ذُكِرَ في أوصافه تعالى إلى هنا هو يجب في حقِّهِ تعالى ، وإذا عُلِمَ ما يجبُ عُلِمَ ما يستحيلُ ؛ وهو ضدُّ ذلك الواجب .
This which has been mentioned concerning His attributes, may He be exalted, up to this point, is what is necessary (wajib) with respect to Him, may He be exalted. And when what is necessary is known, what is impossible (mustahil) is known; and it is the opposite of that necessary thing.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a concluding statement that defines the logical relationship between the necessary attributes (wajib) and the impossible attributes (mustahil) for Allah. The author states that everything that has been discussed so far regarding the attributes of Allah constitutes what is wajib (necessary) for Him. Then he states a key methodological principle: when you know what is wajib, you can derive what is mustahil (impossible) for Him, because the mustahil is simply the opposite (ضد) of the wajib. This establishes a binary framework: for each wajib attribute, its negation is mustahil. This principle is foundational in kalam for deriving the full set of divine attributes.

Key Terms
واجب
مستحيل
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the mustahil attributes are unrelated to the wajib attributes, requiring separate evidence.
Correction: Sanusi emphasizes they are direct opposites: knowing the wajib automatically gives knowledge of the mustahil.

Checkpoint

A) By studying the Quran and Sunnah
B) By knowing what is necessary (wajib) for Him, since the impossible is its opposite
C) By examining the physical world
D) By consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Knowing the necessary attributes of Allah directly gives knowledge of the impossible ones, because they are opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does knowing that the mustahil is simply the opposite of the wajib change your understanding of tawhid? Commit today to reflect on both affirmation and negation when you say 'Allah is ...' and 'Allah is not ...'.
?
Is there an attribute you have assumed could be absent in Allah (e.g., that He might not know something) that this principle shows is impossible? How can you guard your heart against such thoughts? Write down one mustahil attribute you will actively affirm as impossible today.
?
Does your heart truly appreciate that Allah is free from every defect? How does the fact that mustahil attributes are directly derived from the wajib deepen your awe and love for Him? Make a sincere dua asking Allah to grant you certainty in His perfection and to protect you from attributing deficiency to Him.

Proof of the Permissibility of Seeing Allah from Texts
Sanusi states the permissibility of seeing Allah and provides textual proofs from Quran, Musa, ijma, and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four textual proofs Sanusi uses to establish the permissibility of seeing Allah and the methodological principle that multiple apparent evidences yield certainty.

Core Idea
Multiple textual proofs converge to make the permissibility of seeing Allah a certain doctrine.

Sanusi opens a new chapter on the permissibility of seeing Allah (ru'yat Allah) in the Hereafter. He first clarifies that seeing Allah is possible but without direction or corporeality, then he marshals four distinct proofs from the Quran, the request of Musa, the consensus of the salaf, and prophetic hadith. Finally, he explains why the accumulation of these evidences makes the doctrine certain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read its translation. Notice the structure: a ruling, a qualification, then four proofs, then a methodological note.
The Arabic is concise; each proof is introduced with 'لـ' (because of).
2
Identify the four proofs
Go through the passage and list each proof Sanusi gives: (1) Quran 75:23, (2) Musa's request, (3) consensus of the salaf, (4) hadith 'You will see your Lord'. Understand why each supports the permissibility.
3
Understand the qualification
Sanusi says seeing Allah is 'not in a direction nor in a face-to-face manner'. This avoids anthropomorphism. Ask yourself why this is necessary.
This guards against misconceptions that seeing Allah implies physicality.
4
Grasp the methodological principle
The final sentence states that when many apparent evidences (zawahir) converge, they produce certainty (qat'). Why is this principle important for kalam?
It shows how multiple probable texts can together yield a definitive belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في جواز رؤيته جل وعز ويجوز في حقه تعالى أن يُرى بالأبصار على ما يليق به جل وعلا ، لا في جهة ولا مقابلة ؛ لقوله تعالى : ﴿ إِلَى رَبِّهَا ناظِرَةٌ ﴾ ، ولسؤال موسى عليه السلام لها ؛ إذ لو كانت مستحيلة ما جهل أمرها ، ولإجماع السلف الصالح قبل ظهور البدع على ابتهالهم إلى الله تعالى وطلبهم النظر إلى وجهه الكريم ، ولحديث : « سَتَرَوْنَ رَبَّكُمْ » ونحوه مما ورد ، والظواهر إذا كثرت في شيء أفادت القطع به .
A chapter on the permissibility of seeing Him, glory and majesty. And it is permissible with respect to Him, the Exalted, to be seen by eyesight in a manner befitting Him, glory and majesty, not in a direction nor in a face-to-face manner. Because of His statement, the Exalted: 'To their Lord they are looking' [al-Qiyama 75:23], and because of the request of Moses, peace be upon him, for seeing Him; for if it had been impossible, he would not have been ignorant of its matter. And because of the consensus of the righteous predecessors before the emergence of innovations, their supplication to Allah and their request to look at His noble face. And because of the hadith: 'You will see your Lord' and similar reports that have come. And when multiple apparent evidences (zawahir) converge on a matter, they yield certainty (qat') about it.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on the permissibility of seeing Allah (ru'yat Allah) in the Hereafter. Sanusi first affirms that it is permissible (ja'iz) for Allah to be seen by the eyes (absar) in a manner befitting His majesty, not in a direction or face-to-face as physical beings are seen. Then he provides four textual proofs: (1) The Quranic verse 'To their Lord they are looking' (75:23) which indicates that the believers will see Allah on the Day of Resurrection. (2) The request of Prophet Musa (Moses) to see Allah in this world, as mentioned in the Quran. If seeing Allah were inherently impossible (mustahil), then a prophet like Musa would not have been ignorant of that fact; he would have known it is impossible and would not have asked. (3) The consensus (ijma') of the righteous predecessors (salaf salih) before the emergence of innovations; they used to supplicate and ask Allah to allow them to look at His noble face. This demonstrates their belief in the possibility of seeing Him. (4) The hadith 'You will see your Lord' and similar narrations. Finally, Sanusi notes a methodological principle: when many probable evidences (zawahir) converge on a single matter, they yield certainty (qat'). Thus, the multiplicity of these textual proofs establishes the permissibility of seeing Allah as a certain doctrine.

Key Terms
رؤية
جواز
إجماع
ظواهر
قطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing Allah implies that Allah has a physical body, direction, or location.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly says seeing Allah is 'not in a direction nor in a face-to-face manner' but in a way befitting His majesty, thus denying any corporeality.

Checkpoint
What methodological principle does Sanusi use to argue that the textual proofs for seeing Allah yield certainty?
A) The Quran is clear and unambiguous
B) The hadith are mutawatir
C) Multiple apparent evidences (zawahir) when they converge on a matter give certainty (qat')
D) The consensus of the salaf is infallible

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of seeing Allah is established as a certain doctrine through the convergence of multiple textual proofs, even though each alone is only probable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long to see Allah, or has the love of this world distracted your heart from that ultimate goal? What practical step can you take today to increase your longing for the vision of your Lord?
?
The salaf used to supplicate asking to look at Allah's noble face. When is the last time you made such a du'a with sincerity and longing? Commit to making this du'a after every prayer for the next week.
?
If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow, would your faith in the permissibility of seeing Him be merely intellectual or a deep conviction that transforms your life? How does this belief affect your worship today?

Refuting the Objection from 'La Tudrikuhu al-Absar'
Sanusi refutes the objection that verse 6:103 negates seeing Allah by explaining 'idrak' means encompassing perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that Quran 6:103 negates the possibility of seeing Allah by distinguishing between 'idrak' and 'ru'ya' and by offering two alternative interpretations.

Core Idea
The verse negates encompassing perception, not vision itself.

A common objection to the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter is the Quranic verse 'Vision perceives Him not' (6:103). Sanusi addresses this by clarifying the precise meaning of 'idrak' (perception) versus 'ru'ya' (vision). He then provides two lines of reasoning to show that the verse does not contradict the proofs for the beatific vision. This lesson unpacks his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two answers
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi gives two distinct answers to the objection. The first focuses on the meaning of 'idrak', the second on the scope of the negation.
The passage is concise but packed with theological nuance.
2
Understand the distinction between idrak and ru'ya
Sanusi argues that 'idrak' is more specific than 'ru'ya' because it implies encompassing (ihata). Therefore, the verse only negates comprehensive perception, not mere vision.
This is the primary argument and a key concept in Ash'ari theology.
3
Analyze the second answer's conditional logic
Even if 'idrak' were synonymous with 'ru'ya', Sanusi offers two possibilities: the verse refers only to this world, or it negates all instances of vision but not the absolute possibility (a logical distinction between 'kull' and 'kulliyya').
This shows Sanusi's thoroughness in defending the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يعارضها قوله تعالى : ﴿ لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ لأنَّ الإدراك أخص ؛ لإشعاره بالإحاطة ، ولا شك أنَّها منفية مطلقاً . سلَّمنا أنَّهُ الرؤيةُ ، لكن المراد في الدنيا ، أو هو مِنْ بابِ الكل لا الكلية
And verse 'Vision perceives Him not' [al-An'am 6:103] does not contradict these proofs, because 'idrak' (perception) is more specific (akhass) due to its implication of encompassing (ihata), and there is no doubt that it is absolutely negated. Even if we grant that it means vision (ru'ya), then the intended meaning is in this world, or it is of the type 'all' but not 'totality' (i.e., negating all instances but not absolutely).
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection raised against the permissibility of seeing Allah based on the Quranic verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Vision perceives Him not). Sanusi provides two answers. First, he argues that the word used in the verse is 'idrak' (perception), not 'ru'ya' (vision). 'Idrak' is more specific (akhass) because it implies encompassing (ihata) and complete comprehension. So the verse negates complete encompassing perception, not simply seeing. And there is no doubt that such encompassing perception of Allah is absolutely negated (munfiyatan mutlaqan). Second, even if we grant (sallamna) that 'idrak' here means 'ru'ya' (vision), then the verse can be interpreted either as referring to this worldly life (fi al-dunya) only, not the Hereafter, or it is a negation of 'all' (kull) in a general sense but not 'totality' (kulliyya), meaning it negates all instances but not absolutely—this is a nuanced logical distinction. Thus, the verse does not actually contradict the proof for the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

Key Terms
إدراك
رؤية
إحاطة
كل
كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that Quran 6:103 absolutely prohibits seeing Allah in any form, including in the Hereafter.
Correction: The verse uses the word 'idrak' which implies encompassing perception, not simple vision. Moreover, even if it meant vision, it can be interpreted as referring only to this world or as a negation of every instance but not the absolute possibility.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the primary difference between 'idrak' (perception) and 'ru'ya' (vision) in the context of the verse?
A) 'Idrak' implies direction and face-to-face
B) 'Idrak' is more specific and implies encompassing (ihata)
C) 'Ru'ya' is impossible while 'idrak' is possible
D) They are synonyms

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' does not negate seeing Allah in the Hereafter because 'idrak' means all-encompassing perception, not mere vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar', does your heart immediately assume that seeing Allah is impossible forever, or do you pause to consider the scholarly distinction between idrak and ru’ya? How can you train yourself to seek deeper understanding before settling on a conclusion that might limit your hope of beholding your Lord in Jannah? Write one specific action you will take this week to study the nuances of Allah’s attributes rather than relying on surface-level readings.
?
Sanusi offers two interpretations even after granting the opponent’s assumption that idrak means vision. In your own life, are you as generous in seeking valid interpretations when you encounter verses that seem to challenge your beliefs? When a difficult text confronts you, do you quickly dismiss it or do you actively search for scholarly explanations that reconcile it with established truths? Commit to not rushing to judgment about a verse’s meaning until you have consulted trusted scholars and their reasoning.

Refutation of the 'Lan Tarani' Objection
Sanusi refutes the objection from Musa's verse 'Lan tarani' by showing it applies only to the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'Lan tarani' does not negate the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter, based on contextual and logical analysis.

Core Idea
The verse 'Lan tarani' is contextually limited to this world, not an absolute denial.

One of the strongest objections to the Sunni belief in the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter is the verse in which Allah tells Musa, 'You shall not see Me.' In this passage, Sanusi demonstrates that the verse is specifically a response to Musa's request in this world, and therefore it does not preclude the beatific vision in the afterlife. He supports his argument with a principle from logic about temporal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Pay close attention to the key terms: mutabaqa, naqid, and waqtiyya.
2
Understand Sanusi's reasoning
Identify why Sanusi argues that the reply is limited to this world. Notice the linguistic distinction between 'Lan tarani' and alternative phrasings like 'Lan ura'.
The principle of mutabaqa (accordance) ties the reply to the context of Musa's request.
3
Connect the logical principle
Reflect on how the logical rule about the contradictory of a temporal proposition supports the argument. How does restricting the negation to a specific time reinforce the point?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا قوله جل وعزّ : ﴿ لَن تَرَانِي ﴾ لأنَّ المراد في الدنيا ؛ إذْ هو المسؤول لموسى عليه السلام ، والأصل في الجواب المطابقة ، ولهذا قالَ : لَن تَرَينِي ولم يقل : لنْ أُرى ، أو : لن تمكن رؤيتي ، وقد يُتَأَنَّسُ لذلكَ بما تقرَّرَ في المنطقِ : أَنَّ نقيض الوقتيّةِ يُؤخَذُ فِيهِ وقتها المعيَّنُ .
And neither does His statement, might and majesty, 'You shall not see Me' [al-A'raf 7:143] contradict it, because the intended meaning is in this world, since it was a reply to Musa, peace be upon him. And the principle in a reply is accordance (mutabaqa). And for this reason He said, 'You shall not see Me' and did not say, 'I shall not be seen' or 'My vision is not possible.' And one may find support for this in what is established in logic: that the contradictory of a temporal proposition takes into account its specified time.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage addresses another objection based on the verse in which Allah responds to Prophet Musa's request to see Him in this life by saying 'Lan tarani' (You shall not see Me). Sanusi argues that this verse refers specifically to the worldly life, because it was a direct response to Musa's request made in this world. The basic principle (asl) in a response is that it should match the context (mutabaqa). So the reply is confined to this world. Sanusi highlights that Allah said 'Lan tarani' (passive form) and did not say 'Lan ura' (I will not be seen) or 'Lan tumkinu ru'yati' (My vision is not possible), which would be a more general negation. The specific wording indicates that only Musa's seeing in this world is negated. Furthermore, Sanusi draws an analogy from logic: the negation (naqid) of a temporal proposition (waqtiyya) takes into account its specified time. Just as a temporal statement is restricted to its time, the negation of the request is limited to that worldly context, leaving open the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

Key Terms
مطابقة
نقيض
وقتيّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Lan tarani' is an absolute negation of all vision of Allah, both in this world and the Hereafter.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the verse is a specific reply to Musa's request in this world, and therefore it does not negate the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that the verse 'Lan tarani' does not negate the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter?
A) Because the word 'lan' does not imply eternity
B) Because the reply was specific to Musa's request in this world
C) Because Musa was not a prophet
D) Because the verse was abrogated

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that the verse 'Lan tarani' is context-bound to this world, thus it does not contradict the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The principle of mutabaqa shows that Allah's reply to Musa was perfectly suited to his request. Reflect on a time when you asked Allah for something and received an answer that seemed like a 'no' or a limitation. How might that answer actually be perfectly matched to your worldly situation, even if you did not realize it? Write down one unanswered prayer and consider how it might be a 'Lan tarani' answer for this world, not for the next. What would change if you truly believed that?
?
Sanusi uses rigorous logic to defend a belief about the afterlife. Are you willing to apply the same careful reasoning to your own beliefs about Allah? Identify one belief you hold about Allah that you have never critically examined. What would it mean to test it with the same care Sanusi applies here? Commit to one step you will take this week to examine that belief.
?
The wording 'Lan tarani' is specific to Musa. In what specific areas of your life have you claimed general prohibitions from Allah that are actually context-bound? Could you be using a verse or a hadith to close a door that Allah actually left open for a different time or place? Choose one area (like a relationship, a career move, or a personal goal) where you have assumed a blanket rule, and ask yourself: Is this really a universal prohibition, or is it a 'Lan tarani' for now? What would change if you saw it as temporary?

Sanusi's Critique of the Rational Proof for Seeing Allah
Sanusi criticizes the rational proof citing insufficiency of existence as cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational proof based on existence is considered weak in Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
Existence cannot cause visibility because it is identical to the existent.

Sanusi has already presented textual evidence for the permissibility of seeing Allah. Now he examines a well-known rational proof and finds it flawed. This passage introduces a key logical principle: a cause must be distinct from its effect, otherwise the argument becomes circular.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation once to get the gist. Notice the structure: the proof is stated, then dismissed, then the reason is given.
The ellipsis in the Arabic is likely a pause; the meaning is clear.
2
Understand the proof being criticized
The rational proof claims that the factor making an object visible is its existence (wujud). Since Allah exists, He can be seen. Grasp this reasoning before seeing the refutation.
3
Identify Sanusi's objection
Sanusi says the proof is weak because existence is identical to the existent (al-wujud 'ayn al-mawjud). That means existence is not a separate attribute; it is the very reality of the thing. Therefore, it cannot serve as a cause ('illa).
A cause must be distinct from its effect.
4
Reflect on the theological method
Sanusi shows that rational proofs are not always reliable. He distances himself from this argument and relies on textual evidence from revelation. This highlights the Ash'ari approach: reason is used to understand revelation, not to override it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إثباتها بالدليل العقلي المشهورِ ؛ وهو أَنَّ مُصحِّحَ الرؤيةِ الوجود .. فضعيفٌ ؛ لأنَّ الوجود عين الموجودِ ، فلا يصلح علَّةً .
As for proving it (i.e., the permissibility of seeing Allah) by the well-known rational proof, which is that that which makes vision valid (musahhih al-ru'ya) is existence (wujud) — it is weak, because existence is identical to the existent (al-wujud 'ayn al-mawjud), so it cannot serve as a cause ('illa).
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage evaluates the well-known rational argument used to prove the permissibility of seeing Allah. The argument states: 'The factor that makes an object capable of being seen (musahhih al-ru'ya) is its existence (wujud). Since Allah exists, He is capable of being seen.' Sanusi dismisses this proof as weak (da'if). His reasoning is that existence is identical to the existent (al-wujud 'ayn al-mawjud) — i.e., existence is not a separate attribute but is the very reality of the existent. Therefore, existence cannot serve as a cause ('illa) that makes vision possible because the cause must be distinct from the effect. The argument is circular: if the existent is just existence, then saying 'existence makes it visible' is like saying 'the existent makes itself visible,' which is not a valid causal explanation. Thus, Sanusi distances himself from this rational proof, relying instead on the textual evidences he presented earlier.

Key Terms
مصحّح الرؤية
وجود
موجود
علّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rational proof that existence makes an object visible is logically sound.
Correction: Sanusi shows it is weak because existence is identical to the existent, so it cannot be a separate cause.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi consider the rational proof based on existence as weak?
A) Because existence is not a real attribute
B) Because existence is identical to the existent, so it cannot be a cause
C) Because the proof contradicts the Quran
D) Because the proof requires direction

Key Takeaway
Sanusi rejects the rational proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah based on existence because existence is identical to the existent, making it an invalid cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi dismissed the rational proof because existence is identical to the existent. What will you do to ensure your faith rests on authentic revelation rather than human reasoning?
?
The argument that 'existence makes vision valid' appears straightforward, yet Sanusi shows its weakness. How does this challenge your confidence in your own intellectual abilities when it comes to matters of the divine? Will you remain content with superficial understanding, or will you strive to learn the deeper principles of theology?
?
In this lesson, Sanusi relies on textual evidence over rational proof. Are there areas in your life where you prioritize your own reasoning over the revealed text? What is one specific way you can align your thinking more closely with the Quran and Sunnah?

Refuting the Rational Proof for Ru'yah
Sanusi refutes the rational proof for ru'yah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational argument that existence enables vision is weak according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
Existence is identical to the existent, so it cannot be a cause.

Sanusi begins by addressing a popular rational proof used to support the possibility of seeing Allah. He quickly dismisses it by pointing out a logical flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the concise structure: statement, objection, conclusion.
2
Identify the proof
The proof claims that existence (al-wujud) is what makes an object visible. Mark how the author phrases it.
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi says existence is identical to the existent (ayn al-mawjud), so it cannot be a separate cause. Reflect on why this invalidates the proof.
4
Reflect on the implication
If this rational proof fails, what does that mean for arguments about ru'yah? This lesson establishes that rational proofs must be carefully examined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إثباتها بالدليل العقلي المشهورِ ؛ وهو أَنَّ مُصحِّحَ الرؤيةِ الوجود .. فضعيفٌ ; لأنَّ الوجود عين الموجودِ ، فلا يصلح علَّةً . ومعتمدُ مَنْ أحالَها مِنَ المبتدعة : أنَّها تستدعي الجهة والمقابلة ، وهو باطل ; لأنَّ ذلكَ مُفرَّع على انبعاث الأشعة ، فتتصل بالمرئي ، وذلك لو صح لوجب ألا يرى الإنسانُ إلا قدْرَ حدقتِهِ ، وهو باطل على الضرورة . قالوا : إنَّما ذلكَ لاتصال الشعاع بالهواء وهو مضيء ، فأعانَ على رؤية ما قابلَهُ كالبلور المُعِين بإشراقه على رؤية ما فيه . قلنا : فيلزمُ أَلا يَرى مِنَ الهواء إلا قدر حدقتِهِ ، وأيضاً : فنحن نرى والهواء مظلم ما نراه والهواء مشرقٌ . ومما ينقضُ عليهم : عدم رؤية الجوهر الفردِ معَ اتصال الشعاع بهِ ، ولا ينالُهُ مِنْ ذلكَ وحدَهُ إِلا ما ينالُهُ معَ غيرِهِ ، ورؤية الكبيرِ معَ البعدِ صغيراً معَ اتصال الشعاع والمقابلة لجميعه . قالوا : إنَّما ذلكَ لأنَّ الشعاعَ نفذَ مِنْ زاويةٍ حادةٍ لمثلث قاعدتُهُ المرئيُّ ، فقامَ خطّاً مستقيماً بوسط القاعدة على زوايا قائمة ، ومعلوم أنَّهُ أصغرُ ممَّا يقومُ عليها مِنْ سائر الخطوطِ فزيادة ذلك البعدِ لغيرِهِ منعَتْ مِنْ رؤية طرفي المرئي . قلنا : فيلزمُ إذا انتقل المرئيُّ إلى مقدارِ تلك الزيادةِ مِنَ البعد ألا يُرى ، والمشاهدة تكذبه . ومما ينقضُ عليهم : رؤية الأكوانِ معَ أَنَّ الأشمَّةَ لم تتصل بها .
As for establishing it by the well-known rational proof—that the enabler of vision is existence—it is weak, because existence is identical to the existent, so it is not suitable as a cause. And the reliance of those who deem it impossible from the innovators (mubtadi'a) is that it requires direction and facing, which is false, because that is derived from the emission of rays connecting with the visible object. If that were correct, man would necessarily see only the size of his pupil, which is false by necessity. They said: That is due to the connection of the ray to the air, which is luminous, so it helps in seeing what faces it, like a crystal that helps by its illumination to see what is in it. We said: Then it necessarily follows that he would not see from the air except the size of his pupil, and also: we see in darkness what we see in brightness. And what refutes them: not seeing the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) despite the connection of the ray to it, and it does not receive from that alone except what it receives with others, and the seeing of a large object as small at a distance despite the connection of the ray and the facing of all of it. They said: That is because the ray penetrates from an acute angle of a triangle whose base is the visible object, so it stands as a straight line in the middle of the base at right angles, and it is known that it is smaller than what stands on it from other lines, so the increase of that distance due to others prevents seeing the edges of the visible object. We said: Then it necessarily follows that if the visible object moves to the extent of that increase in distance, it would not be seen, but observation belies it. And what refutes them: the seeing of accidents (akwan) despite the fact that the rays have not connected to them.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a two-part refutation: first of the rational proof for the vision of Allah, and second of the innovators' (mubtadi'a) argument that vision requires direction and rays. The author begins by dismissing the well-known rational proof that 'existence' enables vision, because existence is identical to the existent and cannot be a separate cause. Then he addresses the innovators' claim that vision requires direction (jiha) and facing (muqabala). The author argues that this is based on the false assumption that vision occurs via rays emitted from the eye that connect with the object. If that were true, a person would only see the size of his pupil, which is absurd. The innovators respond that the ray connects through the luminous air, which helps, like a crystal aiding sight. The author counters that then one would not see more of the air than the pupil's size, and we see even in darkness. Further refutations: we do not see the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) even though the ray connects to it; a large object appears small at a distance despite full facing. The innovators explain that the ray enters at an acute angle, creating a triangle with the object as base, and the central straight line is shorter, so the edges are unseen at extreme distances. The author replies that if the object were moved to that extreme distance, it would become invisible, but observation shows otherwise. Finally, we see accidents (akwan) without any ray connecting to them, which disproves the ray theory.

Key Terms
الدليل العقلي
الوجود
الموجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that existence is a separate attribute that enables vision.
Correction: Existence is the very being of the existent, not a distinct cause.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the rational proof that existence enables vision?
A) Because existence is not real.
B) Because existence is identical to the existent, so it cannot be a cause.
C) Because vision does not require any cause.
D) Because the proof is not from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The rational proof that existence enables vision is invalid because existence is not separate from the existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What beliefs about Allah have you held based on your own reasoning rather than clear revelation? Are you willing to abandon them if they are shown to be flawed?
?
The rational proof you thought was solid for ru'yah was invalid. How many of your other 'certainties' about faith might be built on similar logical errors? Will you take time this week to examine them?
?
Is your faith rooted in arguments that could collapse, or in the Qur'an and Sunna that never fail? What would happen if every rational proof you relied on were refuted—would your conviction remain?

Refuting the Ray Theory of Vision
Sanusi refutes the mubtadi'a's ray theory of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's logical refutation of the claim that vision requires physical rays and direction.

Core Idea
The ray theory leads to absurdities and is contradicted by observation.

The innovators (mubtadi'a) argue that vision requires direction and facing, implying Allah cannot be seen. Sanusi dismantles their underlying physical theory by showing its internal inconsistencies and contradictions with observable experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the entire Arabic passage and translation. Notice it is a structured debate: claim, objection, response, counter-objection.
The word 'الأشمَّةَ' is likely a scribal error for 'الأشعة' (rays).
2
Understand the innovators' claim
They claim vision requires direction (jiha) and facing (muqabala) via rays emitted from the eye. Identify their initial argument.
3
Follow Sanusi's first objection
If vision were by rays connecting to the object, man would only see the size of his pupil—this is false by necessity.
4
Examine the counter-arguments
The innovators try to refine the theory: rays connect through luminous air, or form a triangle. Sanusi refutes each with further absurdities and observational evidence.
5
See the final refutation
Sanusi points out that we see accidents (akwan) without any ray connecting to them, which completely undermines the ray theory.

The Connection Condition for Vision Refuted
Sanusi refutes the condition of connection for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that vision requires connection to the object is flawed, and how Sanusi refutes it using counterexamples.

Core Idea
Vision does not require physical connection; abstract qualities like color are not always seen.

The opponents argue that the visible must be connected to the viewer or to something connected. Sanusi immediately points out the absurd consequence: tastes and smells, which subsist in bodies, would then be visible. When they restrict the claim to things that 'accept vision,' Sanusi gives concrete examples showing that we often see things without their color or without seeing all of what is around them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first response
Read the opening exchange where the opponents state their condition and Sanusi gives a counterexample involving tastes and smells.
Notice how Sanusi uses logical implication (lāzim) to show absurdity.
2
Examine the restriction
The opponents restrict their claim to 'what accepts vision.' Understanding this move is key to the dialectic.
3
Analyze the counterexamples
Sanusi provides three concrete examples: distant object seen without color, sun disc seen without birds below, fire seen without surroundings. Reflect on how each undermines the connection condition.
These examples are meant to be intuitively clear.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Consider why refuting the connection condition is important for establishing that God can be seen without physical contact.
This prepares for later lessons on vision of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : المرئي ما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ ، أو قام بما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ قلنا : فيلزمُ أنْ تُرى الطعوم والروائح ؛ لقيامها بما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ . قالوا : إنَّما ذلك فيما يقبلُ الرؤية . قلنا : ها هو البعيدُ يُرى دونَ لونِهِ ومما ينقض عليهم : رؤيةً قُرْص الشمس مع عدم رؤيةِ ما دونَها مِنَ الطير إذا علا في الجو ، ورؤية النار على البعدِ دون ما دونَها ، وأيضاً : الانبعاث إنَّما يكون عن اعتماد إلى جهة ، والسبر يبطله ثم لزوم المقابلة يَبطُلُ برؤيةِ الإنسانِ نفسَهُ في المرآةِ والماء . قالوا : لم تتشبَّثِ الأشعة فيهما لعدم التضريس ، فانعكست إلى الرائي . قلنا : فيلزم ألا يرى المرآة والماء لعدم قاعدة الأشعة قالوا : إنَّما رأى صورةً منطبعةً ، لا نفسَهُ قلنا : فيلزم ألا تبعد ببعدِهِ ، ولا تتحرك بحركتِهِ . وممَّا يلزم على اشتراط المقابلة : ألا يرى الرائي إلا قدْرَ ذاتِهِ ؛ إذ لا يقابل أكبر منها . قالوا : الشعاعُ أعان على ذلك قلنا : قد تقدَّمَ جوابه ولو سُلَّمَ ذلكَ كلُّهُ فرؤيةُ اللهِ تعالى لكلِّ موجود ولا بنية ولا شعاعَ وليس في جهةٍ ولا مقابلة .. يهدمُ ما أصلُوهُ وأيضاً : فما ثبتَ مِنْ رؤية النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ الجنةَ مِنْ موضعِهِ معَ غاية البعدِ وكثافةِ الحُجُبِ الكثيرة .. يمنع ما تخيَّلوهُ مِنَ الأَشعَةِ والموانعِ وإذا تقرر هذا فالبصرُ عند أهل الحقِّ : عبارة عن معنى يقومُ بمحل ما يتعلق بالمرتبات ، ويتعدَّدُ في حقنا بتعددِها ، وما لم يُرَ في الموجوداتِ فلموانع قامَتْ بالمحل على حَسَبها ، وهل قام في العمى مانع واحدٌ يُضادُّ جميعَ الإدراكاتِ ، أو موانع تعدَّدَتْ بعددِ ما فاتَتْ رؤيتُهُ مِنَ الموجوداتِ ؟ فيهِ تردَّد
They said: The visible is that which is connected to it [the viewer], or which subsists in what is connected to it. We said: Then it would be necessary that tastes and smells be seen, because they subsist in what is connected to it. They said: That is only regarding what accepts vision. We said: Here is the distant being seen without its color. And among things that refute them: seeing the sun's disc without seeing what is below it of birds when it rises in the sky, and seeing fire at a distance without what is around it. Also: emission [of rays] only occurs by inclination to a direction, and empirical testing refutes it. Then the necessity of facing [muqābala] is invalidated by a person seeing himself in a mirror and in water. They said: The rays did not attach to them because of lack of roughness [taḍrīs], so they reflected to the viewer. We said: Then it would be necessary that the mirror and water not be seen because of the lack of a base for the rays. They said: He only saw an impressed image, not himself. We said: Then it would be necessary that it not become distant with his distance, nor move with his movement. And among things necessary on the condition of facing: that the viewer should only see the measure of his own self, because he does not face anything larger than it. They said: The ray helped with that. We said: Its answer has already preceded. And even if all that is granted, the vision of God—may He be exalted—of every existent without structure, without ray, while He is not in a direction nor in facing, demolishes what they have established. Also: what is established of the Prophet's, may God bless him and grant him peace, seeing Paradise from his place despite the extreme distance and the density of many veils, prevents what they imagined of rays and obstacles. And when this is established, sight [al-baṣar] according to the People of Truth is an expression of a meaning that subsists in a locus which relates to [different] ranks, and it is multiple in our case by their multiplicity, and whatever among existents is not seen is because of obstacles that subsist in the locus according to them. And whether in blindness there is a single obstacle that opposes all perceptions, or multiple obstacles according to the number of existents whose vision is missed, there is hesitation about that.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a sustained dialectical refutation of objections raised against the possibility of seeing God. The author (Sanusi) presents a series of objections from opponents and answers each one in turn. The first objection claims that the visible must be connected to the viewer or to something connected to the viewer. Sanusi responds that if that were true, tastes and smells would have to be seen because they subsist in connected bodies. The opponents then restrict the claim to what 'accepts vision,' but Sanusi counters by pointing out that distant objects are seen without their color (a necessary accident). He then gives counterexamples: we see the sun's disc but not birds below it in the sky, and we see fire at a distance but not what is around it. He also refutes the notion that vision requires emission of rays from the eye (intromission theory) by noting that emission requires direction and that empirical testing disproves it. The condition of facing (muqābala) is refuted by the example of seeing oneself in a mirror or water—the viewer faces the mirror, not himself. The opponents attempt to salvage by saying rays reflect due to smoothness, but Sanusi responds that then the mirror and water themselves would not be seen because rays have no base to attach. They then claim that what is seen is an impressed image, not the self; Sanusi refutes this by noting that the image moves with the viewer, so it must be the self. Another objection: if facing is required, a person could only see something equal to his own size. The opponents say the ray helps; Sanusi says this answer was already given earlier. He then delivers a decisive argument: even if all these objections were granted, the vision of God—who has no body, ray, direction, or facing—demolishes their entire foundation. Furthermore, the Prophet's vision of Paradise from his place despite extreme distance and many veils proves that the opponents' imagined obstacles are invalid. The passage concludes with a definition of sight according to Ahl al-Haqq: it is a meaning that subsists in a locus and relates to different ranks; it is multiple in us according to the multiplicity of visible things; and when something is not seen, it is due to obstacles. Finally, Sanusi raises a subtle question about whether blindness is due to a single obstacle or multiple obstacles, leaving it as a point of hesitation (taraddud).

Key Terms
مرئي (mar'ī)
اتصال (ittiṣāl)
رؤية (ru'ya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision requires physical contact or direct continuity with the object.
Correction: Sanusi shows that we see distant objects without connection, and even when connected, we do not see all subsisting qualities (like taste).

Checkpoint
What did the opponents claim about the visible?
A) It must be connected to the viewer or to something connected to the viewer.
B) It must be illuminated.
C) It must be directly in front.
D) It must be larger than the pupil.

Key Takeaway
The connection condition is refuted by the absurdity that tastes and smells would be seen, and by everyday counterexamples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often rely on your senses to form beliefs. This lesson shows that what you see is not always complete—you may see an object without its color or its surroundings. When you look at the world around you, do you assume you have full knowledge of what you perceive? How might this remind you that your perception is limited and that you need revelation to know what is beyond your senses?
?
Sanusi refutes the idea that physical connection is necessary for vision. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes feel that you need a tangible 'connection'—a feeling, a sign, or an experience—to be certain of His existence? How does this lesson challenge that assumption? What does it mean for you to 'see' Allah through the evidence of creation without demanding a physical connection?
?
The opponents in this lesson restrict their claim to 'what accepts vision.' Are you guilty of restricting God's attributes to what fits your limited understanding? This lesson exposes the danger of imposing human conditions on divine matters. Reflect on a time when you placed a condition on Allah—perhaps thinking He must act in a certain way. How can you let go of those conditions and submit to His absolute power?

Refuting Emission and Facing Conditions
Sanusi refutes emission and facing conditions with dialectical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi refutes the conditions of ray emission and facing (muqābala) for vision, using examples like seeing oneself in a mirror.

Core Idea
Vision does not require rays from the eye or direct facing; mirror reflection disproves both.

After refuting the connection condition, Sanusi turns to two other common theories: that vision occurs by emission of rays from the eye, and that the viewer must directly face the object. He argues that emission requires direction and is empirically false, and facing is disproved by seeing oneself in a mirror. The opponents try to salvage their theories with further objections, but Sanusi counters each one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the emission theory refutation
Sanusi argues that emission of rays requires a direction and is refuted by empirical testing. Notice how he dismisses it briefly.
The 'sabr' (empirical testing) is a reference to observed evidence.
2
Analyze the facing condition and mirror counterexample
The condition of facing (muqābala) is disproved by seeing oneself in a mirror—one does not face oneself directly.
This is a powerful example because it is universally experienced.
3
Follow the dialectic on ray reflection
The opponents claim rays reflect off smooth surfaces; Sanusi counters that then the mirror itself would not be seen.
Notice the logical give-and-take.
4
Examine the image objection and response
The opponents say only an image is seen; Sanusi shows that the image moves with the viewer, so it must be the self.
This refutes the distinction between self and image.
5
Consider the size objection and final answer
If facing is required, one could only see something equal to one's own size. The opponents appeal to the ray; Sanusi says the answer was already given.
This shows the opponents' reliance on previously refuted assumptions.

قالوا : المرئي ما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ ، أو قام بما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ قلنا : فيلزمُ أنْ تُرى الطعوم والروائح ؛ لقيامها بما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ . قالوا : إنَّما ذلك فيما يقبلُ الرؤية . قلنا : ها هو البعيدُ يُرى دونَ لونِهِ ومما ينقض عليهم : رؤيةً قُرْص الشمس مع عدم رؤيةِ ما دونَها مِنَ الطير إذا علا في الجو ، ورؤية النار على البعدِ دون ما دونَها ، وأيضاً : الانبعاث إنَّما يكون عن اعتماد إلى جهة ، والسبر يبطله ثم لزوم المقابلة يَبطُلُ برؤيةِ الإنسانِ نفسَهُ في المرآةِ والماء . قالوا : لم تتشبَّثِ الأشعة فيهما لعدم التضريس ، فانعكست إلى الرائي . قلنا : فيلزم ألا يرى المرآة والماء لعدم قاعدة الأشعة قالوا : إنَّما رأى صورةً منطبعةً ، لا نفسَهُ قلنا : فيلزم ألا تبعد ببعدِهِ ، ولا تتحرك بحركتِهِ . وممَّا يلزم على اشتراط المقابلة : ألا يرى الرائي إلا قدْرَ ذاتِهِ ؛ إذ لا يقابل أكبر منها . قالوا : الشعاعُ أعان على ذلك قلنا : قد تقدَّمَ جوابه ولو سُلَّمَ ذلكَ كلُّهُ فرؤيةُ اللهِ تعالى لكلِّ موجود ولا بنية ولا شعاعَ وليس في جهةٍ ولا مقابلة .. يهدمُ ما أصلُوهُ وأيضاً : فما ثبتَ مِنْ رؤية النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ الجنةَ مِنْ موضعِهِ معَ غاية البعدِ وكثافةِ الحُجُبِ الكثيرة .. يمنع ما تخيَّلوهُ مِنَ الأَشعَةِ والموانعِ وإذا تقرر هذا فالبصرُ عند أهل الحقِّ : عبارة عن معنى يقومُ بمحل ما يتعلق بالمرتبات ، ويتعدَّدُ في حقنا بتعددِها ، وما لم يُرَ في الموجوداتِ فلموانع قامَتْ بالمحل على حَسَبها ، وهل قام في العمى مانع واحدٌ يُضادُّ جميعَ الإدراكاتِ ، أو موانع تعدَّدَتْ بعددِ ما فاتَتْ رؤيتُهُ مِنَ الموجوداتِ ؟ فيهِ تردَّد
They said: The visible is that which is connected to it [the viewer], or which subsists in what is connected to it. We said: Then it would be necessary that tastes and smells be seen, because they subsist in what is connected to it. They said: That is only regarding what accepts vision. We said: Here is the distant being seen without its color. And among things that refute them: seeing the sun's disc without seeing what is below it of birds when it rises in the sky, and seeing fire at a distance without what is around it. Also: emission [of rays] only occurs by inclination to a direction, and empirical testing refutes it. Then the necessity of facing [muqābala] is invalidated by a person seeing himself in a mirror and in water. They said: The rays did not attach to them because of lack of roughness [taḍrīs], so they reflected to the viewer. We said: Then it would be necessary that the mirror and water not be seen because of the lack of a base for the rays. They said: He only saw an impressed image, not himself. We said: Then it would be necessary that it not become distant with his distance, nor move with his movement. And among things necessary on the condition of facing: that the viewer should only see the measure of his own self, because he does not face anything larger than it. They said: The ray helped with that. We said: Its answer has already preceded. And even if all that is granted, the vision of God—may He be exalted—of every existent without structure, without ray, while He is not in a direction nor in facing, demolishes what they have established. Also: what is established of the Prophet's, may God bless him and grant him peace, seeing Paradise from his place despite the extreme distance and the density of many veils, prevents what they imagined of rays and obstacles. And when this is established, sight [al-baṣar] according to the People of Truth is an expression of a meaning that subsists in a locus which relates to [different] ranks, and it is multiple in our case by their multiplicity, and whatever among existents is not seen is because of obstacles that subsist in the locus according to them. And whether in blindness there is a single obstacle that opposes all perceptions, or multiple obstacles according to the number of existents whose vision is missed, there is hesitation about that.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a sustained dialectical refutation of objections raised against the possibility of seeing God. The author (Sanusi) presents a series of objections from opponents and answers each one in turn. The first objection claims that the visible must be connected to the viewer or to something connected to the viewer. Sanusi responds that if that were true, tastes and smells would have to be seen because they subsist in connected bodies. The opponents then restrict the claim to what 'accepts vision,' but Sanusi counters by pointing out that distant objects are seen without their color (a necessary accident). He then gives counterexamples: we see the sun's disc but not birds below it in the sky, and we see fire at a distance but not what is around it. He also refutes the notion that vision requires emission of rays from the eye (intromission theory) by noting that emission requires direction and that empirical testing disproves it. The condition of facing (muqābala) is refuted by the example of seeing oneself in a mirror or water—the viewer faces the mirror, not himself. The opponents attempt to salvage by saying rays reflect due to smoothness, but Sanusi responds that then the mirror and water themselves would not be seen because rays have no base to attach. They then claim that what is seen is an impressed image, not the self; Sanusi refutes this by noting that the image moves with the viewer, so it must be the self. Another objection: if facing is required, a person could only see something equal to his own size. The opponents say the ray helps; Sanusi says this answer was already given earlier. He then delivers a decisive argument: even if all these objections were granted, the vision of God—who has no body, ray, direction, or facing—demolishes their entire foundation. Furthermore, the Prophet's vision of Paradise from his place despite extreme distance and many veils proves that the opponents' imagined obstacles are invalid. The passage concludes with a definition of sight according to Ahl al-Haqq: it is a meaning that subsists in a locus and relates to different ranks; it is multiple in us according to the multiplicity of visible things; and when something is not seen, it is due to obstacles. Finally, Sanusi raises a subtle question about whether blindness is due to a single obstacle or multiple obstacles, leaving it as a point of hesitation (taraddud).

مقابلة (muqābala)
شعاع (shu'ā')
تضريس (taḍrīs)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision requires emission of rays from the eye or direct physical facing.
Correction: Sanusi uses the mirror example to show that neither condition is necessary, and empirical testing disproves emission.

Checkpoint
What example does Sanusi use to refute the condition of facing?
A) Seeing the sun's disc without birds.
B) Seeing fire at a distance.
C) A person seeing himself in a mirror.
D) The Prophet seeing Paradise.

Key Takeaway
Both emission theory and the condition of facing are refuted by empirical examples and logical consistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi uses empirical testing to refute a long-held theory. Which of your own deeply held assumptions about faith or reality have you never really tested? Are you willing to let them go if they don't hold up?
?
When you see yourself in a mirror, you immediately know it's you. Now reflect: how do you know that you are truly seeing God's attributes? Could your own conditions be blocking you?
?
The opponents kept offering counterarguments, but Sanusi's logic exposed their inconsistencies. In your own life, do you defend your beliefs with the same rigor, or do you settle for answers that feel comfortable? Ask God for the courage to pursue truth even when it unsettles you.

The Decisive Argument and the Definition of Sight
Sanusi presents decisive arguments and defines sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the nature of God's vision and the Prophet's vision to demolish the opponents' conditions, and how he defines sight according to Ahl al-Haqq.

Core Idea
God's vision of all things without structure or direction proves that vision does not require physical conditions.

After refuting specific objections, Sanusi delivers a decisive argument: even if all those conditions were granted for physical vision, God's vision of every existent—without body, ray, direction, or facing—utterly demolishes the opponents' system. He also cites the Prophet's vision of Paradise from afar, proving that distance and obstacles do not prevent vision. Finally, he defines sight as a meaning that relates to ranks and explains that unseen things are due to obstacles.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the decisive argument from God's vision
Sanusi argues that even if all previous conditions were valid for physical objects, God's vision of everything—without structure, ray, direction, or facing—completely undermines the opponents' theory.
This is a powerful theological refutation.
2
Consider the Prophet's vision of Paradise
The Prophet's ability to see Paradise from his place despite extreme distance and many veils shows that the opponents' imagined obstacles are invalid.
This is a revealed proof from the mi'raj tradition.
3
Understand the definition of sight
Sanusi defines sight as a meaning that subsists in a locus and relates to different ranks; it is multiple in us corresponding to visible things.
This is the positive doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq.
4
Reflect on the question about blindness
Sanusi raises a subtle question: is blindness due to a single obstacle or multiple? He leaves it as a matter of hesitation.
This shows the depth of kalam investigation.

Refuting Objections to Seeing God
Sanusi refutes objections about divine vision using logical and prophetic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes objections against the vision of Allah by denying physical conditions and citing prophetic evidence.

Core Idea
God's vision is not physical; it transcends direction and obstacles.

Sanusi addresses common objections that vision requires physical conditions like rays or direction. He argues that Allah's vision does not depend on such conditions, and the Prophet's experience of seeing Paradise despite obstacles demonstrates this.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp Sanusi's refutation.
Notice the logical structure: first a conditional argument, then a prophetic example.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find the logical argument (even if premises granted, divine vision needs no physical conditions) and the prophetic evidence (Prophet saw Paradise despite distance and veils).
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why Sanusi uses these two arguments together and how they undermine the opponents' claims about rays and obstacles.
This connects to the broader doctrine that Allah's attributes are not like creation's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : قد تقدَّمَ جوابه ولو سُلَّمَ ذلكَ كلُّهُ فرؤيةُ اللهِ تعالى لكلِّ موجود ولا بنية ولا شعاعَ وليس في جهةٍ ولا مقابلة .. يهدمُ ما أصلُوهُ وأيضاً : فما ثبتَ مِنْ رؤية النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ الجنةَ مِنْ موضعِهِ معَ غاية البعدِ وكثافةِ الحُجُبِ الكثيرة .. يمنع ما تخيَّلوهُ مِنَ الأَشعَةِ والموانعِ وإذا تقرر هذا فالبصرُ عند أهل الحقِّ : عبارة عن معنى يقومُ بمحل ما يتعلق بالمرتبات ، ويتعدَّدُ في حقنا بتعددِها ، وما لم يُرَ في الموجوداتِ فلموانع قامَتْ بالمحل على حَسَبها ، وهل قام في العمى مانع واحدٌ يُضادُّ جميعَ الإدراكاتِ ، أو موانع تعدَّدَتْ بعددِ ما فاتَتْ رؤيتُهُ مِنَ الموجوداتِ ؟ فيهِ تردَّد
We said: its answer has already preceded. And if all that is granted, then the vision of God, exalted, of every existent without structure, ray, direction, or facing... destroys what they have established. Also, what has been established of the Prophet's (peace be upon him) seeing Paradise from his place despite extreme distance and many dense veils prevents what they imagined of rays and obstacles. And when this is established, vision (al-basar) according to the people of truth is: an expression for a meaning subsisting in a locus that relates to ranks, and it is multiple in our case by their multiplicity. And what has not been seen among existents is due to obstacles that arose in the locus according to them. And whether in blindness there is a single obstacle that opposes all perceptions, or obstacles multiplied by the number of missed existents, there is hesitation concerning it.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of objections against the vision of God (ru'yat Allah). It begins by reminding that the answer has already been given, then argues that even if the opponent's premises are accepted, the vision of God does not require any physical conditions like rays or direction. It provides an example: the Prophet saw Paradise from his place despite extreme distance and obstacles, disproving the claimed conditions. Then it defines 'basar' (vision) according to Ahl al-Haqq as a meaning (ma'na) that perceives ranks, explaining multiplicity and obstacles. Finally, it notes hesitation about whether blindness is caused by a single obstacle or multiple obstacles.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision of Allah requires a physical eye and direction.
Correction: Allah's vision is not physical; it does not depend on rays, direction, or any material condition.

Checkpoint

A) That vision requires rays
B) That obstacles always prevent vision
C) That vision can occur without physical conditions
D) That Paradise is near to the earth

?
Are you comfortable admitting that your intellect cannot fully grasp how Allah sees, or do you secretly rely on analogies from creation? What step will you take today to submit to Allah's transcendence?

Defining Vision According to Ahl al-Haqq
Sanusi defines basar as a meaning subsisting in a locus that relates to ranks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of basar as a meaning subsisting in a locus that perceives ranks, and the role of obstacles.

Core Idea
Basar is a non-physical meaning that perceives according to ranks.

After refuting objections, Sanusi defines what vision (basar) actually is according to the people of truth. He explains that it is a meaning that subsists in a locus and relates to ranks, and that unseen things are due to obstacles. He then raises a hesitation about whether blindness is caused by a single or multiple obstacles.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of basar: 'عبارة عن معنى يقوم بمحل ما يتعلق بالمرتبات'. Understand each term.
Note that 'ma'na' is a technical term for a non-physical attribute.
2
Understand multiplicity and obstacles
See how basar is multiple in us according to ranks, and how absence of vision is due to obstacles in the locus.
3
Consider the hesitation
Reflect on the open question about blindness: is there one universal obstacle or multiple specific ones?
This shows the limits of human knowledge in divine matters.
