The Impossibility of Obligation and Injustice in God's Actions
Sanusi proves impossibility of obligation, injustice, and purpose in God's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why obligation and injustice are impossible for God, and why His actions cannot have a purpose.

Core Idea
God's actions are beyond obligation, injustice, and purpose – He is absolutely sovereign.

Sanusi presents a concise proof that two concepts are impossible for God: being obligated (wujub) and committing injustice (zulm). He then shows that this entails that God's actions cannot be motivated by a purpose (gharad). This lesson walks through the reasoning, including a Quranic verse that underscores God's absolute freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Notice the strong logical structure: statement, reason, conclusion.
2
Understand why obligation and injustice are impossible
Focus on Sanusi's reasons: obligation would require the possibility of disobedience, and injustice would require acting against what is proper. Both are incompatible with God's nature.
This is a key distinction from human moral frameworks.
3
Trace the argument to purpose and the Quranic support
See how the impossibility of obligation and injustice leads to the conclusion that God's actions cannot have a purpose. The Quranic verse (28:68) seals the proof by affirming God's absolute freedom of choice.
Reflect on why a purpose would imply obligation or coercion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالوجوب إذا والظلم عليه محالان؛ إذ الوجوب يستدعي تعاصي بعض الممكنات، والظلم يستدعي التصرف على خلاف ما ينبغي. ومِن هنا استحال أن يكون فعله تعالى لغرض؛ لأنه لو كان له غرض في الفعل لأوجبه عليه، وإلا لم يكن علة له، فيكون مقهوراً، كيف وربك يخلق ما يشاء ويختار [القصص:٦٨]؟!
Obligation and injustice are therefore impossible for Him; because obligation would require that some contingent things disobey, and injustice would require acting contrary to what is proper. Hence it is impossible for His action to be for a purpose; because if He had a purpose in the act, it would obligate Him, otherwise it would not be a cause for Him, so He would be overpowered. How, and your Lord creates what He wills and chooses (28:68)?!
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a proof. It starts by stating that both obligation (wujub) and injustice (zulm) are impossible for God. The reason: obligation would require that some contingent things disobey His will (since obligation implies a command that might be disobeyed). Injustice would require acting against what is proper. Then it concludes that consequently, God's action cannot be for a purpose (gharad). Because if there were a purpose, it would obligate Him to act, or else the purpose would have no causal role, making Him subject to coercion. The Quranic verse 'Your Lord creates whatsoever He wills and chooses' is cited as support, emphasizing God's absolute freedom.

Key Terms
وجوب
ظلم
غرض
مقهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's actions have a purpose like human actions, which implies God is subject to obligation.
Correction: Sanusi shows that a purpose would obligate God or make Him subject to coercion, contradicting His sovereignty.

Checkpoint
Why is obligation impossible for God according to the passage?
A) Because God is not required to do anything
B) Because obligation would imply that some contingents might disobey His will
C) Because God is unjust
D) Because actions cannot have a purpose

Key Takeaway
God is free from obligation, injustice, and the need for a purpose in His actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you secretly expect God to 'owe' you something in return? How does Sanusi's proof that God is free from obligation challenge that expectation? Will you repent from that mindset today?
?
Think of a recent hardship you faced. Did you, even for a moment, feel that God was being unfair? How can the impossibility of injustice in God's actions transform the way you interpret your trials? What specific step will you take to trust His justice more deeply?
?
Have you ever insisted in your prayer that God answer a certain way because you thought it was 'the right thing'? Does this lesson reveal a hidden assumption that God is bound by your sense of purpose? What does it mean for you to surrender your own purposes to His absolute will?

Dichotomy of Purpose: Eternal or Originated
Sanusi gives a second proof against purpose using a dichotomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's second proof that God's actions are not for a purpose, using the dichotomy of eternal versus originated purpose and the problem of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Purpose leads to contradiction: either an eternal act or an impossible infinite regress.

Sanusi presents a second, concise argument against attributing purposes to God's actions. He sets up a clear either-or scenario: the purpose is either eternal or originated. Both options lead to unacceptable conclusions. This logical proof reinforces that God's actions are free from external purposes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dichotomy
The passage sets up two possibilities for the purpose of God's action: eternal or originated.
This is a standard logical technique in kalam.
2
Examine the first branch
If the purpose is eternal, then the act would be eternal, contradicting its temporal origination.
The act's origination is already proven elsewhere.
3
Examine the second branch
If the purpose is originated, it would require another purpose, leading to an infinite regress.
Infinite regress of originated things is impossible.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since both branches lead to impossibility, the assumption that there is a purpose is false.
Thus, God's actions are free from purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً: فالغرض إما قديم فيلزم قدم الفعل، وقد مر برهان حدوثه، أو حادث فيفتقر إلى غرض، ثم كذلك ويتسلسل، فيؤدي إلى حوادث لا أول لها، وقد مر بطلانه
Also: the purpose is either eternal, then the act would be eternal, and the proof of its temporal origination has already passed; or originated, then it would need a purpose, then likewise ad infinitum, leading to originated things with no first, whose invalidity has already passed.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This is a concise proof. It presents a dichotomy: the supposed purpose (gharad) of God's action is either eternal (qadim) or originated (hadith). If eternal, then the act itself would be eternal, but it has been proven that the act is originated (hadith). If originated, then that purpose itself would need a purpose, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) of originated purposes, which is invalid because it would imply an endless chain of originated events with no beginning, which has been proven impossible. Therefore, the assumption that God's actions have a purpose is false.

Key Terms
غرض
قديم
حادث
تسلسل
برهان حدوثه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The purpose of God's actions could be an endless chain of purposes.
Correction: An infinite regress of originated purposes is impossible because it would entail an infinite series of contingent events with no first cause, which is rationally invalid.

Checkpoint
What does the proof conclude if the purpose is eternal?
A) The act would also be eternal
B) The act would be originated
C) The purpose would be unnecessary
D) God would be weak

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's proof shows that attributing a purpose to God's actions leads to contradictions, thereby affirming that God acts without external purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that God created you for a specific purpose, how do you reconcile that with Sanusi's argument that God's actions cannot have external purposes? Does your belief require God to have a need or lack? How might you reframe your understanding to avoid attributing a motive to God?
?
When life is hard, do you catch yourself thinking, 'There must be a reason for this suffering'? How does this lesson's claim that God acts without purpose challenge that thought? Can you still trust God without expecting a hidden purpose behind every event? What would it mean to worship a God who acts purely from His nature, not to achieve any goal?
?
This proof forces us to stop projecting human-like motives onto God. In what areas of your life do you still treat God as if He acts to fulfill your expectations or desires? Will you commit to surrendering that narrative and accepting that His actions are beyond your framework of purpose?

The Third Proof: Purpose Cannot Benefit God or His Action
Sanusi presents a third proof that a purpose would involve a benefit either for God or His action, both impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why any purpose (gharad) in God's action is impossible, whether the benefit returns to God or to His action.

Core Idea
Any purpose in God's action either compromises His transcendence or leads to logical absurdity.

Sanusi now presents a third proof against attributing purpose to God's actions. He argues that any supposed purpose would either be a benefit returning to God Himself—impossible because it would entail originated accidents in His essence—or a benefit returning to His action, which leads to three separate impossibilities. This concise argument strengthens the Ash'ari position that God's actions are not for purposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice how Sanusi presents a dichotomy: the purpose is either a benefit for God or for His action.
The word 'مصلحة' (benefit) is key.
2
Identify the first impossibility
Why is a benefit returning to God impossible? The text says it would entail 'attribution of originated accidents to His lofty essence'. Understand what that means for divine transcendence.
Originated accidents (hawadith) cannot be in God's essence.
3
Analyze the second impossibility
A benefit returning to God's action is impossible for three reasons. List them: (1) God is not obliged to consider what is beneficial, (2) He can deliver benefit directly, (3) it leads to circularity or infinite regress.
The third reason is a technical argument: if the benefit itself needs a purpose, you get ta'lil al-shay' bi-nafsihi or tasalsul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً: فالغرض إما مصلحة تعود إليه، أو إلى فعله. والأول محال؛ لاستلزام اتصاف ذاته العلية بالحوادث. والثاني محال؛ لعدم وجوب مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح، ولأنه قادر على إيصال تلك المصلحة إلى العبد مثلاً بغير واسطة، ولأنه يلزم فيه تعليل الشيء بنفسه، أو التسلسل لنقل الكلام إلى تلك المصلحة نفسها
Also: the purpose is either a benefit returning to Him, or to His action. The first is impossible because it entails the attribution of originated accidents to His lofty essence. The second is impossible because it is not obligatory to consider what is beneficial and most beneficial, and because He is able to deliver that benefit to the servant, for example, without an intermediary, and because it would entail either explaining a thing by itself or an infinite regress when the discussion is transferred to that same benefit.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage provides a third proof. It divides the possible purpose (gharad) into two: either a benefit (maslaha) that returns to God Himself, or a benefit that returns to His action. The first is impossible because it would require that God's essence be affected by originated events (hawadith). The second is impossible for three reasons: 1) It is not obligatory for God to consider what is beneficial or most beneficial for creatures; 2) God is able to deliver that benefit directly without needing an intermediary act; 3) It leads to either circular reasoning (ta'lil al-shay' bi-nafsihi) or infinite regress, because the benefit itself would then need to be explained.

Key Terms
مصلحة
اتصاف
حوادث
تعليل الشيء بنفسه
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions have wise purposes that benefit His creation.
Correction: This proof shows that any such purpose either affects God's essence (impossible) or leads to logical absurdities (circularity or regress).

Checkpoint
Why is a benefit returning to God impossible?
A) Because God does not need benefit
B) Because it would mean God's essence is affected by originated events
C) Because God cannot receive benefit
D) Because benefits only apply to creatures

Key Takeaway
Attributing any purpose to God's action is rationally impossible because it either compromises His transcendence or leads to logical inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God does not act for any purpose or benefit, what does that mean for your own acts of worship? Do you perform them with the expectation that God needs them, or do you truly believe He is self-sufficient and that your worship is solely for your own good?
?
The proof shows that attributing purpose to God's actions leads to logical absurdities. How does this challenge any hidden belief you might hold that your prayers or good deeds somehow 'earn' you God's favor in a way that adds to His perfection?
?
This lesson asserts that God can deliver benefit to you without any intermediary. In what areas of your life are you still relying on means and intermediaries as if God is not capable of direct action? Are you willing to trust His direct provision and stop seeking secondary causes as if they are necessary?

Why God's Actions Are Not Foolish
Sanusi refutes the objection by defining foolishness and showing its inapplicability to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's actions are not considered foolish despite lacking a purpose, based on the definition of foolishness.

Core Idea
Foolishness requires ignorance or craving, which God transcends completely.

The objection that God's actions would be foolish without purpose stems from a human analogy. Sanusi answers by defining foolishness precisely—it involves ignorance of consequences or seeking immediate pleasure. God, being omniscient and transcendent, cannot be described by such terms. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice how the objection is stated and how the answer begins with a definition.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the rhetorical question at the end.
2
Examine the definition of foolishness
Identify the key components of the definition: ignorance of consequences and preference of immediate pleasure. Why do these make an action foolish?
The definition is conventional (عرفاً), not revealed.
3
Apply the definition to God
See how the definition does not apply to God: He is free from ignorance and base desires. The rhetorical question emphasizes the contrast.
Consider the terms 'متعالي عن تجدد كمال أو نقصان'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: إذا لم يكن غرض فالفعل سفه. قلنا: السفه عرفاً: ما فعل مع الجهل بالعواقب، أو ترجيح اللذة الحاضرة، حتى يفعل السفيه ما فيه ضرره أو حتفه وهو لا يشعر، وأين هذا من فعل المتعالي عن تجدد كمال أو نقصان، الذي لا يعزب عن علمه شيء على الإطلاق في سر وإعلان؟!
They said: If there is no purpose, then the action is foolish. We say: Foolishness conventionally is that which is done with ignorance of consequences, or preferring immediate pleasure, so that the fool does what contains his harm or death without feeling. And where is this compared to the act of the Exalted, who is above acquiring perfection or deficiency, from whose knowledge nothing at all is hidden in secret or in public?!
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that an action without a purpose (gharad) is foolish (safah). The answer defines foolishness as acting out of ignorance of consequences or excessive desire for immediate pleasure, which leads to self-harm. God is far above this: He is transcendent above acquiring any perfection or deficiency, and His knowledge encompasses all things. So His actions cannot be described as foolish. The rhetorical question 'Where is this compared to...?' emphasizes the incomparability.

Key Terms
سفه
جهل
عواقب
لذة
متعالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that purpose is necessary for rational action; but the definition of foolishness does not apply to God because He is omniscient and immutable.
Correction: Foolishness is defined by ignorance or base desire, which are absent in God. Therefore His actions, even without purpose, are not foolish.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the conventional definition of foolishness?
A) Acting without thinking
B) Acting with ignorance of consequences or preference of immediate pleasure
C) Acting without purpose
D) Acting with reason

Key Takeaway
God's actions are never foolish because He has perfect knowledge and no base desires.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you acted out of ignorance of the consequences or to satisfy an immediate pleasure, and what was the result? How does this lesson's definition of foolishness challenge you to be more mindful of your actions?
?
If God's actions are never foolish because He has perfect knowledge and no base desires, then your actions—often driven by ignorance and cravings—reveal something about your own character. Are you willing to confront how often your choices are guided by short-term gratification rather than wisdom? What will you change today?
?
The objector assumed that action without purpose is foolish, but Sanusi shows that foolishness is about ignorance and lust. How often do you judge others' actions as foolish without considering their knowledge and motives? How can you adopt a more God-like perspective of understanding before labeling?

The Impossibility of Purpose in Divine Actions
Sanusi presents a proof against purpose in divine actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's actions cannot have a purpose and why assigning a purpose leads to impossible consequences such as infinite regress or attributing change to Allah.

Core Idea
God's actions need no purpose; any supposed purpose leads to absurdities.

Sanusi begins by refuting the claim that divine actions require a purpose. He presents a proof that if God's actions had a purpose, it would either benefit God (impossible, as it would imply change in His essence) or benefit the action itself (also impossible, as it would lead to circular reasoning or infinite regress). This argument establishes a key Ash'ari tenet: Allah is absolutely free from need and His acts are not motivated by external purposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof of infinite regress
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi argues that if an action is temporally originated and requires a purpose, then that purpose would also require another purpose, leading to an infinite chain of purposes—which is impossible.
This argument is a standard Ash'ari refutation of the idea that God acts for reasons.
2
Examine the two possibilities for purpose
Sanusi then analyzes the two possibilities for what the purpose could be: a benefit returning to God, or a benefit returning to His action. Identify why each is impossible.
3
Understand why both are impossible
The first possibility would necessitate change (hawadith) in Allah's essence, which is impossible. The second would either render God obligated to do what is best (which is not obligatory), or imply that God cannot directly benefit the servant without an action, or lead to circular reasoning or infinite regress.
This demonstrates that any attempt to assign a purpose to divine actions fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان حدوثِهِ ، أو حادث فيفتقر إلى غرض ، ثم كذلكَ ويتسلسل ، فيؤدّي إلى حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها ، وقد مرَّ بطلانه
وأيضاً : فالغرضُ إِمَّا مصلحة تعود إليه ، أو إلى فعله
والأوَّلُ محال ؛ لاستلزامِ اتِّصافِ ذاتِهِ العليَّةِ بالحوادثِ والثاني محال ؛ لعدم وجوبِ مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح ولأنَّه قادر على إيصال تلكَ المصلحة إلى العبدِ مثلاً بغير واسطة ، ولأنَّه يلزمُ فيهِ تعليلُ الشيء بنفسِهِ ، أو التسلسل
لنقل الكلامِ إلى تلك المصلحة نفسها
قالوا : إذا لم يكن غرض فالفعلُ سفه
قلنا : السفهُ عرفاً : ما فُعِلَ معَ الجهل بالعواقبِ ، أو ترجيح اللذة الحاضرة ، حتى يفعل السفيه ما فيه ضرره أو حتفه وهو لا يشعرُ ، وأينَ هذا مِنْ فعل المتعالي عن تجدُّدِ كمال أو نقصان ، الذي لا يعزب عن علمه شيء على الإطلاق في سر وإعلان ؟!
وإذا عرفت بما ذُكِرَ عدم رجحان بعض الأفعال على بعض بالنسبة إليهِ تعالى .. عرفت جهالة مَنْ تسَوَّرَ على الغيب ، ورأى أنَّ العقل يتوصل دونَ شرع إلى إدراكِ الحسَنِ والقبيح عندَهُ جلَّ وعلا ، على أنَّهُ لو سُلَّمَ لهم ذلكَ جدلاً لم يجزم العقل بشيء ؛ لتعارض أوجُهِ مِنَ النظرِ في ذلك متضادة
فإذاً ؛ لم نعرف وجوبَ الإيمان ولا تحريم الكفران إلا بعد مجيء الشرع
A proof of its temporal origination: or it is originated and thus stands in need of a purpose, then likewise and it would regress infinitely, leading to an infinite series of originated events, and its falsehood has already been mentioned. Also: the purpose is either a benefit that returns to Him, or to His action. The first is impossible because it necessitates the description of His exalted essence with accidental occurrences; and the second is impossible because there is no obligation to consider what is good and what is best, and because He is able to convey that benefit to the servant, for example, without an intermediary, and because it would entail the cause of something by itself or an infinite regress. (This is because) we would transfer the discussion to that same benefit. They said: If there is no purpose, then the action is foolishness. We say: Foolishness by convention is that which is done with ignorance of consequences, or preferring immediate pleasure, so that the foolish person does what harms him or causes his destruction while he is unaware. And how far is this from the action of the Exalted One, who is above the renewal of perfection or deficiency, from whose knowledge nothing at all escapes, in secret or in public?! And if you have understood, from what has been mentioned, the lack of preference of some actions over others with respect to Him, the Exalted, then you have understood the ignorance of one who trespasses upon the unseen and thinks that the intellect, without a revealed law, can attain to the perception of good and evil with respect to Him, the Mighty and Majestic. Moreover, even if that were conceded to them as a dialectical point, the intellect could not be certain of anything because of the contradictory aspects [lit. faces] of consideration in that matter. Thus, we do not know the obligation of faith nor the prohibition of disbelief except after the coming of the revealed law.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument defending the Ash'ari position that God's actions are not motivated by purposes (aghrāḍ) and that the human intellect without revelation cannot determine moral value. The passage begins with a proof of the impossibility of assigning a purpose to God's action: if the action were temporally originated, it would require a purpose, leading to an infinite regress of purposes, which is false. Then it analyzes the two possibilities for the purpose—either a benefit returning to God or to His action. Both are impossible: the first because it would necessitate change in God's essence; the second because God is not obliged to do what is most beneficial, He can achieve the benefit without mediation, and it would cause circular reasoning or regress. The objectors then argue that an action without purpose is foolish (safah). The answer redefines safah as acting with ignorance of consequences or preference for immediate pleasure—qualities impossible for God given His perfect knowledge and lack of change. The conclusion is that since God has no preference for one action over another, the intellect cannot independently know good and evil, so we need revelation to know the obligation of faith and the prohibition of disbelief.

Key Terms
غرض
تسلسل
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God must have a purpose for His actions, just like humans.
Correction: This passage shows that any possible purpose leads to absurdities (infinite regress, change, obligation, circularity), so God's actions are not motivated by purposes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the first possibility (purpose as a benefit returning to God) impossible?
A) Because it would imply God is ignorant of the benefit.
B) Because it would necessitate describing Allah’s essence with accidental occurrences (hawadith).
C) Because it would lead to an infinite regress.
D) Because benefit cannot be conceived for God.

Key Takeaway
Allah's actions are not motivated by purposes; any purpose would lead to impossible consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, you almost always weigh outcomes and purposes. But this lesson tells you that Allah—the Creator—does not act for any purpose. What does that reveal about how deeply you still think of Allah as a being like you, subject to needs and motivations? Can you truly accept that His actions are free from any purpose, even when it seems hard for you to understand?
?
This passage argues that any purpose in God's actions would imply deficiency. Yet, how often do you find yourself questioning God's wisdom or demanding reasons for His decrees? What does that say about your heart's attitude toward His absolute transcendence?
?
The infinite regress argument shows that purposes lead to an endless chain. In your own life, do you sometimes feel that your goals and purposes are endless, leaving you anxious? How does knowing that Allah has no need for purposes give you a perspective to trust His plan without needing to see the end goal?

Redefining Foolishness and the Need for Revelation
Sanusi redefines foolishness and argues that revelation is necessary for moral knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conventional definition of foolishness (safah) and why it does not apply to Allah, leading to the conclusion that the intellect alone cannot know moral values without revelation.

Core Idea
True foolishness requires ignorance; God is all-knowing, so His actions are never foolish.

After refuting the claim that God's actions need a purpose, Sanusi addresses the objection that purposeless action is foolish. He defines safah in human terms—acting with ignorance of consequences or preference for immediate pleasure—and shows that these qualities are impossible for God. This leads to a powerful conclusion: since God has no preference among actions, the human intellect cannot independently determine good and evil; we need the revealed law (shar‘) to know the obligations of faith and prohibitions of disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the objection that an action without purpose is foolish, and Sanusi's redefinition of foolishness.
The objection is based on a common human intuition, but Sanusi shifts the definition.
2
Analyze the definition of safah
Notice that safah is defined conventionally as acting with ignorance of consequences or preferring immediate pleasure. Understand why this definition cannot apply to Allah.
Allah has perfect knowledge and is not subject to change or pleasure-seeking.
3
Connect to the role of revelation
Sanusi concludes that since Allah has no preference among actions, the intellect cannot determine moral value without revelation. Understand why this follows from the previous arguments.
This is a foundational Ash'ari principle: moral values are known only through shari‘ah.

Source Text

Proof that lying is impossible for God
Sanusi proves that lying is impossible for God because His speech must match His knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that lying is impossible for God because His speech must match His necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
A lie would negate God's knowledge, which is impossible.

Sanusi presents a concise proof that lying is impossible for God. He argues that God's speech (خبر) is always in accordance with His knowledge, so it must be true. If it were false, it would imply that God lacks knowledge, which is impossible because knowledge is a necessary attribute of God. This short passage uses the logical concept of ملزوم (necessary concomitant) to establish the impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify key components
Break down the passage into the claim, the premise, and the logical implication. The claim is that lying is impossible. The premise is that God's speech matches His knowledge. The implication is that if speech were false, knowledge would be negated.
Notice the logical term 'ملزوم' (necessary concomitant).
3
Understand the logical chain
The proof uses a conditional: if truth of speech is negated, then its necessary condition (knowledge) is also negated. Since knowledge is necessary, its negation is impossible, so the truth of speech cannot be negated—hence lying is impossible.
This is a proof by contradiction.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this goes beyond just saying 'God does not lie'. It shows lying is not merely prohibited but logically impossible due to God's essential nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكذب عليه جلَّ وعلا محالٌ؛ لأنَّ خبرَهُ على وَفْق علمِهِ، فيكون صدقاً، فلو انتفى لانتفى العلم ملزومه، وهو محال؛ لما عرفتَ مِنْ وجوبِهِ
And lying upon Him, exalted and majestic, is impossible; because His news is in accordance with His knowledge, so it is true. Thus if it [the truth] were negated, its necessary concomitant — knowledge — would be negated, and that is impossible due to what you have known of its necessity.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a proof that lying is impossible for God. It begins by stating the claim: lying upon God is impossible. The proof: God's speech (خبر) must match His knowledge; if He knows something to be true, His report must be true. So truth is necessary. If truth were negated (i.e., if He lied), then His knowledge (which is the necessary condition for truth) would also be negated, which is impossible because God's knowledge is necessary. The phrase 'ملزومه' refers to knowledge being the necessary concomitant of truth: truth cannot exist without knowledge. So a lie would entail lack of knowledge, impossible for God.

Key Terms
الكذب
محال
الخبر
العلم
الصدق
الملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lying is merely forbidden for God but still possible.
Correction: This proof shows lying is impossible by logical necessity, not just forbidden. A lie would negate God's knowledge, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why is lying impossible for God according to this proof?
A) Because God is merciful
B) Because God's speech must match His knowledge, and its opposite would negate His knowledge
C) Because God cannot speak lies
D) Because prophets are trustworthy

Key Takeaway
Lying is impossible for God because His speech must correspond to His necessary knowledge, making false speech entail a negation of that knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that lying is impossible for Allah because His speech must match His knowledge. Knowing that Allah is all-knowing and all-truthful, examine your own speech: When was the last time you lied or exaggerated? Did you realize that you were contradicting the very attribute of truthfulness that defines your Lord? Write down one specific situation where you are tempted to lie, and commit to speaking the truth even if it is uncomfortable. What concrete action will you take today to align your speech with Allah's perfect truth?
?
This proof shows that lying would negate Allah's knowledge, which is impossible because His knowledge is necessary. Reflect on your life: Do you truly live as if Allah knows everything, including your private thoughts and hidden deeds? How does this certainty reshape your fear of Allah? If you truly believed that every word you speak is measured against His perfect knowledge, would you change anything? Write a sincere du'a asking Allah to make you among the truthful and to protect you from the evil of false speech.
?
Sanusi uses a logical argument to prove that lying is impossible for Allah. Have you ever considered that your own actions can imply a deficiency in Allah's attributes? For example, when you sin in secret, are you acting as if Allah does not know? This lesson teaches that Allah's knowledge is necessary and encompasses all. How will you remind yourself daily of His encompassing knowledge? Choose one practical reminder (e.g., a note, a phone wallpaper, a specific du'a) and commit to using it this week to increase your awareness of His presence.

Answering the Objection: Human Lying and Divine Truthfulness
Sanusi answers the objection that humans sometimes lie despite knowing the truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi resolves the objection that humans sometimes lie despite knowing the truth, by distinguishing between inner speech and outward expression.

Core Idea
Genuine knowledge cannot produce a lie in the heart; lies are only in outward words.

The objection seems plausible: if humans can lie even when they know the truth, perhaps God's speech could also be false. But Sanusi shows this analogy fails because the part of the heart that possesses knowledge can never contradict it. The lie is only in outward expression, which does not apply to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the passage once, noticing its structure: a hypothetical objection followed by a refutation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that Sanusi distinguishes between inner speech (الخبر النفسي) and outer words. This distinction is central to his answer.
3
Understand the human example
Sanusi argues that a human's inner speech cannot be false; only outward speech can. So the objection only applies to outward speech, which God does not have.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself why this distinction is important for understanding God's attribute of truthfulness. How does it preserve divine perfection?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : قد وجدنا العالِمَ منا بالشيء يخبر عنه بالكذب . قلنا : كلامنا في الخبر النفسي، لا في الألفاظ؛ لاستحالة اتصاف الباري تعالى بها، والعالم منا بالشيء يستحيلُ أنْ يخبر الجزء مِنْ قلبِهِ الذي قام به العلم بخبرٍ كذب على غيرِ وَفْقِ علمِهِ، غايتُهُ : أَنْ يجد في نفسِهِ تقدير الكذب، لا الكذب
If you say: We have found among us a person who knows something yet reports it falsely. We say: Our discussion is about the inner speech, not about the words; because it is impossible to attribute [outer speech] to the Most High. And the one among us who knows something: it is impossible for the part of his heart in which knowledge resides to report a lie that does not accord with his knowledge. His utmost is that he finds in himself the estimation of a lie, not the lie itself.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector argues that humans sometimes lie despite knowing the truth, so why can't God? The answer distinguishes between inner speech (الخبر النفسي) and outward expression. In God's case, only inner speech is relevant because God does not have physical speech. For humans, the part of the heart that contains knowledge cannot produce a lie; the lie is only in the outward expression or in a different part of the self that estimates a falsehood. Thus, the analogy fails.

Key Terms
الخبر النفسي
الكذب
العلم
القلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the objection disproves God's truthfulness because humans can lie despite knowing the truth.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the objection confuses outward expression with inner speech. Divine speech is inner speech, which in humans is never false; thus the analogy fails.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi respond to the objection that humans lie despite knowledge?
A) He denies that humans lie
B) He says human lies are only in outward words, not in the inner speech that matches knowledge
C) He says the objection is irrelevant
D) He says God's speech is different

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defends God's necessary truthfulness by showing that the objection confuses outward expression with inner speech, which alone is relevant to divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you said something you knew was false. Which part of you spoke: the part that knew the truth, or something else? What does this passage reveal about the integrity of your heart's knowledge? Will you commit to letting your true knowledge govern your speech from now on?
?
If God's speech is necessarily truthful, how does that change the way you read the Quran? Do you ever approach it with doubt? What would it mean for your life to fully trust that every word is true?
?
Sanusi says the heart's knowledge cannot lie, yet we often act as if we don't know what we know. Is there an area of your life where your actions or words contradict what you truly believe? What would it cost you to align them, and what would you gain?

Another Proof: Lying as an Eternal Attribute Would Preclude Truthfulness
Sanusi gives an additional argument that if lying were an eternal attribute of God, truthfulness would be impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional rational proof that lying cannot be an attribute of God, because it would contradict His necessary truthfulness.

Core Idea
If lying were eternal in God, truthfulness would be impossible—but God is truthful.

Sanusi presents a second, closely related argument against the possibility of lying in God. This proof works by showing that if lying were an eternal attribute, it would make truthfulness impossible. Since we know God is truthful (as He has perfect knowledge), the impossibility of lying is established. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if ... then ...'.
2
Understand the conditional premise
Identify the hypothetical: if God were described by lying, and that attribute is eternal, then truthfulness would be impossible.
Contrary attributes cannot coexist in the same subject.
3
Connect to God's knowledge
The proof invokes God's necessary knowledge: because He knows all, He must be truthful. So the impossibility of what we know to be sound (truthfulness) shows the premise (lying) is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : لوِ اتَّصفَ البارى تعالى بالكذب ولا تكونُ صفتُه إلا قديمة .. لاستحال اتصافه بالصدق مع صحة اتصافِهِ بهِ؛ لأجلِ وجوبِ العلم له تعالى، ففيه استحالة ما علمنا صحتَهُ
Also: if the Most High were described by lying, and His attribute is only eternal, then His description by truthfulness would be impossible, despite the validity of His being described by it, because of the necessity of knowledge for Him, Most High. So in it is the impossibility of what we have known to be sound.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This is another proof against the possibility of lying for God. It argues by considering the hypothetical: if lying were attributed to God as an eternal attribute, then it would be impossible for Him to also have truthfulness as an attribute, since contrary attributes cannot coexist. But we know that God does describe Himself as truthful (e.g., 'And who is more truthful than Allah?'). Moreover, truthfulness is necessitated by His perfect knowledge, so lying is impossible.

Key Terms
الصدق
الكذب
الصفات القديمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God could have both lying and truthfulness as attributes in different respects or contexts.
Correction: But divine attributes are essential and eternal—they are not temporary or context-dependent. If lying were eternal, it would be a permanent quality, making truthfulness impossible to coexist.

Checkpoint
What is the reasoning of the second proof?
A) If God could lie, He would not be eternal
B) If God had lying as an eternal attribute, He could not also be truthful, but we know He is truthful
C) God cannot have attributes
D) Truthfulness is optional

Key Takeaway
Lying cannot be an attribute of God because it would contradict the necessary truthfulness that follows from His perfect knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot lie, what does that mean for your own trust in His promises? Is there any promise you doubt? How can you anchor your certainty in His truthfulness?
?
Consider an area of your life where you have deceived yourself or others. Knowing that God is perfectly truthful, how does that challenge you to be truthful? What step will you take today?
?
God's truthfulness is rooted in His perfect knowledge. In what area of your life are you settling for ignorance or half-truths? How will you pursue the full truth, even if it is uncomfortable?

The World's Agent Acts by Choice
Sanusi presents a proof that the world's agent must be voluntary, leading to the world's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the Maker of the world must be a voluntary agent and why this entails the world's creation.

Core Idea
Only a voluntary agent can specify one like over another.

Sanusi now moves from proving that the world has a Maker to proving that this Maker acts by choice, which implies the world is created. He begins by addressing the philosophers who accept the possibility of a voluntary Maker but need a separate proof. This passage provides that proof by analyzing the modes of influence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how Sanusi structures his argument with an enumeration and refutation.
2
Identify the three modes of influence
Find where Sanusi lists the three ways an agent can act: by essence, by nature, or by choice. Understand the reasoning behind this division.
The division is exhaustive: every agent either can refrain or cannot.
3
Understand the refutation of necessity and nature
Sanusi argues that the first two modes (necessity and nature) cannot produce specification of one like over another. See why this is impossible for a necessary cause or natural agent.
A necessary cause produces uniform effects; it cannot explain differences.
4
Follow the conclusion to creation
Since the Maker specifies likes over likes, He must be voluntary. Then, Sanusi deduces that whatever is brought about by choice must be created, because choice presupposes prior non-existence.
This avoids the fallacy of 'تحصيل الحاصل' (acquiring what is already attained).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقول الفلاسفةُ ، واحتمالِ أنْ يكونَ صانعاً بالاختيار ، فيكون العالم حادثاً ، فيحتاج إلى دليل آخر لإثباتِ هذا المطلب - أعني : مطلب حدوث العالم - بعدَما فرغتَ مِنْ مطلب وجودِ الصانع الذي نظرُكَ فيهِ ونظرُ الفيلسوفيّ واحدٌ ، وإنَّما تنفرد عنه بهذا المطلب الثاني ، فإنَّه لم يهتد هو إليه ، فتقولُ :
صانع العالم : إمَّا أنْ يكونَ أوجبَهُ لذاتِهِ ، أو اقتضاه بطبعه ، أو أوجده باختياره ، وجهات التأثيرِ منحصرة في هذه الأوجه الثلاثة ، ووجه الحصرِ : أَنَّ كلَّ مُؤثر لا يخلو : إما أن يصح منه التركُ أو لا ، والأوَّلُ : الفاعلُ المختارُ ، والثاني : إمَّا أَنْ يَتوقَّفَ اقتضاؤُهُ على شرط وانتفاء مانع أو لا ، والأوّل : الطبيعةُ ، والثاني : العِلَّةُ
ثم تقولُ : لا جائزَ أنْ يكونَ المؤثر في هذه الممكناتِ مُوجِباً لها بذاتِهِ كالعِلَّةِ ، ولا مقتضياً لها بطبعه ؛ لأنَّ ما يُؤثر كذلكَ لا يجوز أَنْ يُخصِّصَ مِثْلاً عن مِثْلِ ؛ لاستحالة الاختلاف في معلولِ العِلَّة الواحدة ، ومطبوع الطبيعة الواحدة ، وفاعلُ العَالَمِ قد خَصَّصَ مِثْلاً عن مِثْلِ ، فتعيَّنَ أَنْ يكونَ مُوجِداً بالاختيار .
فتقولُ حيثَذٍ : العالَمُ مُوقَعٌ بالاختيار ، وكلُّ مُوقع بالاختيار حادث ؛ إذ اختيار وجودِهِ يستلزم سبق عدمِهِ ، وإلا كان تحصيل الحاصل في الوجودِ ، وثبوت تمكن ممَّا لا يصح كونه في العدم (۱) ؛ فينتج : العالم حادث .
(۱) قوله : ( وثبوت تمكُن ) يعني : وكان اختيار وجوده ذا ثبوت تمكن ، = والتمكُنُ : التأثير ، وقوله : ( مما ) ( من ) بمعنى ( في ) ، و( ما ) واقعة على موجود ؛ أي : وكان اختيار وجوده تأثيراً في موجود ليس متصفاً بالعدم بل بالوجود ، وهذا تحصيل للحاصل . مفادٌ « حامدي » ( ص ۱۰۸ )
You say to the philosophers: and the possibility that He is a Maker by choice, so the world would be created, so it needs another proof to establish this claim – I mean: the claim of the creation of the world – after you have finished from the claim of the existence of the Maker, in which your consideration and the philosopher's consideration are one, and you are distinguished from him only by this second claim, for he did not arrive at it. So you say: The Maker of the world: either He necessitates it by His essence, or it is required by His nature, or He brings it into existence by His choice. The modes of influence are limited to these three modes. The reason for the limitation: that every agent is not free from: either it is possible for it to refrain or not. The first is the voluntary agent. The second: either its requirement depends on a condition and the absence of an obstacle or not. The first is nature, and the second is the cause. Then you say: It is not permissible that the one influencing these possible things be a necessitator of them by His essence like the cause, nor a requirer of them by His nature; because what influences in such a way does not permit specification of a like in preference to a like, due to the impossibility of difference in the effect of a single cause and the imprint of a single nature. And the Maker of the world has specified a like in preference to a like, so it is determined that He is a creator by choice. So you say then: The world is brought about by choice, and everything brought about by choice is created; because the choice of its existence entails the precedence of its non-existence, otherwise it would be the acquisition of what is already attained in existence, and the establishment of ability for what cannot be in non-existence; so it results: the world is created. (1) His words: (wa thubūt tamakkun) means: and the choice of its existence was a possessor of established ability. And tamakkun is influence. And His words: (mimmā) – (min) is in the sense of (fī), and (mā) refers to an existent; that is, the choice of its existence was an influence on an existent not described by non-existence but by existence, and this is the acquisition of what is already attained. From the benefit of Hamidi (p. 108).
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the world is created, specifically by establishing that its Agent acts by choice. Sanusi begins by addressing the philosophers, noting that they accept the possibility of a voluntary Maker, which implies the world could be created. However, they need a separate proof for this claim, distinct from the proof of the Maker's existence. Sanusi then provides that proof.
The proof is structured as an enumeration of the possible modes of influence (وجهات التأثير), which are three: necessitation by essence (الإيجاب بالذات), requirement by nature (الاقتضاء بالطبع), and creation by choice (الإيجاد بالاختيار). He then gives the reason for this enumeration: every agent either can refrain from acting or cannot. If it can refrain, it is a voluntary agent. If it cannot refrain, then either its action depends on conditions (nature) or it does not (cause).
Next, Sanusi argues that the first two modes are impossible for the Maker of the world. The reason is that a necessary cause (علة) or a natural agent (طبيعة) cannot produce variation in effects; their effects are uniform. Yet the Maker of the world has specified (خصص) one like over another – for example, making one body here and another there, or causing one thing to exist before another. This specification cannot come from a necessary or natural agent. Therefore, the Maker must be a voluntary agent (فاعل مختار).
From this, the conclusion follows: the world is brought about by choice, and whatever is brought about by choice is created (حادث), because choosing to create something implies it was previously non-existent. Otherwise, choosing it would be redundant (تحصيل الحاصل), and a power over what already exists is impossible. The footnote clarifies the phrase 'وثبوت تمكن' explaining that it refers to the impossibility of having influence on an already existing thing.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار
طبيعة
علة
إيجاب بالذات
اختيار
حادث
مِثْل
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world could have been produced by a necessary cause or natural agent without any choice.
Correction: Sanusi argues that a necessary cause or natural agent cannot produce the specification (تخصيص) observed in the world, such as one like being preferred over another. Therefore, only a voluntary agent can account for this.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why cannot the Maker of the world be a necessary cause (علة) or a natural agent (طبيعة)?
A) Because they are not capable of creating anything
B) Because they cannot produce specification of like over like
C) Because they require a prior cause
D) Because they are not eternal

Key Takeaway
The Maker of the world must be voluntary because only a voluntary agent can specify one like over another, and this implies the world is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world were the necessary effect of a blind cause, you would owe nothing to anyone. But since He chose to create you deliberately, what does that choice demand from your life today?
?
Every detail of your existence – your location, your family, your talents – is a specification of a like over a like. Are you living as if this specification is accidental, or as a sign that you were deliberately placed by a personal Lord who expects a response?
?
Sanusi says that a natural or necessary cause cannot produce variety. Look at the variety in your own body and circumstances. How does this confront your tendency to treat God as a distant, impersonal force rather than a wilful Creator who chose you?

First Claim: The Maker is a Free Agent
Sanusi proves the first claim that the Maker is a free agent using a first-figure syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that the Maker of the self must be a free agent using a first-figure syllogism, and why nature and cause cannot account for specification.

Core Idea
Only a free agent can choose between equally possible alternatives.

Sanusi now presents the first of two claims concerning the Maker: that He is a free agent (mukhtār). The proof is a carefully constructed syllogism that examines the human self—its dimensions, accidents, and parts—to demonstrate that these specific features could have been otherwise. Since nature and cause act by necessity, they cannot produce one specific configuration among equally possible ones. Therefore, the specifier must be a free agent who chooses. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi uses a first-figure syllogism with an omitted major premise. Identify the minor and major premises.
The minor premise is that the self is specified with possibilities. The major premise is that anything specified like that has a free agent.
2
Examine the minor premise: the self's specification
Sanusi argues that the self as a whole and its parts are specified with particular dimensions, shapes, colors, and organs, while other possibilities are equally possible. List three examples from the text.
Examples: length vs width, shape, eye vs ear.
3
Examine the major premise: why nature/cause cannot specify
Nature and cause act by essential appropriateness, so they cannot produce one specific outcome among equals. Understand the reasoning: they would necessitate uniformity, not particularization.
This is why a free agent is needed.
4
Synthesize the syllogism and draw the conclusion
Combine the minor and major premises: the self is specified among possibilities, so its agent must be free. Confirm that the conclusion is proven.
The syllogism is valid and sound.
5
Reflect: what this proof establishes
The proof establishes that the Maker of the self has free will. Consider how this refutes fatalism or necessitarianism.
This is the first claim of the section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولُهُ الترتيب : : ( فتعلمُ قطعاً أنَّ لصانعِكَ اختياراً ) ادَّعى دعويَيْنِ على الأولى : أنَّ صانع ذاتِكَ فاعل مختارُ : واحتج عليها ببرهان مِنَ الشكلِ الأَوَّلِ ، حذفَ فِيهِ الكبرى للعلم بها، وتقريرُهُ أنْ تقول : ذاتُكَ اختصَّتْ بجائز بدلاً عن جائز باعتبارِ مجموعها وباعتبار أجزائها وكلُّ ما كانَ كذلك ففاعلُهُ مختار لفعله؛ فينتج : ذاتُكَ فاعلها مختار لفعله . ودليل الصغرى ظاهر ؛ فإنَّ مجموع الذاتِ قدِ اختص ببعض المقادير ؛ مِنْ كونه ذا طول مخصوص وعرض مخصوص، والطولُ أكثرُ مِنَ العرض مثلاً معَ جواز أنْ يكونَ على خلاف ذلك، والأشكال الهندسيَّةُ كلُّها في حقِّهِ جائزة لا رجحانَ لبعضها على بعض باعتبارِ ذاتِهِ، وكذا أيضاً قدِ اختصَّ ببعض الأعراض مِنَ الألوان والأصوات ونحوهما دون بعض وأما باعتبار أجزائها : فقدِ اختَصَّ بعضُها مع استوائها بأنْ كانَ عيناً، وبعضُها بأنْ كانَ أُذُناً، وبعضُها بأنْ كانَ يداً . . . إلى غير ذلكَ مِنَ الاختلافاتِ، وكلٌّ في محلّ مخصوص، ولهُ عَرَضُ مخصوص ومِقدار مخصوص، مع جواز غير ذلك في الجميع وأما دليلُ الكبرى : فلأنَّ تأثير الطبيعةِ والعِلَّةٍ لمَّا كانَ بالمناسبة الذاتية فيستحيلُ أنْ يناسب الضدَّين وأنْ يخصّ مِثْلاً عن مِثْلِ، فَتَعَيَّنَ أَنْ يكونَ المُخصِّص لذاتِكَ مختاراً .
His saying: 'So you know certainly that your Maker has choice.' He claimed two claims: the first: that the Maker of your self is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). He argued for it with a proof from the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal), omitting the major premise because it is known. Its formulation is to say: your self was specified with a possible (jāʾiz) in place of a possible, with respect to its totality and with respect to its parts. And everything that is such, its agent is free in its action. So it results: the agent of your self is free in its action. And the proof of the minor premise is apparent: for the totality of the self has been specified with certain dimensions—being of a specific length and specific width, length being more than width for example—while it is possible to be otherwise, and all geometric shapes are possible with respect to it, no one has preponderance over another by virtue of its essence. Likewise, it has been specified with certain accidents (aʿrāḍ) of colors, sounds, and the like, to the exclusion of others. And as for the parts: some of them, despite their equality, have been specified as an eye, some as an ear, some as a hand, etc., up to other differences, each in a specific place, having a specific accident and specific quantity, while the opposite is possible in all. And as for the proof of the major premise: because the effect of nature (ṭabīʿa) and cause (ʿilla), since it is by essential appropriateness (al-munāsaba al-dhātiyya), it is impossible that it be appropriate for opposites or that it specify a like from a like. So it is determined that the specifier of your self is free.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two claims made by the author: that the Maker of your self is a free agent (mukhtār). The proof is given as a syllogism in the first figure, with the major premise omitted for being obvious. The reader is asked to consider the self, which is characterized by specific dimensions and accidents that could have been otherwise (e.g., length could be less, shape could be different). This specification (ikhtiṣāṣ) of one possible over another cannot be due to nature or cause, because nature and cause act by essential appropriateness, which would necessitate uniformity, not particularization. Therefore, the specifier must be a free agent who chooses one possibility over another. The minor premise is then argued in detail: the self as a whole is specified with a particular height and width, and with particular colors, sounds, etc., while other possibilities are equally possible. Similarly, the parts of the self (eye, ear, hand) are each specified even though they are equal in being parts. The major premise is argued: nature and cause, because they necessitate based on essence, cannot produce one specific configuration among equally possible ones; they would produce the same result in all cases. Hence, the specifier must be free.

Key Terms
مختار (mukhtār)
جائز (jāʾiz)
برهان الشكل الأول (burhān al-shakl al-awwal)
طبيعة (ṭabīʿa)
علة (ʿilla)
مناسبة ذاتية (al-munāsaba al-dhātiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on the impossibility of alternate possibilities.
Correction: Actually, both alternatives are equally possible; the proof shows that a free agent is needed to choose one.

Checkpoint
What is the figure of the syllogism used to prove the first claim?
A) The second figure
B) The first figure
C) The third figure
D) The fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The proof establishes that the Maker of the self must be a free agent because the self's specification among equally possible alternatives cannot be explained by nature or cause, which act by necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every detail of your body—the length of your fingers, the color of your eyes—was chosen by a free Agent from infinite possibilities. How often do you complain about or wish to change these details instead of thanking the One who specifically selected them for you? What would it look like to truly accept that they were divinely chosen?
?
The proof shows that your existence is not the product of blind nature but of a deliberate, free choice. Do you live as if you are a random accident or a purposeful creation? What would change in your daily life if you fully believed that the Maker freely chose to bring you into being?
?
If the Maker had to be free because nature cannot specify, then your very shape is a sign of His freedom. Are you grateful for this sign, or do you take it for granted? Starting today, how can you actively thank the Free Agent for the specific qualities He gave you?

The Maker is Not a Drop or a Nature
Sanusi proves the second claim using a second-figure syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a second-figure syllogism to prove that the Creator is not a blind cause like nature, but a free agent.

Core Idea
The Creator is a free agent, not a necessary cause like nature.

Sanusi now moves to the second claim, which is the main target: proving that the Maker is not a drop (nufṭa) — meaning not any nature or cause. This argument completes the proof that the Maker is a free agent distinct from all natural causes. The proof is a syllogism in the second figure, relying on the previously established minor premise that the Maker is a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi states the claim and then provides the proof.
The passage begins by identifying the second claim as the intended conclusion.
2
Identify the syllogism components
Look for the minor premise, major premise, and conclusion. The minor premise is 'the Maker is a free agent'. The major premise is 'nothing of the drop is a free agent'. The conclusion is 'the Maker is not a drop'.
The syllogism is in the second figure.
3
Understand the second figure
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. Here, 'free agent' is the middle term. Ask why this figure is appropriate.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why the Maker cannot be a nature or cause. Nature acts by necessity, but the Maker acts by free choice. This distinguishes the Creator from all created causes.
This builds on the first claim about the Maker's free agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانيةُ مِنَ الدعويين - وهي المقصودة، والأولى وسيلة لها - : أنَّ صانع ذاتِكَ ليس بنطفةٍ، وفي معناها نفْيُ أنْ يكونَ طبيعةً أو عِلَّةً على العموم، ودليل هذه الدعوى مِنَ الشكل الثاني أن تقول : صانع ذاتِكَ فاعل مختار، ولا شيء مِنَ النطفة - وفي معناها كلُّ طبيعة أو عِلَّةٍ - بفاعل مختار، فينتجُ : صانعُ ذاتِكَ ليسَ بنطفة، وفي معناه : ليسَ بطبيعةٍ ولا عِلَّةٍ عموماً، ودليل الصغرى والكبرى سبق
The second of the two claims—and it is the intended one, while the first is a means to it—: that the Maker of your self is not a drop (nufṭa), and by its meaning [is] the negation of its being a nature (ṭabīʿa) or a cause (ʿilla) universally. The proof of this claim from the second figure is to say: the Maker of your self is a free agent, and nothing of the drop—and by its meaning every nature or cause—is a free agent. So it results: the Maker of your self is not a drop, and by its meaning: is not a nature or a cause universally. The proof of the minor and major premises has preceded.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the second claim, which is the intended conclusion of the overall argument: that the Maker of your self is not a drop (nufṭa). The term 'drop' here is representative of any nature or cause. The proof is a syllogism in the second figure (al-shakl al-thānī). The minor premise is 'the Maker of your self is a free agent' (already proven in the first claim). The major premise is 'nothing of the drop—meaning any nature or cause—is a free agent' (because nature and cause act by necessity, not choice). Therefore, the conclusion follows: the Maker of your self is not a drop, i.e., not a nature or cause. The proof of both premises was already given in the previous passage.

Key Terms
نطفة (nufṭa)
شكل ثاني (shakl thānī)
مختار (mukhtār)
طبيعة (ṭabīʿa)
علة (ʿilla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves the Maker is not material (i.e., not a physical drop).
Correction: The argument proves the Maker is not a blind cause like nature, not that He is immaterial per se.

Checkpoint
What is the figure of the syllogism used for the second claim?
A) First figure
B) Second figure
C) Third figure
D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The Maker is not a drop, nature, or cause because He is a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your existence is not merely the result of biological processes or natural laws, but the deliberate act of a free Creator? How does this belief shape your daily sense of purpose and accountability?
?
If the Maker is a free agent, then your relationship with Him is not mechanical but personal. Are you living as if you are in a personal relationship with the One who chose to bring you into existence?
?
When you look at yourself, do you see a product of nature or a sign of a free Creator? What would change in your life if you fully internalized that you are not a drop but a deliberate creation?

intermediate

Refutation of Nature in Growth
Sanusi refutes that nature or cause governs the growth of the human self.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the idea that nature or cause can explain the growth of the human body, and why a free agent is necessary.

Core Idea
Growth's finite and varied nature proves it is not from nature but from a free agent.

Sanusi continues his argument against natural causation by addressing a subtle objection. One might think that while the specification of body parts comes from Allah, the actual growth of those parts could be left to nature. Sanusi shows that even growth cannot be explained by nature, because growth stops at a specific limit and differs across body parts—features that require a choosing agent. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by addressing a potential misunderstanding about specification and nature.
2
Identify the two main refutations
The passage refutes nature in two ways: first, in the specification of which part becomes which; second, in the growth of those parts. Find where each refutation occurs.
3
Understand the 'stopping at a limit' argument
Focus on the sentence: 'that stopping at a specific amount in growth and its cessation from beyond that, while it is possible, prevents growth from being an effect of nature.' Ask yourself why this is a decisive proof.
If nature caused growth, it would continue indefinitely because nature is uniform and has no reason to stop.
4
Consider the uniformity argument
Note the additional point: if nature caused growth, the same nature would produce the same growth in all parts, yet hands and heads grow differently. This also shows nature cannot be the cause.
5
Summarize the necessity of a free agent
Combine the two arguments to conclude that both specification and growth require a free agent who chooses limits and variations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولا في نموها ( هذا مبالغةٌ في الرَّدَّ لِمَا يُتوهَّمُ أَنَّ الفاعل المختار خصَّص بعض النطفة بكونه يداً، والبعض بكونه رجلاً، والبعض بكونه رأساً، والبعضَ بكونِهِ أُذُناً ... إلى غير ذلك؛ ولا تأثير للنطفة بل ولا لطبيعة ولا لعِلَّةٍ في شيءٍ مِنْ ذلكَ؛ لِمَا ذُكِرَ قبلُ، وإنَّما طبعها في نمو تلك الأجزاء المُخصَّصة بالغيرِ، والنمو معنى واحد فلم يلزم مِنْ تأثير النطفة والطبيعة فيه اختلافُ مطبوعها. ووجه الردّ بما ذكرَ : أنَّ الوقوف على مقدار مخصوص في النموّ وانقطاعَهُ عمَّا فوقَ ذلكَ معَ جوازِهِ .. يمنعُ أنْ يكونَ النمو أيضاً أثراً لطبيعة، وفي معناها العِلَّةُ؛ إذ لو كانَ أثراً لهما للزمَ ألا تقف الذاتُ في نموها، ولكانَتْ تنمو أبداً، على أنَّ تقديرها مؤثرةً في النمو لا يدفعُ لزوم اختلافِ مطبوعها أيضاً؛ لأنَّ النمو الذي في اليدِ مثلاً...
His saying: 'And not in its growth (numuww).' This is an exaggeration in refuting what might be imagined, that the free agent specified some of the drop as a hand, some as a leg, some as a head, some as an ear... etc. And there is no effect of the drop, nor of nature nor cause in any of that, due to what was mentioned before. Rather, its nature (ṭabʿ) is in the growth of those parts specified by another, and growth is a single meaning; so the effect of the drop and nature does not necessitate difference in what is impressed (maṭbūʿ). And the point of the refutation with what was mentioned: that stopping at a specific amount in growth and its cessation from beyond that, while it is possible [to continue], prevents growth from being an effect of nature, and by its meaning cause; for if it were an effect of them, it would be necessary that the self not stop in its growth, and it would grow forever. Moreover, their being effective in growth does not repel the necessity of difference in what is impressed; because the growth which is in the hand, for example...
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: that the free agent's specification of parts (hand, leg, etc.) might be attributed to nature, but the author argues that nature (or cause) cannot be responsible for the growth of those parts. First, the drop (nufṭa) has no effect on the specification of which part becomes what—that is done by the free agent. Nature's role is only in growth after specification. But even growth cannot be an effect of nature because if it were, growth would never stop; it would continue indefinitely. Yet we observe that growth stops at a specific limit, while it is possible to grow more. This shows that even the growth process is controlled by a free agent, not by nature. Furthermore, if nature caused growth, then the same nature would produce the same growth in all parts, but different parts grow differently (e.g., hand vs. head), which contradicts the uniformity of nature. Thus, growth also requires a specifying free agent.

طبع (ṭabʿ)
مطبوع (maṭbūʿ)
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ)
المختار (al-mukhtār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Growth is a purely natural process that does not require divine intervention.
Correction: The finite and differentiated nature of growth shows that it requires a free agent who chooses specific limits and variations.

Checkpoint
Why does the cessation of growth at a specific limit refute that growth is caused by nature?
A) Because nature would cause unlimited growth if it were the only cause
B) Because growth is too complex for nature
C) Because growth is an accident
D) Because nature is uniform but growth is varied

Key Takeaway
The finite and varied nature of bodily growth necessitates a free agent, not nature or cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body—your height, your features, your health—do you truly see them as direct creations of God's will in every moment, or do you take them for granted as 'natural'? What would it mean for your gratitude if you acknowledged His constant act of specifying and sustaining your growth?
?
If every aspect of your physical development—from infancy to adulthood—is determined by God's free choice, then what about your spiritual growth? Have you been relying on your own efforts and 'nature' to change, or are you actively turning to the One who alone can cause transformation?
?
The argument says that if growth came from nature, it would never stop. In your life, have you ever experienced a point where you 'stopped growing'—in wisdom, patience, or faith? Could it be that your stagnation is a sign that growth is not automatic, but depends on the will of the Free Agent? Will you ask Him today to resume His work in you?

Proving God's Eternity and Defining Qidam
Sanusi proves God must be eternal and distinguishes two meanings of qidam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Creator must be eternal (qadim) based on a rational proof and how the term 'qidam' has two distinct meanings, only one of which applies to Allah.

Core Idea
God's eternity is proven by reason: only two options, regress or circularity, both impossible.

This lesson begins with a concise rational proof that the Creator cannot have a beginning. If He were not eternal, He would require a cause, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul) or a logical circle (dawr)—both rationally absurd. After establishing the necessity of qidam, Sanusi clarifies the word itself, showing that it can mean either 'ancient in time' or 'beyond time', and only the second fits Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical argument and the definitional distinction.
The passage has two main parts: a proof and a semantic clarification.
2
Understand the proof structure
The proof is a conditional: if God is not qadim (i.e., preceded by non-existence), then He would need a maker, leading to either infinite regress (if the maker is not His effect) or circularity (if it is). Both are impossible, so God must be qadim.
Infinite regress is impossible because it would require completing an infinite series, which is absurd. Circularity is impossible because it would make something prior to itself.
3
Distinguish the two meanings of qidam
Sanusi presents the linguistic usage: qidam can mean 'that over which times have passed' (temporal oldness) or 'that to which time does not apply' (timelessness). The first is impossible for God; the second is the correct attribute.
The first meaning is what people call 'old' like an old building. The second is what we affirm of Allah.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Many think 'qadim' just means 'very old' in time. This passage shows that the true meaning for God is 'without beginning' and 'outside of time'. Consider why temporal oldness would imply change and dependence on time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفات التلوب صفة القدم فصل في إثبات صفة القدم له تعالى، ونفي الزمان عنه ] ثم نقولُ : يجب أن يكون هذا الصانع لذاتِكَ ولسائر العالم قديماً ؛ أي : غير مسبوق بعدم ، وإلا لافتقر إلى محدث ، وذلك يؤدّي إلى التسلسل إنْ كانَ محدثه ليس أثراً له ، أو إلى الدَّورِ إنْ كانَ ، والتسلسل والدور محالانِ ؛ لما في الأوَّلِ مِنْ فراغ ما لا نهاية له بالعددِ ، وفي الثاني مِنْ كون الشيء الواحد سابقاً على نفسه مسبوقاً بها اعلمْ : أَنَّ ( القِدَمَ ) يُطلَقُ في مقتضى اللسان بإزاء معنيين : يُطلق على : ما توالَتْ على وجودِهِ الأزمنة ، وكرَّ عليه الجديدانِ الليل والنهارُ ، ومنه قوله تعالى : ﴿ كَالْعُرْجُونِ الْقَدِيمِ ﴾ [يسَ : ٣٩] ، وبهذا الاعتبار يُقالُ : أساس قديم ، وبناء قديم . ا لا يمر عليهِ عزَّ وجلَّ زمان ، وبيان حقيقةِ الزمانِ ] وهذا الاعتبار مستحيل في حقه جل وعلا ؛ إذ وجوده ليسَ وجوداً
Attributes of affirmation? The attribute of Qidam. Chapter on establishing the attribute of Qidam for Him, Exalted, and negating time from Him. Then we say: It is necessary that this Maker be eternal in His Essence and for the whole world; i.e., not preceded by non-existence. Otherwise He would need a maker, and that leads to infinite regress if His maker is not His effect, or to circularity if it is. And infinite regress and circularity are impossible: due in the first to the completion of what has no end in number, and in the second to the thing being prior to itself and preceded by itself. Know that 'qidam' in linguistic usage is applied to two meanings: it is applied to that over which times have passed sequentially and the two new things—night and day—have repeated, and from it is His statement: 'Like an old palm stalk' (Q 36:39). In this sense one says: an old foundation, an old building. i.e., time does not pass over Him, Mighty and Majestic, and [this is] explaining the reality of time. And this consideration is impossible in His case, Mighty and Sublime, because His existence is not an existence...
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on proving the divine attribute of qidam (eternity/past-eternity) and negating time from God. The author first gives a rational proof: if God were not eternal (i.e., preceded by non-existence), He would need a creator, leading to either infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr), both absurd. Thus, God must be eternal. Then he clarifies the term 'qidam' by distinguishing two linguistic meanings: (1) that which has continued through time, like an old building; (2) that which is not subject to time. The first meaning implies change and is impossible for God, while the second is the correct attribute. The author then begins to explain the reality of time, concluding that the first meaning is impossible for God because His existence is not temporal.

قديم
زمان
تسلسل
دور
لا مسبوق بعدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'qadim' means simply 'ancient' or 'old in time', implying Allah has existed through time.
Correction: Sanusi shows that 'qadim' has two meanings: one temporal (old) and one atemporal (without beginning). Only the second applies to Allah, as time does not pass over Him.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's proof, what would happen if the Maker were not qadim?
A) He would be eternal anyway
B) It would lead to infinite regress or circularity
C) He would be made of matter
D) He would be similar to creation

Key Takeaway
God must be eternal (qadim) with no beginning, and this is proven by the impossibility of infinite regress and circularity; the term 'qadim' for God means timelessness, not old age.

?
If God is truly timeless and has no beginning, how does that reshape your understanding of your own life's smallness and your need for His eternal perspective? What would it mean to trust His eternal plan instead of your limited timeline?
?
The proof shows that everything besides God must have a cause and is contingent. Are there areas in your life where you treat created things (wealth, status, relationships) as ultimate causes—as if they are the foundation of your security? How can you shift that reliance to God alone?
?
Sanusi warns against confusing linguistic 'old' with divine 'timeless'. When you think of God's eternity, do you still imagine Him as existing for a very long time rather than outside time entirely? How might that subtle confusion affect how you pray or wait upon Him?
