Two Meanings of Qidam and Negation of Time
Sanusi distinguishes two meanings of qidam and proves time is impossible for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of qidam (eternity) and why time cannot be attributed to Allah according to Sanusi's argument.

Core Idea
Allah is beyond time; qidam means eternal, not ancient.

Sanusi begins by clarifying the linguistic meaning of qidam (eternity) and immediately shows why it cannot be applied to Allah in the temporal sense. He then provides a detailed rational proof that time itself has no relation to the Divine. This passage establishes a key principle of Islamic theology: Allah's existence is timeless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the structure: first a definition, then a refutation, then the detailed proof.
The passage is dense; take your time to absorb the argument.
2
Identify the two meanings of qidam
Sanusi explicitly gives the first meaning (temporal antiquity) and states it is impossible for Allah. The second meaning, the kalam technical sense (not preceded by non-existence), is implied.
3
Follow Sanusi's proof against time
Trace his two definitions of time and why each fails for Allah. First, time as a relation between two originated events; second, time as celestial movements.
Focus on the logical steps: no originated events in al-azal, so no time; celestial movements are themselves created.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that time is absolutely impossible for Allah, in pre-eternity and future. Reflect on the significance: Allah is timeless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم: أن القِدم يُطلق في مقتضى اللسان بإزاء معنيين: يُطلق على: ما توالَتْ على وجودِهِ الأزمنة، وكرَّ عليه الجديدان الليل والنهار، ومنه قوله تعالى: {كَالْعُرْجُونِ الْقَدِيمِ} [يسَ: ٣٩]، وبهذا الاعتبار يُقال: أساس قديم، وبناء قديم. وهذا الاعتبار مستحيل في حقه جل وعلا؛ إذ وجوده ليس وجوداً زمانياً، ولا نسبة للزمان إلى وجوده ألبتة؛ إذ هو من صفات المحدث، فيكون حادثاً ضرورة؛ فإن الزمان إما عبارة عن مقارنة متجدد لمتجدّد؛ أي: حادث لحادث؛ كمقارنة السفر لطلوع الشمس مثلاً، فثبوته فرع وجود حادثين مقترني الوجود؛ لأنه نسبة بينهما، والنسبة يتأخر وجودها عن وجود المنتسبين، ولا متجدّد في الأزل، فلا زمان، والتجدد لوجوده جل وعلا وصفات ذاته العلية محال، فنسبة الزمان إليه تعالى محال على الإطلاق، في الأزل وفيما لا يزال وإما عبارة عن حركات الأفلاك، وما يرجع إليها من الساعات وأجزائها، وتعاقب الليل والنهار: إذ الليل: عبارة عن مغيب الشمس تحت الأفق، والنهار: عبارة عن ظهورها فوق الأفق، وذلك في الحقيقة عبارة عن سير الفلك الأعظم معدل النهار بها تحت الأفق أو فوقه على ما تزعم الفلاسفة، والساعة: عبارة عن سير معدل النهار خمس عشرة درجة؛ أي: خمسة عشر قسماً من ثلاث مئة وستين قسماً متساوية قسموا الفلك بها اصطلاحاً.
Know that 'eternity' (al-qidam) is used in the language in two senses: It is used for that upon which times have followed in succession and the two new things, night and day, have come repeatedly. And from this is His saying, the Exalted: {Like an old palm stalk} [Ya Seen: 39]. In this sense it is said: an ancient foundation, an ancient building. And this sense is impossible with respect to Him, may He be exalted and glorified, because His existence is not a temporal existence, and there is no relation of time to His existence at all, because it is from the attributes of the originated thing, so it would necessarily be originated. For time is either an expression of a correlation of a renewing thing with a renewing thing; i.e., an originated thing with an originated thing, like the correlation of travel with sunrise for example. So its affirmation is a branch of the existence of two originated things whose existence is concurrent, because it is a relation between them, and the relation's existence is delayed from the existence of the two related things. And there is no renewing thing in pre-eternity (al-azal), so there is no time. And renewal with respect to His existence, may He be glorified and exalted, and the attributes of His exalted essence is impossible. So the relation of time to Him, the Exalted, is absolutely impossible, in pre-eternity and in what will never cease. Or it is an expression of the movements of the spheres, and what pertains to them of hours and their parts, and the alternation of night and day: for night is an expression of the sun's setting below the horizon, and day is an expression of its appearing above the horizon. And that is in reality an expression of the movement of the greatest sphere, the equinoctial (mu'addal al-nahar), below the horizon or above it according to what the philosophers claim. And the hour is an expression of the movement of the equinoctial by fifteen degrees; i.e., fifteen parts of three hundred and sixty equal parts by which they divided the sphere conventionally.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and refutation. Sanusi first clarifies the two meanings of the term 'qidam' (eternity/antiquity). The first meaning is temporal antiquity: something that has existed for a long time, having many times pass over it. This is the common linguistic usage, as in the Quranic verse about an old palm stalk. This sense is impossible for Allah, because His existence is not temporal; time applies only to originated things. Then Sanusi provides a detailed argument: time is either (a) the relation between two renewing (originated) events, or (b) the movements of the celestial spheres. In the first sense, time depends on the existence of two concurrent originated things, and there are no originated things in the beginningless past (al-azal) because everything originated has a beginning. Therefore, time cannot exist in the azal. Moreover, renewal (tajaddud) is impossible for Allah's essence and attributes. So time cannot be related to Him at all, whether in the past or future. In the second sense, time is the movement of the spheres and the alternation of night and day. This definition relies on the existence of celestial bodies and their motions, which are themselves originated. Hence, this sense also cannot apply to the eternal Creator, as it presupposes creation. Thus, Allah is absolutely beyond time.

Key Terms
القدم (al-qidam)
الزمان (al-zaman)
الأزل (al-azal)
التجدد (al-tajaddud)
المحدث (al-muhdath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qidam for Allah means 'existing for a very long time' (temporal antiquity).
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that temporal antiquity is impossible for Allah; qidam for Allah means not preceded by non-existence, not temporal duration.

Checkpoint
What is the first meaning of qidam given by Sanusi, and why is it impossible for Allah?
A) Existing without beginning; impossible because Allah has a beginning
B) Existing for a long time with times passing over it; impossible because Allah's existence is not temporal
C) Being prior to all things; impossible because that would make Allah temporal
D) Being self-sufficient; impossible because it implies dependence

Key Takeaway
Allah's existence is beyond time, and qidam for Allah means without beginning, not ancient.

عندما تذنب، هل تعتمد على مرور الوقت ليمحوه، كأن الزمان له قدرة على المغفرة؟ كيف يفضح هذا الدرس ذلك الوهم؟

Qidam as a Negative Attribute
Sanusi defines qidam as a negative attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that qidam (past-eternity) is defined as a negative attribute, meaning 'not preceded by non-existence,' and why the verifiers among later scholars chose this definition.

Core Idea
Qidam is not a positive quality but the negation of a beginning.

Sanusi clarifies the nature of qidam, one of God's essential attributes. Rather than being a positive, entitative attribute, qidam is a negative attribute: it simply means that God's existence is not preceded by non-existence. This brief but precise definition sets the stage for understanding the Ash'ari approach to divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the key phrase 'غير مسبوق بعدم'.
The passage is very concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the definitional structure
Sanusi interprets 'al-qadīm' as 'not preceded by non-existence.' Recognize that this is a definition by negation.
Compare with positive definitions you may have encountered.
3
Discuss the significance of 'negative attribute'
Reflect on why the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) among later scholars chose to view qidam as a negative attribute. How does this avoid implying multiplicity in God?
This connects to the Ash'ari principle of tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله في تفسير القديم : ( أي : غير مسبوق بعدم ) تنبيه منه على أنَّ المختار في القدم أنَّهُ صفةٌ سلبيةٌ ، وقد اختاره المحققونَ مِنَ المتأخرين .
His saying in the interpretation of al-qadīm: (i.e., not preceded by non-existence) is a warning from him that the chosen view regarding qidam (past-eternity) is that it is a negative (salbiyya) attribute, and the verifiers among the later scholars have chosen it.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This is a brief definitional passage. Sanusi explains that the interpretation of 'al-qadīm' (the Eternal, as an attribute of God) is 'not preceded by non-existence' (ghayr masbūq bi-ʿadam). He points out that this indicates that the preferred position (al-mukhtār) concerning qidam (past-eternity) is that it is a negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya)—i.e., it does not affirm a positive quality but merely negates the absence of existence before it. He adds that this view was chosen by the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) among the later scholars (mutaʾakhirūn). This is consistent with the Ashʿarī position that attributes of negation (such as qidam, baqāʾ, etc.) are not positive entitative attributes but rather negations of their opposites.

Key Terms
قديم
قدم
صفة سلبية
غير مسبوق بعدم
محققون
متأخرون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think qidam is a positive attribute like existence, affirming a quality of being without beginning.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that qidam is a negative attribute: it only negates being preceded by non-existence, not a positive quality.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi define the attribute of qidam (past-eternity)?
A) As a positive attribute that affirms eternal existence.
B) As 'not preceded by non-existence,' which is a negative attribute.
C) As the absence of a beginning in time.
D) As an attribute that is neither negative nor positive.

Key Takeaway
Qidam (past-eternity) is a negative attribute meaning 'not preceded by non-existence,' chosen by the verifiers among later scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's eternity—that He was never preceded by non-existence—do you truly feel the weight of your own contingency? In what specific area of your life do you most need to remember that you had a beginning and He did not?
?
If qidam is a negative attribute that simply denies a deficiency, what does that say about your own tendency to define yourself by what you are not? Are you more focused on avoiding wrong beliefs than on affirming positive truths about God?
?
Consider a moment when you felt self-sufficient or independent. How does the truth that only God is eternal (not preceded by non-existence) expose the illusion of your self-sufficiency? What concrete step can you take today to live more dependently on the Eternal One?

4 min
The Three Opinions and the Chosen Negative View
The commentator states the three opinions on pre-eternity and adopts the negative view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three opinions on pre-eternity and everlastingness, and why the negative view is chosen.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity is simply 'not preceded by non-existence'—a negative attribute.

The commentator begins by framing the debate: are pre-eternity and everlastingness negative, self-existent, or positive attributes? He then immediately presents the chosen view—that they are negative—and notes that the later verifiers have adopted it. This lesson introduces the classification and the first option.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the commentator poses the question and then states the chosen view.
The opening question sets the structure for the entire discussion.
2
Understand the three categories
Identify the three types of attributes mentioned: salbiyya (negative), nafsiyya (self-existent), and wujūdiyya (positive). These are the key terms.
The negative view defines pre-eternity as simply 'not preceded by non-existence'.
3
Focus on the chosen view
Note that the commentator explicitly says the chosen view is the negative one, and that the 'verifiers among the later scholars' have adopted it. This indicates the preferred position in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدمُ والبقاء : هل هما صفتانِ سلبيتان أو نفسيتان أو وجوديتان ؟ ] قوله في تفسير القديم : ( أي : غير مسبوق بعدم ) تنبيه منه على أنَّ المختار في القدم أنَّهُ صفةٌ سلبيةٌ ، وقد اختاره المحققونَ مِنَ المتأخرين . وقيل : هو صفةٌ نفسيَّةٌ ؛ أي: ليس بزائد على الذاتِ ومرجعه إلى الوجود المستمر أزلاً . ورُدَّ : بأنه لو كان نفسياً للوجودِ لَمَا عَريَ عنه وجود ، كيف والجوهر في أوَّلِ أزمنة وجودِهِ لا يتّصفُ بالقدم ، وإنَّما يطرأ عليه بعد ذلك إذا توالت على وجودِهِ الأزمنة ، والصفةُ النفسيَّة لا تكونُ طارئة ؟! وقيل : هو صفةُ معنى ؛ أي : صفةٌ وجودية زائدة على الذاتِ ؛ كالعلم والقدرة ونحوهما مِنْ صفاتِ المعاني ورُدَّ : بأنَّهُ يلزمُ أنْ يكونَ هذا القدم الموجود في حقه تعالى قديماً ؛ لاستحالة اتصافه تعالى بالحوادثِ ، ولأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ وجودٌ في الأزلِ عارياً عن وصف القدم ، ويجب أن يكون بقدم موجودِ زائد على ذلك القدم ، قائم به ، وإلا لزم نقضُ الدليل ، ثم ننقل الكلامَ إلى قدم القدم ، فيلزمُ فيه مثلُ ما لزمَ في الأَوَّلِ ، ثم كذلك ، ويلزم التسلسل ، وقيام المعنى بالمعنى وهذه الأقوال الثلاثةُ مُقرَّرةً أيضاً في صفةِ البقاء : فقيل : هو نفسي ؛ أي : عبارةٌ عنِ الوجودِ المستمر فيما لا يزالُ . وقيل : صفةُ معنى ؛ أي: موجود زائد على وجودِ الذاتِ ؛ كالعلم ونحوه .
Pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqāʾ): Are they two negative attributes (ṣifatān salbiyyatān), or self-existent (nafsiyyatān), or positive (wujūdiyyatān)? His statement in the interpretation of 'pre-eternal' (qadīm): 'i.e., not preceded by non-existence'—a reminder from him that the chosen view concerning pre-eternity is that it is a negative attribute, and the verifiers among the later scholars have chosen it. And it was said: it is a self-existent (nafsiyyah) attribute, i.e., not additional to the essence; and its reference is to existence that is continuous from pre-eternity. And it was refuted: if it were self-existent to existence, no existence would be devoid of it. How [can that be], when the substance (jawhar) in the first moment of its existence is not described by pre-eternity; rather, it occurs to it after that when times elapse over its existence, and a self-existent attribute cannot be incidental?! And it was said: it is an attribute of meaning (ṣifat maʿnā), i.e., a positive attribute (ṣifat wujūdiyyah) additional to the essence, like knowledge, power, and similar attributes of meaning. And it was refuted: that it would necessitate that this pre-eternity existing in His—exalted—right be pre-eternal; because it is impossible for Him—exalted—to be described with contingent accidents (ḥawādith), and because an existence in pre-eternity that is devoid of the description of pre-eternity is inconceivable, and it must be [characterized] by a pre-eternity that is an existent additional to that pre-eternity, subsisting by it; otherwise it would entail a violation of the proof. Then we transfer the discussion to the pre-eternity of pre-eternity, and there would follow similar [problems] as followed in the first, and so on, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul) and the subsistence of an attribute by an attribute. And these three opinions are also established concerning the attribute of everlastingness (baqāʾ). For it was said: it is self-existent, i.e., an expression of existence that is continuous in that which never ceases (fīmā lā yazāl). And it was said: it is an attribute of meaning, i.e., an existent additional to the existence of the essence, like knowledge and the like.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the opinions on the attributes of pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqāʾ). The commentator first states the question: are they negative, self-existent, or positive attributes? He then presents the chosen view—that pre-eternity is a negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya), meaning simply 'not preceded by non-existence'—and notes that the later verifiers have adopted it. Next, he presents the second view: pre-eternity as a self-existent attribute (nafsiyya), equivalent to the continuous existence from pre-eternity. He refutes this by noting that if it were self-existent, no existence would lack it, yet contingent substances (jawāhir) do not possess pre-eternity at the first moment of their existence; it occurs later, which contradicts the nature of a self-existent attribute. Then he presents the third view: pre-eternity as a positive attribute (ṣifat maʿnā) additional to the essence, like knowledge. The refutation shows that this leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul), because the pre-eternity itself would need to be pre-eternal, requiring another pre-eternity, and so on, and would entail the subsistence of an attribute by another attribute. Finally, he notes that the same three opinions apply to the attribute of everlastingness (baqāʾ), with analogous statements and refutations.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
بقاء (baqāʾ)
صفة سلبية (ṣifa salbiyya)
صفة نفسية (ṣifa nafsiyya)
صفة وجودية (ṣifa wujūdiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which view on pre-eternity have the 'verifiers among the later scholars' chosen?
A) The self-existent (nafsiyyah) view
B) The positive (wujūdiyyah) view
C) The negative (salbiyyah) view
D) The view that pre-eternity is an accident

Key Takeaway
The chosen view in Ash'ari theology is that pre-eternity and everlastingness are negative attributes, meaning 'not preceded by non-existence' and 'not followed by non-existence'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that Allah is 'pre-eternal,' do you truly understand it as a negation of any beginning, or do you unconsciously imagine a positive quality of 'being old'? How can you purify your heart from any mental image that limits Allah's eternity?
?
This lesson teaches that pre-eternity is not a quality added to Allah's essence. Are you comfortable with a theology that describes Allah more by what He is not than by what He is? Does this challenge your desire to 'know' Allah in a positive, human-like manner?
?
If pre-eternity is simply denying a beginning, then your entire relationship with the Eternal relies on what you negate about Him. Is your faith built on affirming Allah's absolute uniqueness (tanzīh), or are you still clinging to imagined likenesses? What step will you take today to deepen your tanzīh?

5 min
The Self-Existent View and Its Refutation
The commentator presents the self-existent view on pre-eternity and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the self-existent (nafsiyyah) view on pre-eternity and the refutation based on the nature of contingent substances.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity cannot be self-existent because then all existents would have it, but substances acquire it only over time.

The second opinion holds that pre-eternity is a self-existent attribute, meaning it is not additional to the essence but equivalent to continuous existence from pre-eternity. The commentator refutes this by arguing that if it were self-existent, it would be inseparable from existence itself, yet contingent substances (jawāhir) lack pre-eternity at the first moment of their existence. Let's examine this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic for the self-existent view and its refutation, then the translation. Pay attention to the conditional argument.
The refutation uses a counterexample from contingent substances.
2
Understand the self-existent view
Grasp the position: pre-eternity is not a distinct attribute but the same as 'existence continuous from pre-eternity' (al-wujūd al-mustamir azalan). It is not additional to the essence.
This view attempts to avoid multiplying attributes.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation says: if pre-eternity were self-existent, then anything that exists would necessarily have it. But a substance (jawhar) in its first moment of existence does not have pre-eternity; it acquires it only after time passes. Since a self-existent attribute cannot be incidental, the view fails.
The key point: a self-existent attribute must be inherent, not acquired later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدمُ والبقاء : هل هما صفتانِ سلبيتان أو نفسيتان أو وجوديتان ؟ ] قوله في تفسير القديم : ( أي : غير مسبوق بعدم ) تنبيه منه على أنَّ المختار في القدم أنَّهُ صفةٌ سلبيةٌ ، وقد اختاره المحققونَ مِنَ المتأخرين . وقيل : هو صفةٌ نفسيَّةٌ ؛ أي: ليس بزائد على الذاتِ ومرجعه إلى الوجود المستمر أزلاً . ورُدَّ : بأنه لو كان نفسياً للوجودِ لَمَا عَريَ عنه وجود ، كيف والجوهر في أوَّلِ أزمنة وجودِهِ لا يتّصفُ بالقدم ، وإنَّما يطرأ عليه بعد ذلك إذا توالت على وجودِهِ الأزمنة ، والصفةُ النفسيَّة لا تكونُ طارئة ؟! وقيل : هو صفةُ معنى ؛ أي : صفةٌ وجودية زائدة على الذاتِ ؛ كالعلم والقدرة ونحوهما مِنْ صفاتِ المعاني ورُدَّ : بأنَّهُ يلزمُ أنْ يكونَ هذا القدم الموجود في حقه تعالى قديماً ؛ لاستحالة اتصافه تعالى بالحوادثِ ، ولأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ وجودٌ في الأزلِ عارياً عن وصف القدم ، ويجب أن يكون بقدم موجودِ زائد على ذلك القدم ، قائم به ، وإلا لزم نقضُ الدليل ، ثم ننقل الكلامَ إلى قدم القدم ، فيلزمُ فيه مثلُ ما لزمَ في الأَوَّلِ ، ثم كذلك ، ويلزم التسلسل ، وقيام المعنى بالمعنى وهذه الأقوال الثلاثةُ مُقرَّرةً أيضاً في صفةِ البقاء : فقيل : هو نفسي ؛ أي : عبارةٌ عنِ الوجودِ المستمر فيما لا يزالُ . وقيل : صفةُ معنى ؛ أي: موجود زائد على وجودِ الذاتِ ؛ كالعلم ونحوه .

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'self-existent' means the attribute is identical to the essence, so it cannot be denied to anything that exists.
Correction: The refutation shows that if pre-eternity were self-existent, it would belong to all existents, but contingent substances lack it initially. Thus the view is false.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the self-existent view on pre-eternity?
A) Because it implies pre-eternity is an accident
B) Because substances have pre-eternity at their first moment
C) Because substances acquire pre-eternity only after time, contradicting the nature of a self-existent attribute
D) Because it leads to infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The self-existent view fails because pre-eternity would then be intrinsic to all existents, yet contingent things lack it initially.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat God's attribute of pre-eternity as a mere theological concept, or does it transform how you see your own temporary existence? In what specific ways are you living as if you are permanent, and how would acknowledging your contingency change your priorities today?
?
The refutation shows that pre-eternity cannot be acquired over time—it is intrinsic only to God. Have you ever assumed that you could 'earn' closeness to God through mere accumulation of actions or time, rather than seeing all good as dependent on His eternal grace? When will you repent of that assumption?
?
If a self-existent attribute cannot be incidental, then God's attributes are not added to Him but are His very essence. Does your worship reflect that God is not a being with properties like creatures, but is absolutely simple and eternal? What would it look like to worship Him as such starting this moment?

The Positive View, Infinite Regress, and Extension to Everlastingness
The commentator presents the positive view, refutes it with infinite regress, and extends the discussion to everlastingness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positive (ṣifat maʿnā) view on pre-eternity and how it leads to infinite regress, and how the same opinions apply to everlastingness (baqāʾ).

Core Idea
Treating pre-eternity as a positive attribute forces an infinite regress of pre-eternities.

The third opinion holds that pre-eternity is a positive attribute (ṣifat maʿnā), existing as a distinct entity added to the essence, like knowledge or power. The commentator refutes this by showing that such an attribute would itself need to be pre-eternal, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) of attributes. He then notes that the same three opinions are also debated for the attribute of everlastingness (baqāʾ).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic for the positive view and its refutation, then the translation. Pay close attention to the chain of reasoning.
This is the longest section and includes the infinite regress argument.
2
Understand the positive view
The view says pre-eternity is an existent attribute (ṣifat maʿnā) additional to the essence, like knowledge or power. It is a real entity.
3
Follow the refutation argument
The refutation argues: if pre-eternity is a positive attribute, then it itself must be pre-eternal (since God cannot be subject to temporal events). But then that pre-eternity would require another pre-eternity to characterize it, leading to an infinite chain.
This also entails the subsistence of an attribute by another attribute, which is problematic.
4
Note the extension to baqā'
The commentator concludes that the same three opinions are also held for everlastingness (baqā'), with similar statements and refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدمُ والبقاء : هل هما صفتانِ سلبيتان أو نفسيتان أو وجوديتان ؟ ] قوله في تفسير القديم : ( أي : غير مسبوق بعدم ) تنبيه منه على أنَّ المختار في القدم أنَّهُ صفةٌ سلبيةٌ ، وقد اختاره المحققونَ مِنَ المتأخرين . وقيل : هو صفةٌ نفسيَّةٌ ؛ أي: ليس بزائد على الذاتِ ومرجعه إلى الوجود المستمر أزلاً . ورُدَّ : بأنه لو كان نفسياً للوجودِ لَمَا عَريَ عنه وجود ، كيف والجوهر في أوَّلِ أزمنة وجودِهِ لا يتّصفُ بالقدم ، وإنَّما يطرأ عليه بعد ذلك إذا توالت على وجودِهِ الأزمنة ، والصفةُ النفسيَّة لا تكونُ طارئة ؟! وقيل : هو صفةُ معنى ؛ أي : صفةٌ وجودية زائدة على الذاتِ ؛ كالعلم والقدرة ونحوهما مِنْ صفاتِ المعاني ورُدَّ : بأنَّهُ يلزمُ أنْ يكونَ هذا القدم الموجود في حقه تعالى قديماً ؛ لاستحالة اتصافه تعالى بالحوادثِ ، ولأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ وجودٌ في الأزلِ عارياً عن وصف القدم ، ويجب أن يكون بقدم موجودِ زائد على ذلك القدم ، قائم به ، وإلا لزم نقضُ الدليل ، ثم ننقل الكلامَ إلى قدم القدم ، فيلزمُ فيه مثلُ ما لزمَ في الأَوَّلِ ، ثم كذلك ، ويلزم التسلسل ، وقيام المعنى بالمعنى وهذه الأقوال الثلاثةُ مُقرَّرةً أيضاً في صفةِ البقاء : فقيل : هو نفسي ؛ أي : عبارةٌ عنِ الوجودِ المستمر فيما لا يزالُ . وقيل : صفةُ معنى ؛ أي: موجود زائد على وجودِ الذاتِ ؛ كالعلم ونحوه .

Key Terms
صفة معنى (ṣifat maʿnā)

حادث (ḥādith)
بقاء (baqāʾ)
قيام المعنى بالمعنى (qiyām al-maʿnā bi al-maʿnā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What problem arises from the view that pre-eternity is a positive attribute (ṣifat maʿnā)?
A) It contradicts the oneness of God
B) It makes pre-eternity an accident
C) It leads to an infinite regress of pre-eternities
D) It implies pre-eternity is a substance

Key Takeaway
The positive view on pre-eternity leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul) and is therefore invalid; the same logic applies to everlastingness.

?
When you reflect on God's attribute of pre-eternity, do you subconsciously treat it as an 'extra' quality that He has, almost like an item on a list? How does the logical breakdown in this lesson reveal the danger of such a compartmentalized view, and what can you do to ensure your understanding of tawheed remains pure and unified?
?
The refutation shows that adding pre-eternity as a separate attribute leads to an infinite regress—an absurdity. In your own life, are there areas where you add 'extra' layers to your faith—practices or beliefs that are not grounded in revelation—that might lead to spiritual absurdity? What is one specific step you will take this week to strip away an unnecessary addition and return to the core of Islam?

The First View: Qidam as a Self-State Attribute
Sanusi presents and refutes the first view on qidam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qidam cannot be a self-state (nafsiyyah) attribute that is not additional to the essence.

Core Idea
If qidam were identical to existence, all existence would be inherently eternal—but it's not.

Sanusi begins his discussion of pre-eternity by presenting a mistaken view: that qidam is merely a self-state attribute, meaning it is not something extra added to the essence. He then refutes this view with a logical argument and an example from created substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. The passage states the view and then gives the refutation.
Notice the structure: 'قيل' (it was said) then 'ورد' (and it was refuted).
2
Understand the view
The view claims qidam is a self-state (nafsiyyah) attribute, meaning it is not additional to the essence but rather refers to continuous existence.
This implies qidam is identical to existence itself.
3
Identify the refutation
Sanusi argues that if qidam were identical to existence, then existence would always have it. But a substance in its first moment is not pre-eternal; pre-eternity only comes later, so it cannot be a self-state attribute.
The example of a jawhar (substance) demonstrates this.
4
Reflect on the footnote
The footnote clarifies that the nafsi attribute is conceptually distinct from the essence, even if not externally additional.
This prevents misunderstanding that the nafsi attribute is exactly the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : هو صفةٌ نفسيَّةٌ ; أي: ليس بزائد على الذاتِ(۱) ومرجعه إلى الوجود المستمر أزلاً . ورُدَّ : بأنه لو كان نفسياً للوجودِ لَمَا عَريَ عنه وجود ، كيف والجوهر في أوَّلِ أزمنة وجودِهِ لا يتّصفُ بالقدم ، وإنَّما يطرأ عليه بعد ذلك إذا توالت على وجودِهِ الأزمنة ، والصفةُ النفسيَّة لا تكونُ طارئة ؟! وقيل : هو صفةُ معنى ؛ أي : صفةٌ وجودية زائدة على الذاتِ ; كالعلم والقدرة ونحوهما مِنْ صفاتِ المعاني ورُدَّ : بأنَّهُ يلزمُ أنْ يكونَ هذا القدم الموجود في حقه تعالى قديماً ; لاستحالة اتصافه تعالى بالحوادثِ ، ولأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ وجودٌ في الأزلِ عارياً عن وصف القدم ، ويجب أن يكون بقدم موجودِ زائد على ذلك القدم ، قائم به ، وإلا لزم نقضُ الدليل ، ثم ننقل الكلامَ إلى قدم القدم ، فيلزمُ فيه مثلُ ما لزمَ في الأَوَّلِ ، ثم كذلك ، ويلزم التسلسل ، وقيام المعنى بالمعنى . (۱) أي : لا تحقق له خارجاً حال كونه زائداً على الذات ، فلا ينافي أنه مغاير للذات في المفهوم ، وظاهر الشارح : أنه عين الذات ، وليس بمراد ؛ لأنه يجب مغايرة الصفة للموصوف . ( حامدي » ( ص ١٥٠ )
And it was said: It is a self-state (nafsiyyah) attribute, meaning: not additional to the essence (dhat). And its reference is to continuous existence eternally (azalan). And it was refuted: Because if it were self-state for existence, existence would never lack it. How is it that a substance (jawhar) in the first moment of its existence is not described by pre-eternity (qidam), but rather it occurs to it after that when the times (azminah) follow upon its existence? And the self-state attribute is not supposed to be accidental (tari'ah)?! And it was said: It is an attribute of meaning (ma'na), i.e., an existential attribute additional to the essence, like knowledge, power, and similar attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani). And it was refuted: That it necessarily follows that this pre-eternity (qadam) existing in His, may He be exalted, right is itself pre-eternal; because it is impossible for Allah to be described with originated things (hawadith), and because no existence in pre-eternity (al-azal) can be conceived devoid of the description of pre-eternity. And there must be a pre-eternity of an existence additional to that pre-eternity, subsisting in it; otherwise the proof would be broken. Then we transfer the discussion to the pre-eternity of pre-eternity, and the same that was necessary in the first occurs, then likewise, and infinite regress (tasalsul) becomes necessary, and the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning. (1) i.e., it has no realization externally while being additional to the essence, so it does not contradict that it is different from the essence in concept, and the apparent meaning of the commentator is that it is identical to the essence, but that is not intended; because the attribute must be distinct from the described. (Hamidi, p. 150).
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes two views on the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam). First view: qidam is a nafsi attribute, meaning it is not additional to the essence but rather is the continuous existence itself. Refutation: If it were identical to existence, then existence would never be without it, but a substance (jawhar) in its first moment of existence is not described as pre-eternal; pre-eternity only comes later after time passes, yet a nafsi attribute should be inseparable. So this view is inconsistent. Second view: qidam is an attribute of meaning (ma'na), i.e., an additional existential attribute like knowledge and power. Refutation: This would require that this pre-eternity in God is itself pre-eternal, because God cannot be described with originated things, and every existence in pre-eternity must be described as pre-eternal. Then we would need a pre-eternity for that pre-eternity, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul) and the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning, which is impossible. The footnote clarifies that the nafsi attribute is not realized as additional externally, but it is conceptually distinct.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
صفة نفسية (sifah nafsiyyah)
جوهر (jawhar)
ذات (dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The nafsi attribute is exactly identical to the essence in every respect.
Correction: The footnote clarifies that while it is not an additional external entity, it is still conceptually distinct from the essence.

Checkpoint
What example does Sanusi use to refute the first view?
A) The attribute of knowledge
B) A substance (jawhar) in its first moment of existence
C) The attribute of power
D) The essence of God

Key Takeaway
Qidam cannot be merely a self-state attribute because it would have to be present in all existence at all times, but created substances acquire pre-eternity only after time passes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If pre-eternity were merely a description of existence, then everything that exists would be eternal. Yet you and everything around you are clearly temporal. Does your daily life reflect a deep awareness that you are not self-sufficient or eternal? In what specific ways do you live as if you are the permanent owner of your time, health, or reputation?
?
The refutation shows that pre-eternity cannot be acquired after time passes; it either is or isn't. When you consider your own deeds, do you treat some of them as if they have always been good or bad, forgetting that your actions are evaluated by an eternal standard? What habit are you currently justifying by saying 'this is just who I am' instead of acknowledging it is a temporal state you can change?
?
God's pre-eternity is unique and not a mere attribute of existence. Does your heart rest in the certainty of His eternal existence, or do you find yourself seeking permanence in created things that fade? What created relationship or possession do you need to release today to better align your heart with the Eternal One?

The Second View: Qidam as an Attribute of Meaning
Sanusi presents and refutes the second view on qidam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qidam cannot be an additional attribute of meaning (sifah ma'na) without leading to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Claiming qidam is an extra attribute forces an infinite chain of pre-eternities.

The second view treats qidam as a real attribute added to the essence, similar to knowledge or power. Sanusi shows this leads to an impossible infinite regress and the absurdity of a meaning subsisting in another meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation for the second view and refutation.
Notice the logical chain Sanusi builds.
2
Understand the second view
This view sees qidam as an existential attribute additional to the essence, like other attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani).
This would mean pre-eternity is a real quality.
3
Follow the refutation
Sanusi argues that if qidam is an additional attribute, then it itself must be pre-eternal (since God cannot have originated attributes). Then we need another pre-eternity for that pre-eternity, leading to infinite regress.
This is the problem of tasalsul.
4
Recognize the impossibility
Infinite regress is impossible in divine attributes. Additionally, it leads to the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning, which is absurd.
The chain cannot stop.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Three Views on Everlastingness (Baqā') and the Verified Position
Sanusi applies the same three views to baqa' and affirms the third as correct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three theological views on the divine attribute of everlastingness (baqā') and why the verified position is that it is a negation of non-existence.

Core Idea
Everlastingness (baqā') is not an extra existence but the negation of annihilation.

In this passage, Sanusi extends the same three views discussed for pre-eternity (qidam) to the attribute of everlastingness (baqā'). He presents each view—self-state, additional meaning, and negation—and then affirms the negation view as the verified position. This lesson will help you see the consistent methodology in Islamic theology for understanding eternal attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to get a clear sense of Sanusi’s argument.
Notice how the passage begins: 'وهذه الأقوال الثلاثةُ مُقرَّرةً أيضاً في صفةِ البقاء'.
2
Identify the three views
List the three views presented: nafsi, ma'na, and salb. Pay attention to how each is defined briefly by Sanusi.
3
Note which view is called the verified position
Identify which of the three views Sanusi labels as 'التحقيق' (the verified). Read the sentence carefully.
The passage says: 'وهو التحقيق أيضاً'.
4
Reflect on the objection statement
Observe that Sanusi says the objections against the first two views are exactly the same as those for qidam. Ask yourself why he does not repeat them.
This shows the consistency of the theological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه الأقوال الثلاثةُ مُقرَّرةً أيضاً في صفةِ البقاء : فقيل : هو نفسي ; أي : عبارةٌ عنِ الوجودِ المستمر فيما لا يزالُ . وقيل : صفةُ معنى ; أي: موجود زائد على وجودِ الذاتِ ; كالعلم ونحوه . وقيل : صفة سلب ; أي : عبارة عن نفي العدم اللاحق بعد الوجود، وهو التحقيق أيضاً ، والاعتراض على الأوَّلين كالاعتراض عليهما في صفة القدم سواء بسواء
And these three statements are also established in the attribute of everlastingness (baqa'): For it was said: It is self-state (nafsi), i.e., an expression for the continuous existence in that which will not cease (ma la yazalu). And it was said: It is an attribute of meaning (ma'na), i.e., an existence additional to the existence of the essence, like knowledge and the like. And it was said: It is an attribute of negation (salb), i.e., an expression for the negation of subsequent non-existence after existence, and it is the verified (tahqiq) position as well. And the objection against the first two is exactly the same as the objection against them in the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam), without difference.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage extends the same three views discussed for pre-eternity to the attribute of everlastingness (baqa'). First view: baqa' is a nafsi attribute, meaning the continuous existence in the future without end. Second view: baqa' is an attribute of meaning, an additional existence like knowledge. Third view: baqa' is an attribute of negation (salb), meaning the negation of non-existence after existence. The author states that this third view is the verified (tahqiq) position. He also notes that the objections against the first two views are exactly the same as those given for pre-eternity, so they are not repeated.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqa')
صفة سلب (sifah salb)
نفي العدم (nafy al-'adam)
تحقيق (tahqiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the three views are distinct novel arguments, but they are exactly the same as those for qidam.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the objections against the first two views are the same as for qidam, so the reasoning is identical.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the verified (tahqiq) view on the attribute of everlastingness (baqa')?
A) It is a self-state attribute (nafsi), expressing continuous existence.
B) It is an additional existential attribute (ma'na), like knowledge.
C) It is a negation (salb) of subsequent non-existence after existence.
D) It is identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
The attribute of everlastingness (baqā') is best understood as the negation of non-existence after existence, not as an additional existential attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's everlastingness is simply the impossibility of His non-existence, why do you still fear that He might abandon you or let you down? What does that fear reveal about the state of your tawakkul?
?
The verified view says baqā' is a negation of subsequent non-existence. Do you live each day as though God's existence is just as certain as your own mortality? What would change if you truly internalized that He can never cease to exist?
?
Since baqā' is not an extra attribute but a statement about what God is not, what temporary things are you clinging to for security? Will you let go of one such thing today and anchor your hope in God's eternal nature instead?

The Definition of Baqa' as a Negative Attribute
Sanusi states the chosen view of baqa' as a negative attribute and defends it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the chosen view in kalam defines baqa' as a negative attribute, meaning the negation of non-existence after existence, and how this view is defended by analogy with the attribute of eternity.

Core Idea
Baqa' is not a positive quality but the denial of annihilation.

In this lesson, we examine a specific attribute of Allah: baqa' (everlastingness). Theologians debated whether it is a positive attribute or a negative one. Sanusi presents the view that it is negative, meaning it simply negates the possibility of non-existence after existence. This view is consistent with the treatment of the attribute of eternity (qidam). Let's read the passage and follow the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic and English translation to get the gist. Notice that the passage presents a definition and a comparison.
The passage is concise; each word matters.
2
Identify the two views being mentioned
The author refers to 'the first two' views that are objected to. Think about what these might be (from context, probably positive attribute or other definitions). Then note that the same objection applies to them as in the case of qidam.
This shows the consistency in Kalam methodology.
3
Grasp the core meaning of baqa' as a negative attribute
Understand that baqa' is not a quality that adds something to Allah's essence, but simply the denial that He can cease to exist. This is a crucial distinction in kalam.
This aligns with the principle that attributes are either positive or negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : صفة سلب ; أي : عبارة عن نفي العدم اللاحق بعد الوجود، وهو التحقيق أيضاً ، والاعتراض على الأوَّلين كالاعتراض عليهما في صفة القدم سواء بسواء
And it is said: a negative attribute, that is: an expression for the negation of non-existence that follows after existence, and it is the verified (view) as well. And the objection to the first two is like the objection to them in the attribute of eternity, exactly the same.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of the attribute of baqa' (eternal duration/everlastingness) as a negative attribute (sifah salb). The author states that the chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that baqa' is a negative attribute, meaning it is an expression for the negation of non-existence after existence. That is, it does not posit a positive quality but simply denies that non-existent can occur after existence. The author asserts that this is the verified (tahqiq) view. He then notes that the objection raised against the first two views (presumably other definitions of baqa') is exactly the same as the objection to the two views regarding the attribute of qidam (eternity). This establishes consistency in the critique of alternative definitions.

Key Terms
صفة سلب
بقاء
عدم
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqa' is a positive attribute that adds a quality to Allah.
Correction: The chosen view is that baqa' is a negative attribute; it simply negates the possibility of non-existence after existence, affirming that Allah's existence is permanent.

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view in this passage, what is baqa'?
A) A positive attribute that adds existence
B) A negative attribute meaning the negation of non-existence after existence
C) The same as qidam
D) An attribute that is neither positive nor negative

Key Takeaway
The attribute of baqa' (everlastingness) is defined as the negation of non-existence after existence, making it a negative attribute consistent with the treatment of qidam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's everlastingness is merely the negation of His non-existence, do you truly rely on His eternal promises as much as you fear temporary losses?
?
How does the fact that Allah cannot cease to exist affect your hope for forgiveness and reward, especially when your sins feel overwhelming?
?
Knowing that Baqa' is a negative attribute—denying annihilation—does your heart find more security in Allah's permanence than in your own fleeting achievements?

6 min
The Proof for the Necessity of Baqa'
Sanusi proves that Allah must have the attribute of baqa' and refutes the possibility of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that Allah must possess the attribute of baqa' (everlastingness) and why the possibility of non-existence is impossible for Him.

Core Idea
If Allah could cease to exist, He would be contingent—contradicting His necessary eternity.

Sanusi now turns to the attribute of baqa' (everlastingness). He provides a logical demonstration that Allah's existence cannot be followed by non-existence. In this passage, you will see how the same rational necessity that proves Allah's eternity also proves His permanence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the forceful rhetorical style: 'How, when His eternity has just passed?'
2
Identify the key claim and conditional
The claim is that Allah must be baqi (everlasting). The argument begins with a conditional: if non-existence could affect Him, then His essence would accept both existence and non-existence.
The conditional leads to the conclusion that He would need a particularizer (mukhassis), making Him originated (hadith).
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the chain: possibility of non-existence → essence receptive to both → need for external preferentiator → contingency → contradiction with proven eternity.
This chain rests on the principle that anything accepting opposites needs a cause to tip the balance.
4
Connect to the broader framework
Reflect on how this proof ties into the earlier proof of qidam (eternity) and the overall kalam method of proving divine attributes through rational necessity.
Sanusi derives a general rule: 'everything whose eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : ويجب أن يكون باقيا ، أي : لا يلحق وجوده عدم ، وإلا لكانت ذاته تقبلهما ، فيحتاج في ترجيح وجوده إلى مخصص ، فيكون حادثا ، كيف وقد مر آنفا وجوب قدمه ؟! ومن هنا تعلم برهان أن كل ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه بالبرهان · قد بينا قبل أن المختار في ( البقاء ) : أنه عبارة عن سلب العدم اللاحق للوجود والدليل على وجوب هذه الصفة له جل وعلا : أنه لو قدر لحوق العدم له تعالى عن ذلك علوا كبيرا .. كانت ذاته العلية تقبل الوجود والعدم ; لفرض اتصافه بهما ، ولا تتصف ذاته بصفة حتى تقبلها لكن قبوله جل وعلا للعدم محال ; إذ لو قبله لكان هو والوجود بالنسبة إلى ذاته سيين ; إذ القبول للذات نفسي لا يختلف ، فيلزم افتقار وجوده إلى موجد يربحه على العدم الجائز ، فيكون حادثا
Then we say: And He must be everlasting, i.e., non-existence does not affect His existence, otherwise His essence would accept both [existence and non-existence], so He would need a particularizer to prefer His existence, so He would be originated. How, when His eternity has just passed? And from this you learn the proof that everything whose eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible by proof. We have stated before that the chosen view regarding baqa' is that it is an expression for the negation of non-existence that follows existence. And the evidence for the necessity of this attribute for Him, exalted and high, is that if non-existence were supposed to attach to Him—exalted is He above that with great elevation—then His noble essence would accept existence and non-existence, because of the assumption of His being described by both. But His essence is not described by an attribute until it accepts it. However, His acceptance of non-existence is impossible, because if He accepted it, then it and existence would be equal with respect to His essence, since acceptance is essential to the essence and does not vary. So it would necessitate the dependence of His existence on a creator who preferentiates it over the possible non-existence, so He would be originated.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of the attribute of baqa' (everlastingness) for Allah. The author begins by stating that Allah must be everlasting, meaning non-existence does not affect His existence. If non-existence could affect Him, then His essence would be capable of accepting both existence and non-existence, which would require an external particularizer (mukhassis) to prefer one over the other—making Him contingent (hadith). The author then exclaims that this is impossible since His eternity (qidam) has already been proven. From this, a general principle is derived: anything whose eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible. The author reiterates the chosen definition of baqa' as the negation of non-existence after existence. He then provides a detailed logical proof: if non-existence were possible for Allah, then His essence would be receptive to both existence and non-existence, because being described by a state implies receptivity to it. But acceptance of non-existence is impossible for Allah because if He accepted it, existence and non-existence would be equal relative to His essence—since the essential capacity to accept does not differentiate—necessitating a creator to tip the balance toward existence, making Him contingent. Since Allah is necessarily existent and eternal, this is impossible. Thus, baqa' is a necessary attribute.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Allah's everlastingness is proven only by scriptural texts.
Correction: This passage demonstrates that baqa' is proven by rational necessity, not just revelation.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given in the proof that Allah cannot cease to exist?
A) Because He is described as everlasting in the Quran
B) Because if He could cease, He would need a cause to continue, making Him originated
C) Because non-existence is impossible for any being
D) Because the attribute of baqa' is identical to His essence

Key Takeaway
Allah's everlastingness is rationally necessary because the possibility of His non-existence would imply contingency, contradicting His necessary existence.

?

Definition and Concise Proof of God's Permanence
Sanusi defines baqa' and gives a concise proof for its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defines God's attribute of permanence as the negation of non-existence and proves its necessity through a concise argument based on the entailment of pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Permanence is simply the inescapable consequence of pre-eternity.

This lesson presents Sanusi's concise definition and proof of God's attribute of permanence (baqā'). He begins by defining permanence as the negation of subsequent non-existence, then demonstrates that if non-existence were possible, it would imply contingency—contradicting God's already proven pre-eternity. Through this argument, Sanusi establishes a universal rule: whatever is pre-eternal cannot cease to exist.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of baqā'
Read the definition: permanence is the negation of subsequent non-existence. Note that it is a negative attribute (salb).
This definition is the 'chosen view' (al-mukhtār).
2
Follow the proof by contradiction
Assume non-existence could attach to God. Then His essence would accept both existence and non-existence equally, requiring a preponderant to tip the scale—making Him contingent.
This consequence contradicts His already proven pre-eternity.
3
See the contradiction with pre-eternity
The necessity of pre-eternity (wujūb al-qidam) was established earlier. Contingency is incompatible with that, so the assumption must be false.
4
Derive the universal rule
From this, Sanusi concludes that the necessity of pre-eternity entails the necessity of permanence, forming a universal rule: everything proven pre-eternal cannot cease to exist.
This rule applies to God and any truly pre-eternal being.
5
Reflect on the proof's conclusiveness
Sanusi emphasizes that this concise proof is definitive (qaṭ‘ī) with no doubt in any of its premises.
It is a short but airtight argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفة البقاء
فصل
[ في إثباتِ صفةِ البقاء ، والدليل عليها ]
ثم نقولُ : ويجبُ أنْ يكونَ باقياً ، أي : لا يلحق وجودَهُ عدمٌ ، وإلا لكانَتْ ذاتُهُ تقبلهما ، فيحتاجُ في ترجيح وجودِهِ إِلى مُخصص ، فيكونُ حادثاً ، كيف وقد مرَّ آنفاً وجوبُ قدمِهِ ؟! ومِنْ هنا تعلمُ برهانَ أَنَّ كلَّ ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمُهُ

قد بيَّنا قبلُ أنَّ المختارَ في ( البقاء ) : أنَّه عبارةٌ عن سلْبِ العدمِ اللاحق للوجود والدليل على وجوبِ هذهِ الصفةِ لهُ جل وعلا : أَنَّهُ لو قُدِّرَ لحوقُ العدم له تعالى عن ذلك علوّاً كبيراً .. لكانَتْ ذاتُهُ العليَّةُ تقبل الوجود والعدم ؛ لفَرْضِ اتصافِهِ بهما ، ولا تَتَصفُ ذاته بصفة حتى تقبلها لكن قبوله جل وعلا للعدم محالٌ ؛ إذْ لو قبلَهُ لكان هو والوجود بالنسبة إلى ذاتِهِ سِيَّيْنِ ؛ إِذِ القَبولُ للذاتِ نفسي لا يختلفُ ، فيلزمُ افتقار وجودِهِ إِلى مُوجِدٍ يُربِّحُهُ على العدم الجائز ، فيكون حادثاً

وجوب القدم يستلزم وجوب البقاء ]
فبانَ لكَ بهذا البرهانِ : أَنَّ وجوبَ القدم يستلزم أبداً وجوبَ البقاء ، وأنَّ تجويز العدم اللاحقِ يُوجِبُ ثبوت العدم السابقِ ، فخرج لنا بهذا البرهان قاعدة كليةٌ ؛ وهي : أَنَّ كلَّ ما ثبَتَ قدمُهُ استحالَ عدمه ؛ لأنَّ القدم لا يكون أبداً إلا واجباً للقديم . وهذا البرهانُ الذي ذكرنا لوجوبِ البقاء مختصر ، وهو معَ اختصارِهِ قطعي لا شبهة في شيءٍ مِنْ مُقدِّماتِه
The attribute of permanence. Chapter [On proving the attribute of permanence and the proof for it]. Then we say: He must be everlasting, i.e., non-existence does not attach to His existence. Otherwise, His essence would accept both [existence and non-existence], so it would need a preponderant to preponderate its existence, so it would be originated. How? When the necessity of His pre-eternity has just passed? And from here you learn the proof that everything whose pre-eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible. We have previously explained that the chosen view concerning (permanence) is that it is an expression of the negation of subsequent non-existence. And the proof for the necessity of this attribute for Him, exalted and high, is that if the attachment of non-existence to Him were supposed—far exalted is He above that—then His supreme essence would accept existence and non-existence, by virtue of assuming it is described by both, and His essence is not described by an attribute until it accepts it. But His acceptance, exalted, of non-existence is impossible; because if He accepted it, then He and existence would be equal with respect to His essence, since acceptance is essential for the essence and does not differ. Then the need of His existence for a bringer into existence that would prefer it over contingent non-existence would follow, so He would be originated. How, when the necessity of His pre-eternity has been established by definitive proof? The necessity of pre-eternity entails the necessity of permanence. So it has become clear to you by this proof that the necessity of pre-eternity always entails the necessity of permanence, and that allowing subsequent non-existence necessitates the affirmation of prior non-existence. So there emerges for us by this proof a universal rule, which is that everything whose pre-eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible, because pre-eternity is never except necessary for the pre-eternal. And this proof that we have mentioned for the necessity of permanence is concise, and despite its conciseness, it is definitive, with no doubt in any of its premises.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a definition and concise proof of God's attribute of permanence (baqā'). It begins with a chapter heading stating the topic: proving the attribute of permanence and its proof. Then Sanusi states that God must be everlasting, meaning non-existence never attaches to His existence. The alternative would mean His essence is capable of both existence and non-existence, requiring an external preponderant, leading to contingency—contradicting His already proven pre-eternity. From this, a general principle is derived: whatever is pre-eternal cannot cease to exist. Sanusi then clarifies that the preferred definition of permanence is the negation of subsequent non-existence. He provides a detailed proof: if non-existence could attach to God, His essence would accept both existence and non-existence equally (since acceptance is essential and uniform), necessitating an external preponderant to tip the balance, which would make Him contingent—impossible given His proven pre-eternity. The conclusion follows: the necessity of pre-eternity entails the necessity of permanence, and allowing later non-existence implies earlier non-existence. This yields a universal rule: whatever is proven pre-eternal cannot cease to exist. Sanusi emphasizes that this concise proof is definitively certain, with no doubt in its premises.

Misconception: Permanence is a positive attribute added to God's essence.
Correction: Sanusi defines permanence as a negative attribute: the negation of subsequent non-existence. It is not a positive quality but rather the impossibility of ceasing to exist.

The Famous Longer Proof for God's Permanence
Sanusi presents the mutakallimun's longer proof for baqa' with its divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the mutakallimun's longer proof for the necessity of God's permanence (baqa') and why all possible determinants of non-existence are eliminated.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot befall God because every possible cause is impossible.

Sanusi now presents the longer, more detailed proof for God's permanence used by the mutakallimun. This proof works by examining every possible way non-existence could occur and showing each is logically impossible. The proof is structured as a division (taqsīm) that systematically eliminates alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument. Notice the repeated terms like 'المقتضي' and the division into cases.
The proof is known as 'the longer proof' because of its detailed divisions.
2
Understand the initial premise
The proof begins by assuming non-existence (العدم) could happen to the pre-eternal (Qadim). For any occurrence, there must be a determinant (مقتض). This is a basic principle of kalam.
The occurrence of something unlikely (مرجوح) without a determinant is impossible.
3
Follow the division of determinant
Sanusi divides the possible determinants into volitional (بالإختيار) and non-volitional (غير بالاختيار). The volitional is dismissed because non-existence is not an act.
4
Examine the non-volitional possibilities
Non-volitional determinants are further divided into absence of a condition (عدم شرط) or occurrence of a contrary (طريان ضد). Each is examined and refuted.
The refutation uses logical arguments to show inconsistency or infinite regress.
5
Conclude the proof
Since all possibilities are impossible, the original assumption that non-existence could occur to the pre-eternal must be false. Therefore, God is necessarily permanent.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليلُ المشهور بينَ المتكلمينَ فيهِ طول وتقسيم لم يُجمع على بطلان أقسامه ؛
جميع
وذلك أنَّهم يقولون : لو طراً العدم على القديم لوجب أن يكونَ لهُ
مقتض ؛ إذْ طرقُ أمرِ لنفسِهِ بغيرِ مقتض ـ لا سيما إنْ كانَ مرجوحاً كهذا - محال ضرورةً ، والمقتضي : إمَّا بالاختيار أو لا ، والمقتضي المختار : لا يفعلُ العدمَ ؛ إذْ ليسَ بفعل ، وغيرُ المختارِ : إِمَّا عدمُ شرط ، أو طريان ضد :
باطل أن يكونَ عدم شرط ؛ لأنَّ ذلك الشرط إن كان قديماً نقلنا الكلام إلى عدمِهِ ، ولزامَ التسلسل ، وإنْ كانَ حادثاً لزم وجود القديم في الأزلِ بدونِ شرطِهِ ، محال
وهو
وباطل أنْ يكونَ طريان ضد ؛ لأنَّهُ إِنْ طراً قبلَ انعدام القديمِ لزمَ اجتماع الضدين ، وإن طراً بعد انعدامه فقد انعدم القديم بغيرِ مقتض
And the famous proof among the mutakallimun has length and division, and it is not agreed upon the falsity of its divisions; all [?]. And that is that they say: If non-existence were to occur to the pre-eternal, it would necessarily have a determinant; because the occurrence of something without a determinant—especially if it is improbable like this—is necessarily impossible. The determinant is either volitional or not volitional. The volitional determinant does not create non-existence, for it is not an act. The non-volitional determinant is either the absence of a condition or the occurrence of a contrary. It is false for it to be the absence of a condition, because if that condition were pre-eternal, we would transfer the discussion to its non-existence, necessitating infinite regress; and if it were originated, then the pre-eternal would exist in pre-eternity without its condition, which is impossible. And it is false for it to be the occurrence of a contrary, because if it occurs before the annihilation of the pre-eternal, the co-existence of contraries would follow; and if it occurs after its annihilation, then the pre-eternal has been annihilated without a determinant.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents the famous longer proof used by the mutakallimun (theologians) for the necessity of God's permanence (baqā'). Sanusi notes that this proof is lengthy and involves division (taqsīm), and it is not universally agreed upon that its divisions are false. The proof proceeds as follows: if non-existence were to happen to the pre-eternal (God), it would require a determinant (muqtadā) because the occurrence of any event without a determinant is impossible, especially an improbable one like the annihilation of God. The determinant is divided into two types: volitional (by choice) or non-volitional. The volitional determinant cannot cause non-existence because non-existence is not an act (fi'l). So the determinant must be non-volitional, which further divides into two possibilities: either the absence of a condition ('adam al-sharṭ) or the occurrence of a contrary (ṭuryān al-ḍidd). The absence of a condition is false: if the condition is pre-eternal, we must explain its own absence, leading to infinite regress; if it is originated, then the pre-eternal would have existed in pre-eternity without that condition, which is impossible. The occurrence of a contrary is also false: if the contrary occurs before the annihilation of the pre-eternal, then two contraries would coexist; if it occurs after annihilation, the pre-eternal has already been annihilated without any determinant, which contradicts the premise. Thus all possibilities are eliminated, proving that non-existence cannot occur to the pre-eternal.

Key Terms
المقتضي
المختار
غير المختار
عدم الشرط
طريان الضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think non-existence could occur by a volitional act, but Sanusi clarifies that non-existence is not an act.
Correction: Non-existence is not an act (فعل), so a volitional agent cannot produce it. Thus the determinant cannot be volitional.

Checkpoint
In the longer proof, why can the determinant not be volitional?
A) Because volitional agents only produce acts
B) Because non-existence is not an act
C) Because God cannot will non-existence
D) Because the determinant must be non-volitional

Key Takeaway
The longer proof systematically eliminates all possible causes for non-existence, establishing that God must be permanently existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's permanence is so certain that every possible cause of non-existence is impossible, then what does it mean for you to rely on anything other than Him for your own stability and security? Aren't you still placing trust in temporary things that can be annihilated at any moment?
?
The proof shows that non-existence cannot be an act (fi'l). When you choose to do things that lead to spiritual 'death' or distance from God, are you not implicitly treating non-existence as something you can bring about? How does this lesson correct your view of your own actions and their effects?
?
The longer proof eliminates all excuses—every possible determinant is ruled out. What 'determinants' do you use to justify your own spiritual negligence or delay in obedience? If no excuse holds for God's permanence, what excuses hold for your own failure to seek Him?

Al-Qadi's View on Annihilation as an Object of Power
Sanusi discusses al-Qadi al-Baqillani's view on whether annihilation is an object of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi al-Baqillani's argument that annihilation can be an object of divine power and the distinction he makes regarding prior non-existence.

Core Idea
Annihilation is a valid act of God because the concept of non-existence is uniform.

This passage presents the view of the prominent Ash'ari theologian al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani on whether the act of bringing about non-existence (i'dam) can be an object of divine power. The passage outlines his position, the logical implication it entails, and a distinction made in response to an objection. However, the text is truncated, so we must infer the full argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the key terms: إعدام, عدم سابق, مؤثر.
The text is truncated; pay attention to the incomplete sentence.
2
Identify al-Qadi's claim
The passage states that annihilation is valid as an object of power. Mark this as the main thesis.
3
Understand the logical implication
Al-Qadi was compelled to accept that prior non-existence must also be attributable to the agent, because the concept of non-existence is the same.
This is a key step in the argument.
4
Note the distinction
A distinction is made: prior non-existence is continuous, while the act of annihilation brings about non-existence after existence. The text cuts off; consider how this distinction might resolve the implication.
The distinction likely argues that continuous non-existence is not an act, so it need not be attributed to power.

And a distinction was made by saying that the prior is continuous, and the continuous dispenses with [?].

The objection is raised that this would require attributing prior non-existence to God, but al-Qadi argues the concept of non-existence is the same. A distinction is then made: prior non-existence is continuous, and the continuous dispenses with... (the text cuts off). The argument likely is that continuous non-existence is not an act, whereas the act of annihilation brings about non-existence after existence.

If God's power is what you rely on for your very existence, and He can also annihilate you in an instant, does your trust in Him truly encompass both the giving and the taking away? Have you prepared for the moment when He exercises that power over you?
?
Al-Qadi argues that non-existence is uniform—whether before creation or after annihilation. Does knowing that God's power is equally at work in both your beginning and your end change how you view the value of your worldly life? What action will you take today to align your priorities with that reality?
?
You believe God is omnipotent, but do you live as if you truly accept that He can bring your blessings to nothing just as easily as He gave them? Identify one blessing you take for granted and ask God to make you grateful, knowing He could remove it at any moment.

Proof Against Divine Composition
Sanusi refutes divine composition via absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God cannot be a composite body and why the claim that a single attribute subsists in the whole is false.

Core Idea
If God had parts, every part would be divine, leading to many gods.

Sanusi now moves from positive attributes to a crucial negative proof: demonstrating that Allah is not a composite body. This argument uses the impossibility of multiple eternal gods to show that composition leads to absurdity. It also refutes a possible counterargument about attributes subsisting in the whole.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the logical flow sink in.
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the hypothetical
The passage starts by supposing God were a body composed of parts. Notice the immediate consequence drawn: each part must possess the divine attributes.
This consequence is justified by two reasons: no other eternal being, and avoiding arbitrary specification.
3
Understand the impossibility
If each part has the attributes, each would be divine, leading to multiple gods—impossible. Then the author refutes the alternative: a single attribute subsisting in the whole composite, which would require dividing the indivisible.
This refutes any attempt to save composition.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Ask yourself why this argument is decisive for Islamic theology. It shows that divine simplicity is not just a postulate but a rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فلو كان جسماً مُركَّباً مِن جزأين فأكثر .. لَلَزِمَ أنْ يقوم بكل جزء منه صفة العلم والقدرة والحياة وسائر صفاتِ الإله ؛ لاستحالة وجودِ قديم غيرِ اللهِ ، ولئلا يلزم الافتقار إلى المخصص في ترجيح بعض الأجزاء بقيام الصفاتِ بها دونَ بعض ، لكن قيام الصفاتِ بكل جزء محال ؛ لأنَّهُ يُوجِبُ تعدُّدَ الآلهة ، وسيأتي برهان وجوب الوحدانية له جلَّ وعلا ، وأمَّا ادّعاءُ أَنَّ الصفة الواحدة تقومُ بالمجموع .. فلا يخفى بطلانه ، وأنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ انقسامُ ما لا يصح انقسامه
Also: If He were a body composed of two or more parts, then it would necessitate that in each part subsist the attributes of knowledge, power, life, and all the attributes of divinity; because of the impossibility of the existence of an eternal other than God, and to avoid the need for a specifier in preferring some parts to have the attributes over others. But the subsistence of the attributes in every part is impossible because it would necessitate multiplicity of gods, and the proof of the necessity of unity for Him will come. As for the claim that a single attribute subsists in the whole, its falsehood is not hidden, and that would necessitate the division of what cannot be divided.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage argues against God being a composite body (جسم مركب). The reasoning: If God were composed of two or more parts, each part would have to possess the divine attributes (knowledge, power, life, etc.). This is because there is no other eternal being that could be the subject of these attributes, and also to avoid needing a specifier to arbitrarily assign attributes to some parts over others. However, if every part has the attributes, then each part would be divine, resulting in multiple gods (تعدّد الآلهة)—which is impossible. The alternative claim—that a single attribute subsists in the whole composite, not in each part—is also false because an attribute is indivisible; if it subsists in the whole, it would be divided among the parts, which is impossible. Thus, God cannot be composite.

مركب
جزء
صفات
تعدّد الآلهة
انقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think attributes could subsist in the whole composite without being in each part, preserving unity.
Correction: Attributes are indivisible; if they subsist in the whole, they would be divided among parts, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that if God were composed of parts, each part would have to possess the divine attributes?
A) Because attributes are indivisible
B) Because there is no other eternal being, and to avoid arbitrary specification
C) Because parts are equal
D) Because attributes are accidents

Key Takeaway
God cannot be composite because composition would lead to multiple gods or the division of indivisible attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you ever imagined God as having parts or pieces, what does this argument reveal about how that image contradicts God's perfection? How will you actively correct that mental image when it arises?
?
This proof shows that attributing composition to God leads to logical absurdity. Have you ever believed or said something about God that, upon reflection, implies composition? What specific steps will you take to root out such beliefs from your heart?
?
The Arabs understood that an attribute like knowledge cannot be divided. When you think of God's attributes, do you treat them as separable or independent parts of God? How can you ensure that your worship unifies God's attributes as one indivisible reality?

Proof that God is Powerful (Qadir)
Sanusi proves God's power via categorical syllogism from acting by choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that God is powerful using a categorical syllogism grounded in His acting by choice.

Core Idea
God's power follows from His acting by choice, not by nature.

Sanusi presents a formal proof for one of God's essential attributes: power. He uses a categorical syllogism that links divine action by choice to necessary power. This lesson unpacks the syllogism, its premises, and the reasoning behind the minor premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the original structure of the proof settle in your mind before analyzing it.
Notice the author's mention of two types of syllogism.
2
Identify the three parts of the syllogism
Find the major premise (God is an agent by choice), the minor premise (every agent by choice is powerful), and the conclusion (God is powerful). Write them down.
The syllogism is categorical, though the wording suggests conditional.
3
Understand why acting by choice implies power
Consider that if God acted by nature or necessitating cause, He would not need power. The proof of the minor premise involves negating these alternatives.
The author refers to earlier proof of the world's temporality.
4
Reflect on the proof's foundation
Ask yourself: why is the negation of nature and necessitating cause sufficient to prove that every agent by choice is powerful? This connects to the definition of power as the ability to act or not act.
This point will be reinforced later in the discussion of God's will.
