The Naturalists' Objection: Delayed Effects and Sanusi's Reply
Sanusi presents the naturalists' objection and begins his answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the naturalists' argument that an eternal nature can have delayed effects due to obstacles and conditions, and how Sanusi begins to refute it by examining the obstacle or condition.

Core Idea
Obstacles and conditions don't save the nature hypothesis — they push the problem back one step.

The naturalists propose that the Maker of the universe is an eternal nature, like fire, but that its effect (the world) can be delayed if obstacles or missing conditions prevent it. They use this to avoid the conclusion that the world must be eternal. Sanusi's response is to ask about the obstacle or condition itself: is it eternal or originated? This moves the argument to a new level.

Guided Path
1
Understand the naturalists' objection
Read the passage to grasp the naturalists' claim: the Maker is an eternal nature, but the world's creation was delayed due to obstacles or missing conditions.
Notice that they grant the Maker is a nature, but deny the effect must be simultaneous.
2
Examine their example of fire
The naturalists use the example of fire: by its nature it burns, but only if it contacts something burnable and there is no moisture. This illustrates how an effect can be delayed.
The example is meant to show that a cause can exist without its effect temporarily.
3
See Sanusi's initial response
Sanusi shifts the discussion to the obstacle or condition itself. He argues that this obstacle/condition must be either eternal or originated, leading to a new dilemma.
This is the beginning of a refutation that will be continued.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإنْ أُجيب عن التأخُرِ في الطبيعة . . . ) إلى آخره : هذا اعتراضُ مِنَ الطبائعيِّينَ على الدليل السابق ؛ وهو لزوم قدم العالم أو استمرار عدمه، وتقريرُهُ : أن قالوا : نختارُ أنَّ صانع الحوادثِ طبيعة ، وأنَّها قديمةٌ ، قولكم : ( فيلزمُ قدم تلك الحوادثِ ) غيرُ مسلَّم ؛ لأنَّ عدم المفارقةِ إنَّما يلزمُ في العلة مع معلولها لأنَّ تلازمهما لا يتوقَّفُ على شيء ، أما ملازمة الطبيعة مطبوعها فمتوقف على عدمِ الموانع ووجودِ الشرائط ، كما نقولُ مثلاً : تأثير النارِ بطبعها على مذهب الفلاسفة - أبعدهم الله - في احتراق الشيء .. يتوقف على وجود شرط ؛ وهو مسُّها مثلاً لذلك المحترق ، وانتفاء مانع ؛ وهو بلل ذلك الممسوس مثلاً ، أما إذا وُجِدَ مانعُها أو انتفى شرطُها فتُوجَدُ هي مع عدم مطبوعها الذي هو الاحتراق . قالوا : فإذا تقرَّرَ هذا فنقولُ : صانعُ هذه الحوادثِ طبيعةٌ قديمة ، لكن تأخَّرَ مطبوعها ولم يكن قديماً لمانع من وجودِهِ أزلاً أو فوات شرط ، فلما انتفى المانع ووُجِدَ الشرط فيما لا يزالُ . . وُجِدَتْ تلك الحوادث ، فلا يلزم على هذا قدمُ الحوادثِ ولا استمرار عدمها كما زعمتم . وجوابُهُ : أنَّا ننقلُ الكلام معهم إلى ذلك المانع مِنْ وجودِ الحوادثِ أوِ الشرط لها المتأخر وجودُهُ ، فنقولُ : ذلك المانع مِنْ تأثير الطبيعة في وجود الحوادث أزلاً لا يخلو : إما أن تُقدروه قديماً أو حادثاً :
His statement: 'If it is answered concerning the delay in the nature...' to the end: this is an objection from the naturalists against the previous proof—which is the necessity of the eternity of the world or its perpetual non-existence. Its presentation: they said: We choose that the Maker of events is a nature, and that it is eternal. Your statement: 'So the eternality of those events follows' is not conceded, because the lack of separation only follows in the case of a cause with its effect, since their inseparability does not depend on anything. As for the inseparability of a nature from its impressed effect (matbu'), it depends on the absence of obstacles and the presence of conditions. For example, the effect of fire by its nature, according to the philosophers—may Allah distance them—in burning something depends on the existence of a condition—such as contact with the burning thing—and the absence of an obstacle—such as moisture on that touched thing. If its obstacle exists or its condition is absent, then the nature exists without its impressed effect, which is burning. They said: So when this is established, we say: The Maker of these events is an eternal nature, but its impressed effect was delayed and was not eternal due to an obstacle to its existence eternally or a missing condition. So when the obstacle was removed and the condition came to be in what is yet to come, those events came to be. Thus, neither the eternality of the events nor the perpetual non-existence of them follows as you claimed. And his answer: We transfer the discussion with them to that obstacle to the existence of events or the condition whose existence was delayed, and we say: That obstacle to the nature's influence in bringing about events eternally is not free: either you assume it eternal or originated:
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the naturalists (al-taba'i'iyyun) against Sanusi's previous proof that the Maker cannot be a nature. They grant that the Maker is an eternal nature, but deny that the world must therefore be eternal. They argue that a nature produces its effect only when obstacles are absent and conditions are present. They give the example of fire: by its nature it burns, but only if it contacts a burnable object and there is no moisture to prevent burning. Thus, an eternal nature can exist without its effect if an obstacle prevents it or a condition is absent. So they claim that the world's events were delayed because of an obstacle or missing condition, and when the obstacle was removed and the condition present, the events occurred. Therefore, neither the world's eternity nor its perpetual non-existence follows. Sanusi begins his answer by moving the discussion to the obstacle or condition itself: that obstacle or condition must be either eternal or originated. This sets up a new dilemma. The answer is incomplete at this page, but we see the line of refutation: the obstacle or condition will itself need explanation, leading back to the same issues.

موانع
شرائط
مطبوع
ملازمة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that invoking obstacles and conditions saves the nature hypothesis from the need for simultaneous effect.
Correction: Sanusi shows that it merely pushes the problem back: the obstacle or condition itself requires explanation, leading back to the same dilemma.

Checkpoint
What is the naturalists' objection regarding the delay of a nature's effect?
A) The nature itself can be delayed by its own will.
B) The effect is delayed due to obstacles or missing conditions.
C) The nature is not eternal, so the effect can be delayed.
D) The cause and effect are inseparable regardless of obstacles.

Key Takeaway
The naturalists' attempt to avoid the eternity of the world by appealing to obstacles and conditions fails because the obstacle or condition itself requires explanation.

How does this lesson's exposure of the weakness of nature as an explanation change how you view everyday events? Will you consciously remind yourself of Allah's direct involvement today?
?
Consider a recent trial or obstacle you faced. Did you see it as a mere consequence of natural circumstances, or did you reflect on the deeper cause in Allah's decree? This lesson teaches that obstacles themselves must have a cause—can you trace that obstacle back to a test from Allah? How will you respond to the next obstacle with this awareness?
?
The naturalists' error is to assume that a nature can act without a voluntary will behind it. In your own life, where have you unconsciously relied on 'second causes' while neglecting the first cause—Allah's will? What concrete step can you take today to shift your reliance from natural expectations to direct trust in Allah?

Refuting Philosophers Using the Spheres
Sanusi presents a refutation of philosophers using the specific number and magnitudes of the spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specific number and sizes of the celestial spheres serve as evidence for Allah's will and choice against the philosophers' theory of necessary causation.

Core Idea
The precise details of creation prove it is the result of choice, not necessity.

This passage introduces a key kalam argument against the philosophers who claim the universe is a necessary emanation. By pointing to the specific number and sizes of the celestial spheres, the author shows that these contingent features must be the result of a free and wise choice, not blind necessity. Let's examine how this simple observation dismantles the deterministic worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to grasp the argument's structure. Notice the rhetorical question that forms the core of the refutation.
2
Identify the philosophers' position
The philosophers affirm causation (ta'lil) and deny choice (ikhtiyar) and will (irada) to the Creator. Understand what they believe before seeing how it is refuted.
3
Analyze the rhetorical question
The author asks why the spheres have a specific number and specific magnitudes. Note that these could have been otherwise, which is the key to the argument.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The contingency of these features implies a free chooser who decided on these specific values. This undermines the idea of a necessary universe.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider how this argument points to a voluntary Creator and how it fits into broader kalam arguments for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يبطل مذهب الفلاسفة القائلين بالتعليل، النافين عن الصانع الاختيار والإرادة: أن يقال لهم: ما بال الأفلاك وقفت على عدد مخصوص ولم تكن أكثر منه ولا أقل؟ ولم كانت على تلك المقادير المخصوصة ولم تكن أكبر منها
And among what invalidates the doctrine of the philosophers who affirm causation, and who deny the Creator choice and will, is that it is said to them: Why did the spheres stop at a specific number, being neither more nor less? And why were they at those specific magnitudes and not larger?
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage introduces a new refutation of the philosophers (falasifa) who believe in necessary causation (ta'lil) and deny that the Creator has choice (ikhtiyar) and will (irada). The author presents a simple but powerful argument: the spheres (the celestial bodies) have a specific number (e.g., nine or ten in classical astronomy) and specific sizes. If the universe were a necessary emanation from a cause, there would be no reason why the number or sizes are exactly as they are—they could have been more or less, larger or smaller. But the fact that they are precisely determined shows that they are the result of a free choice by a wise agent, not a blind necessity. This argument is a staple of kalam theology against Aristotelian cosmology. The passage is incomplete but the direction is clear.

B) Their specific number and magnitudes
C) Their motion and direction
D) Their supposed eternity

Key Takeaway
The specific contingent features of the universe, like the number and sizes of the spheres, point to a Creator with free will and choice, not a necessary cause.

The Specificity of the Cosmos Points to a Free Creator
Sanusi poses rhetorical questions about celestial motions and positions to argue for a free Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingent specificities of the celestial spheres and stars refute the philosophers' doctrine of necessary causation and prove a free Creator.

Core Idea
Every specific detail of the cosmos that could be otherwise requires a free Chooser.

Sanusi now moves from conceptual arguments to concrete physical examples. He poses a series of pointed questions about the precise features of the heavens — the number of spheres, their motions, the positions of stars — that on the philosophers' own principle of necessary causation have no explanation. This demonstrates that these features must be the result of a free and wise Creator who chooses and specifies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the questions
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to each specific feature Sanusi asks about: the number of spheres, their sizes, motions, etc.
The passage is a single continuous argument; try to see the pattern.
2
Understand the logical structure
Each question highlights a contingent feature that could have been otherwise. Sanusi argues that on the philosophers' principle of necessary causation, there is no reason for these specific details.
The key is that if causation were necessary, the cosmos would have no room for such specific choices.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The impossibility of explaining these specificities by necessary causation forces us to accept a free Agent who specifies as He wills.
Sanusi ends with a harsh dismissal of the opposing view and a supplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يبطل مذهب الفلاسفة القائلين بالتعليل، النافينَ عنِ الصانع الاختيار والإرادةَ: أَنْ يُقالَ لهم: ما بال الأفلاكِ وقفَتْ على عددٍ مخصوص ولم تكن أكثرَ منهُ ولا أقل؟ ولِمَ كانَتْ على تلك المقادير المخصوصة ولم تكن أكبر منها ولا أصغر؟ وما بالُ الأعلى منها يتحرَّكُ حركةً واحدةً مِنَ المشرقِ إلى المغرب، وباقي الأفلاك تتحرَّكُ حركتين؛ إحداهما: الحركة اليومية مِنَ المشرقِ إلى المغرب، والأخرى: حركاتها في البروج مِنَ المغرب إلى المشرقِ؟ وما بالُ الحركاتِ كلّها اختصت بما بين المشرق والمغرب، ولم تكن فيما بين الجنوب والشِّمالِ مثلاً؟ ولِمَ اختص كلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ السبعةِ السَّيَّارَةِ بفَلَكِهِ المخصوص معَ جواز أنْ يكونَ في غيرِهِ؟ ولِمَ اختصَّتْ سائر الكواكب الثابتة بالفلكِ الثامن ولم تكن في غيرِهِ؟ ولِمَ كانَ الفلك التاسع أطلس مِنَ الكواكب؟(1)، ولِمَ كانَ بعض الكواكب أكبرَ مِنْ بعض؟ ولِمَ كَانَ بعضُها يلي القطبَ الشَّماليَّ، وبعضُها يلي القطب الجنوبي، وبعضُها على سمتِ الرؤوس، وبعضُها مائلاً عنه؟ ولا مُوجِبَ للتخصيص بجميع ما ذُكِرَ على أصلكم. وهل مذهبكم في إسنادِ ذلك إلى غيرِ الفاعل المختار الذي يخص ما شاءَ بما شاءَ إلا تلاعب لا يرضى بقوله إلا مسلوب العقل والإيمان، ومَنْ لم ينفعهُ اللهُ بشيءٍ ممَّا تعبَ في تعلّمِهِ، وصار يهذو هذيان المجانينِ وغيرِ المميَّرِ مِنَ الصبيانِ، ولا حول ولا قُوَّةَ إلا باللهِ؟! اللهم؛ عافنا بفضلِكَ مِنْ كلّ آفةٍ في ديننا ودنيانا وآخرتنا يا أرحم الراحمينَ، يا ذا الجلال والإكرام. (1) أطلس : أسود ، وقوله : ( من الكواكب ) أي : من أجل خلوه منها ، ففي الكلام حذف ، أو ضمن ( أطلس ) معنى : متجرّد أو عارٍ ، فعدى بـ ( من ) . حامدي » ( ص ۱۸۳ )
And among that which invalidates the doctrine of the philosophers who adhere to causation, and who deny the Creator choice and will, is that it is said to them: What is it that made the spheres stop at a specific number, neither more nor less? And why were they at those specific magnitudes, neither larger nor smaller? And what is it that makes the highest of them move with a single motion from east to west, while the rest of the spheres move with two motions: one of which is the daily motion from east to west, and the other is their motion through the zodiac from west to east? And what makes all the motions restricted to between east and west, and not between south and north, for example? And why was each of the seven planets restricted to its own specific sphere, when it is possible for it to be in another? And why were the rest of the fixed stars restricted to the eighth sphere and not in another? And why was the ninth sphere devoid of stars? And why are some stars larger than others? And why are some of them near the northern pole, some near the southern pole, some at the zenith, and some inclined away from it? There is no cause for specification in all that has been mentioned according to your principle. And is your doctrine of attributing that to other than the free Agent who specifies whatever He wills with whatever He wills anything but a frivolity whose statement is accepted only by one who is deprived of intellect and faith, and one whom God has not benefited with anything of what he has labored to learn, and who became delirious like the delirium of madmen and the indiscriminate of children? And there is no power nor strength except through God! O God, grant us by Your grace from every affliction in our religion, our world, and our afterlife, O Most Merciful of the merciful, O Possessor of majesty and honor. (1) Atlas: black, and His saying: (of the stars) i.e., because of its emptiness from them, so there is an ellipsis in the speech, or (atlas) has the meaning of stripped or bare, so it is transitive with 'min'. Hamidi (p. 183)
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a series of rhetorical questions posed to the philosophers who believe in necessary causation (al-ta'lil) and deny divine choice and will. The author begins by stating that these questions invalidate their doctrine. He then enumerates specific contingent features of the cosmos: the number and sizes of the celestial spheres, the motions of the highest sphere versus the others (single vs. double motions), the restriction of motions to the east-west direction, the assignment of each planet to its own sphere, the fixed stars in the eighth sphere, the emptiness of the ninth sphere, the varying sizes of stars, and their positions relative to the poles and zenith. He argues that on the philosophers' principle of necessary causation, there is no cause that compels these specific details; they could have been otherwise. Therefore, these specificities point to a free Agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) who chooses and specifies. He then concludes harshly that attributing these to anything other than the free Agent is mere frivolity, accepted only by someone devoid of intellect and faith. The passage ends with a supplication to God for protection.

Misconception: The specific details of the cosmos (number of spheres, motions, star positions) can be explained by natural laws or chance alone, without a Creator.
Correction: Sanusi argues that these specificities are contingent and have no necessary cause; they could have been otherwise, so they require a free Agent who chooses.

Key Takeaway

Proof of God's Knowledge from Perfect Design
Proof that God must be knowing from order in creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perfect design of the human body is used in kalam to prove that God is knowing (Alim).

Core Idea
Perfect order in creation proves a knowing Creator.

In this passage, the commentator presents a classic teleological argument for God's knowledge. He focuses on the human body as a clear example of intricate craftsmanship, where each part is perfectly suited to its function. The argument is structured as a conditional proof: if the Maker lacked knowledge, such perfection could not exist. This lesson will guide you through the proof up to the point where the necessary connection is established.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal English translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
The passage ends abruptly due to a page break.
2
Identify the main claim and examples
The commentator asserts that the Maker must be knowing. He gives examples from the human body: each part specializes in a particular benefit and is provided with what preserves it.
3
Understand the conditional structure
The proof is formalized as a conditional: 'if your Maker were not knowing, you would not be characterized by these perfections.' This is a logical implication.
Notice the use of 'law' (if) to set up the conditional.
4
Grasp the intuitive necessary connection
The necessary connection (mulazama) is explained as known by intuition (badiha): an action is not perfected and brought forth except through knowledge. This is a self-evident principle.
This step is where the passage cuts off.
5
Reflect on the incompleteness
Note that the argument is incomplete due to the page break. However, the structure up to this point shows a clear proof: perfection requires a knowing agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يجب أيضاً لصانعِكَ أنْ يكون عالماً ، وإلا لم تكن على ما أنتَ عليهِ مِنْ دقائقِ الصنع ؛ في اختصاص كلّ جزء منكَ بمنفعتِهِ الخاصَّةِ بهِ ، وإمداده بما يحفظها عليه ، ونحو ذلكَ مِنَ المحاسنِ التي تعجز عقول البشر عن الإحاطة بأسرارها . نظم الدليل على لفظِهِ أنْ يُقالَ : لو لم يكن صانعُكَ عالماً لم تكن متصفاً بما أنتَ عليهِ مِنْ غاية الإحكام ودقائقِ المحاسنِ التي يُعجَزُ عن حصرها . وبيان الملازمة : أنَّه معلوم بالبديهة أنَّهُ لا يُحكم الفعل ويُبرِزُهُ في
Then it is also necessary that your Maker be knowing. Otherwise, you would not be as you are of the intricacies of craftsmanship: in the specialization of each part of you for its particular benefit, and its provision with what preserves it, and similar perfections that the minds of humans are incapable of encompassing in their secrets. The proof is formalized in its wording as follows: if your Maker were not knowing, you would not be characterized by what you are upon of utmost perfection and intricate perfections that are impossible to enumerate. The explanation of the necessary connection is that it is known by intuition that an action is not perfected and brought forth in...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God must possess the attribute of knowledge (al-ilm). The author begins by stating that it is necessary (wajib) for the Maker (as-sani') to be knowing, because otherwise the intricate design of the human body would not exist. He gives examples: each organ is specialized for a specific function and is supplied with what preserves it—these are perfections that human minds cannot fully grasp. Then he formalizes the proof as a conditional syllogism (lu… law): if the Maker were not knowing, you would not possess these perfections. The necessary connection (mulazama) between not-knowing and the absence of perfection is explained as an intuitive truth (badiha): no action can be perfected and brought forth (presumably 'in a perfect manner') except by a knowledgeable agent. The argument is cut off at the end of the page, but the structure is clear.

Key Terms
الصانع
العلم
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The order in creation only proves that God exists, not that He is knowing.
Correction: This proof specifically targets the attribute of knowledge: the intricate design of the human body requires a knowing designer, not just a powerful one.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the proof that the Maker must be knowing?
A) The Maker's existence is proven by the human body.
B) Without knowledge, the Maker could not have created such intricate design.
C) The human body is perfect, so its Maker must be powerful.
D) The Maker's knowledge is proven by revelation alone.

Key Takeaway
The intricate design of the human body proves that its Maker must be knowing, because perfection in action requires knowledge in the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the intricate design of your own body proves that your Maker is knowing, how does that change the way you speak about Him when you are alone? Will you begin to address Him as the All-Knowing with more awe and reverence starting today?
?
Do you live as though the One who crafted you with such precision knows your every thought and deed? What specific thought or action today would be different if you truly believed that?
?
How does the realization that your body's perfect structure screams the knowledge of its Creator affect your response to doubts about His wisdom in the trials you are facing right now? Will you surrender to His knowledge even when you do not understand?

The Proof that God is All-Knowing
Sanusi proves the Maker's knowledge via a conditional syllogism from design.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that the Maker must be all-knowing using a conditional syllogism based on the perfection of creation.

Core Idea
Perfect design requires perfect knowledge; only a knower can produce such precision.

Sanusi now turns to prove that the Maker is not only powerful but also all-knowing. He uses an argument that is both logical and intuitive: the intricate design of the human body and the universe cannot be the product of ignorance or chance. This passage presents a conditional syllogism that forms the core of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the conditional structure and the terms 'ملازمة' and 'استثنائية'.
These are technical terms in logic.
2
Identify the conditional syllogism
Find the if-then structure: 'If not knowing, then not perfect.' Then identify the implication and the conditional premise.
3
Understand the implication (ملازمة)
The author explains why the implication holds: it is intuitively known that only a wise, knowledgeable being can produce such perfect work.
This is a self-evident truth.
4
Confirm the conditional premise (استثنائية)
The conditional premise is that the consequent (the perfect organization) is true. This is confirmed by direct observation of the wonders in creation.
No one can deny the perfection.
5
Reflect on the rejection of chance
The author dismisses the possibility of coincidence as irrational. Consider why this is a necessary conclusion.
This guards against a common objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1)-
[ الله تعالى عالم ]
ثم يجب أيضاً لصانعِكَ أنْ يكون عالماً ، وإلا لم تكن على ما أنتَ عليهِ مِنْ دقائقِ الصنع ؛ في اختصاص كلّ جزء منكَ بمنفعتِهِ الخاصَّةِ بهِ ، وإمداده بما يحفظها عليه ، ونحوِ ذلكَ مِنَ المحاسنِ التي تعجز عقول البشر عن الإحاطة بأسرارها .
نظم الدليل على لفظِهِ أنْ يُقالَ : لو لم يكن صانعُكَ عالماً لم تكن متصفاً بما أنتَ عليهِ مِنْ غاية الإحكام ودقائقِ المحاسنِ التي يُعجَزُ عن حصرها .
وبيان الملازمة : أنَّه معلوم بالبديهة أنَّهُ لا يُحكم الفعل ويُبرِزُهُ في غاية الكمال وما لا يُحاط بهِ مِنْ أنواع المحاسنِ .. إلا مَنْ هو عالم حكيم غاية الحكمة .
وأما الاستثنائية : فمعلومةٌ بضرورة المشاهدة ، ولا يخفى أنَّ عجائب مصنوعاتِهِ سبحانَهُ ممَّا لا يحيط بها وصفُ واصفٍ ، ومَنْ جَوَّزَ صدور تلكَ العجائب مع كثرتها وخروجها عن حد الحصرِ مِنَ الجاهل على سبيل الاتفاق .. كان معانداً للحقِّ ، جاحداً للضرورة، وسقطَتْ مكالمته لخروجه عن حيّز العقلاء
(1) [God Most High is All-Knowing.] Then it is also necessary for your Maker to be knowing, otherwise you would not be as you are with the subtleties of craftsmanship: in the allocation of each part of you to its specific benefit, and its supply with what preserves it, and similar perfections that human minds are incapable of encompassing their secrets. The arrangement of the proof in its wording is to say: If your Maker were not knowing, you would not be characterized as you are with utmost precision and subtleties of perfections that are impossible to enumerate. And the explanation of the implication: It is known by intuition that an action is not perfected and brought forth in utmost perfection and with types of perfections that cannot be comprehended, except by one who is all-knowing, all-wise with utmost wisdom. As for the conditional premise, it is known by necessity of observation, and it is not hidden that the wonders of His creations—glory be to Him—are not encompassed by the description of any describer. And whoever permits the occurrence of those wonders, despite their abundance and exceeding enumeration, from an ignorant one by way of coincidence, is an obstinate opponent of truth, a denier of necessity, and his discourse is invalidated for his departure from the realm of rational people.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of the Maker being all-knowing (عالم). The author begins by stating the requirement: your Maker must be knowing, otherwise you would not exist in your current state of intricate design. He then presents a conditional syllogism: if the Maker were not knowing, you would not have such perfect organization. The implication (ملازمة) is explained intuitively: perfect action can only come from a wise, knowledgeable being. The conditional premise (استثنائية) is confirmed by direct observation of the wonders in creation. Anyone who claims such wonders could arise from an ignorant being by coincidence is rejecting clear necessity and is beyond rational discourse.

عالم
بديهة
ملازمة
استثنائية
اتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The wonders of creation could arise from an ignorant being by coincidence.
Correction: Sanusi states that this is impossible because perfect design necessarily implies a knowledgeable designer; anyone who claims otherwise is irrational.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about someone who claims that the wonders of creation could occur from an ignorant being by coincidence?
A) He is excused due to ignorance.
B) He is an obstinate opponent of truth and a denier of necessity.
C) His argument is still valid but weak.
D) He is considered a rational person.

Key Takeaway
The perfect design of creation necessitates that the Maker is all-knowing, and denying this is irrational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the intricate design of your own body—your eyes that adjust to light, your heart that beats without your command—do you truly live as though you belong to an All-Knowing Maker, or do you act as if you are a self-sufficient accident? What would change in your daily habits if you genuinely believed that every perfect detail of you was intentionally placed there by a knower who sees you?
?
The proof says that denying design by appealing to coincidence is to leave the realm of rational people. Is there any area of your life where you intellectually accept this proof, yet function as if coincidence rules? What concrete step can you take today to align your actions with the reality that your existence is known intimately by your Creator?
?
Sanusi points out that human minds cannot fully grasp the secrets of creation. Does this humble you? Do you walk through your day with awe at your own complexity, or do you take your existence for granted? What can you do today to cultivate genuine amazement at the craftsmanship you carry, and gratitude toward the One who made you?

Refuting the Objection of Coincidence
Sanusi refutes the claim that repeated perfect actions could be coincidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why repeated perfect actions cannot be dismissed as coincidence and how Sanusi uses analogical reasoning to refute the objection.

Core Idea
Repeated perfect actions prove knowledge; the objection is self-defeating.

In this passage, Sanusi responds to a skeptical objection against the proof from design. The objector claims that a single perfect action could occur by coincidence, so multiple instances also do not prove a knowing agent. Sanusi shows this reasoning leads to absurd consequences, like denying that many witnesses can give certainty or that many premises can yield a conclusion. This lesson examines his elegant refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objector's claim and Sanusi's immediate dismissal by analogy.
The objection is that a single perfect action from an ignorant person could be coincidental, so repeated occurrences also don't indicate knowledge.
2
Identify the three analogies
Sanusi compares the objection to three absurd statements: that many reports don't give certainty, that a lot of water doesn't quench, and that two premises don't yield a conclusion. Find these in the text.
These analogies expose the fallacy of the objector's reasoning.
3
Understand the final appeal
Sanusi concludes that equating single and multiple instances in this way contradicts sensory experience (ḥiss), custom (ʿāda), and reason (ʿaql). Reflect on why these three sources of knowledge are fundamental.
This reinforces that cumulative evidence is rationally compelling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولُ مَنْ قالَ : قد يقعُ الفعلُ المحكَمُ مِنَ الجَاهِلِ مَرَّةً على سبيل الاتِّفاقِ ولا يدلُّ ، فكذا يجب ألا يدلَّ إذا وقع مرَّاتٍ . . هو نظير قول القائل : إذا لم يُفِدْ خبرُ الواحدِ العلمَ فلا يفيد خبرُ الجماعة ، وإذا لم يُرْوِ قليلُ الماءِ فلا يُرْوي كثيره ، وإذا لم تنتج المقدمة الواحدة فلا تنتج المقدمتانِ ! والتسويةُ في ذلك خلافُ الحس والعادة والعقل(١)
And the statement of one who says: 'Sometimes a perfected action occurs from an ignorant person once by coincidence and does not indicate [knowledge], so likewise it should not indicate if it occurs multiple times'—this is similar to the statement of one who says: 'If a single report does not yield certain knowledge, then the report of a group does not yield it; and if a small amount of water does not quench thirst, then a large amount does not quench it; and if one premise does not produce a conclusion, then two premises do not produce it.' And equating in that is contrary to sensory experience, habit, and reason.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the proof from design. The objector argues that a perfect action could happen once by coincidence from an ignorant person, so multiple occurrences also do not prove knowledge. The author dismisses this as analogous to absurd claims: that if one witness doesn't give certainty, many witnesses don't; if a little water doesn't quench, a lot doesn't; if one premise doesn't yield a conclusion, two don't. This shows the objector's reasoning contradicts sense perception (حس), customary experience (عادة), and rational inference (عقل).

Key Terms
اتفاق
خبر واحد
خبر الجماعة
حس
عادة
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if a perfect action can happen once by coincidence, then repeated occurrences also do not indicate a knowing agent.
Correction: Sanusi shows this reasoning is false because cumulative evidence does yield knowledge, as seen in other domains like witness testimony, quenching thirst, and logical deduction.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi compare the objector's argument to?
A) Saying that if one report doesn't yield knowledge, many reports don't.
B) Saying that if an ignorant person can do something once, he can do it many times.
C) Saying that a small amount of water can quench thirst.
D) Saying that two premises always produce a conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The passage demonstrates that the objector's reasoning is self-refuting because it denies the cumulative effect that is accepted in sensory experience, custom, and rationality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you dismissed repeated signs of God's mercy or guidance in your life as mere coincidence. If you were to treat those events as cumulative evidence, how would your relationship with God change today?
?
Sanusi warns that rejecting cumulative evidence is contrary to reason. Is there a belief or habit in your life that you hold onto despite repeated evidence against it? What would it cost you to honestly evaluate that evidence?
?
The objector's reasoning is self-defeating—it denies what we know from experience. In what areas of your faith might you be using similar reasoning to avoid the demands of submission? What step will you take this week to align your thinking with the evidence God has placed before you?

The Beehive Objection to the Design Argument
Sanusi presents the objection that bees build perfect hexagonal cells without knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the beehive objection to the design argument and why it was considered a challenge to the proof from teleology.

Core Idea
Bees build perfect hexagons without tools or knowledge — but does that refute design?

This lesson presents a classical objection to the design argument (dalīl al-‘ināya). The objector points to bees, which construct intricate hexagonal honeycombs without any apparent knowledge. Only skilled engineers can design such shapes. If nature can produce such order without intelligence, the argument for a Designer seems weakened. Sanusi records this objection — but unfortunately, his answer is not preserved in this page.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice that the passage is cut off — the answer is missing.
The text ends with 'except by...' indicating continuation.
2
Identify the two benefits
The objector says bees chose the hexagonal shape for two benefits: proximity to circular (like the bee's body) and no wasted gaps. Notice how this seems to indicate purpose.
The objector claims bees act without tools (āla) or knowledge.
3
Understand the challenge
The design argument says perfect design proves a Wise Creator. This objection says bees produce design without intelligence, so the proof fails.
The answer likely invokes divine inspiration as a form of guided action.
4
Reflect on the missing answer
Since the answer is not here, consider what a theologian might say. Islamic theology holds that Allah guides bees by inspiration (ilhām).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيلَ : ينتقصُ هذا الدليلُ بما يتَّخِذُهُ النحلُ بغيرِ آلَةٍ مِنَ البيوتِ المحكمة المسدَّسة التي لا يعرِفُ وضع مثلها إلا المهندسون واختارَتْ خصوصية هذا الشكل لجمعِهِ بينَ مصلحتين ؛ وهما قربُهُ مِنْ شكل الدائرة القريبِ مِنْ شكل النحلةِ ، والأمنُ مَعَهُ مِنْ فَرَج تبقى بين الأشكالِ ضائعة لغير فائدة، ومعرفة كون الجمع بين هاتين المصلحتين خاصاً بهذا الشكل المسدَّس (٢) . . ممَّا لا يستخرجه إلا
For if it is said: This proof is undermined by what bees take without tools: well-constructed hexagonal houses that only engineers know how to design, and they chose the specificity of this shape for combining two benefits: its proximity to the circular shape close to the bee's shape, and the security from gaps that remain between shapes wasted without benefit. And knowledge that the combination of these two benefits is specific to this hexagonal shape ... which can only be extracted by
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the beehive. The objector argues that bees build perfect hexagonal cells without any tool or knowledge, which only skilled engineers can design. They choose this shape for two benefits: close to circular (like the bee's shape) and no wasted gaps. The text cuts off, but the objection is that if bees can do this without knowledge, then the proof from design fails. The complete answer is not preserved in this window.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That bees' perfect engineering proves that intelligent design is unnecessary for complex creation.
Correction: The objection is incomplete; the standard answer is that bees act by Allah's inspiration (ilhām), not by their own intelligence.

Key Takeaway
The beehive objection contends that bees produce complex hexagonal cells without knowledge, challenging the design argument, but the answer (likely divine inspiration) is missing from this passage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the intricate hexagonal honeycomb, do you instinctively praise the Creator or do you rationalize it as mere instinct? What does your immediate reaction reveal about the state of your faith?
?
The objector argues that bees build perfectly without knowledge. If your own actions were examined, do they reflect the order and wisdom of divine guidance or the chaos of self-reliance? What needs to change so that every part of your day becomes an act of worship?
?
You have just read a cut-off passage. The missing answer is likely that bees are guided by God. Yet, how often do you ignore clear signs of divine design in your life because you lack the patience to complete the 'answer'? Will you now commit to seeking the full picture before forming conclusions about God's existence?
